THE RESULT OF EXPERIENCE.
There is no doubt that the popularity enjoyed by
CAMEO CIGARETTES
is the result of experience on the part of the smoker. He finds
CAMEOS always the same —of even quality and excellence, and
far and away the choicest Cigarette made.
What do you Smoke?
THE RED PAGE.
Sarah Grand is the unfortunate lady who
was badly treated by a Man and revenges
herself upon Men. Men are red-nosed
vipers who live on dunghills and say
“ D unn ! ” when their wives forget to order
the Avhisky. It is Sarah’s business to strip
the vipers of their masks and paint them in
the true colors of the particular reptile who
bit her. And if this is mixed, it is nothing
like as mixed as Sarah. How Sarah must
have suffered !
* * • •
‘The Beth Book,’ like ‘The Heavenly
Twins,’ is an astonishing farrago of sense
and nonsense, interest and boredom and
suggestion. Sarah’s facts and impressions
leap from her pen helter-skelter, higgledy-
piggledy, ‘ all at once and all o’er, with a
mighty uproar ’ —just like the water comes
down at Lodore. Good episodes, bad
episodes; matter relevant and irrelevant ;
passages well-proportioned for the effect of
the whole and passages out of all proportion
to anything but a literary nightmare ;
Sarah vomits them anyhow and nohow, like
the very active female volcano that she is.
Her mind swims in a sea of memories, and
when she has written the chapter-heading it
dives down and brings up whatever it
clutches —seaweed, mud, pebbles, dead fish,
or now and again a pearl. Everything goes
into ‘The Beth Book.’ It is agreed that—
once fathered—the New Woman can very
well do without the old man for all general
purposes. But she will always want him to
cut the lumber out of her books.
* * * *
It is marriage that spoils Sarah’s book,
just as (O pity !) it spoiled Sarah. Up to
page 324 or so she is busy representing her
childhood and girlhood in a really dramatic
and interesting manner ; one doesn’t recall
another study-of-a-girl so close and capable.
Here, for example, is a charming passage—
true, well-observed, well-written. (Beth’s
father has told her he gets no separate
pleasure from any particular flower : they all
delight him.)
Beth pondered upon this for a little, then she
asked, ‘Do you know which I like best? Hot
primroses.’ Captain Caldwell raised his eyebrows
interrogatively. ‘ When you pick them in the
sun, and put them against your cheek, they’re all'
warm, you know,’ Beth explained ; ‘and then they
are good! And fuchsias are good too, but it
isn’t the same good. You know that one in the
sitting-room Window, white outside and salmon-
colored inside, and such a nice shape—the flowers
—and the way they hang down ; you have to lift
them to look into them. When I look it them
long, they make me feel—oh—feel, you know—feel
that I could take the whole plant m my arms and
hug it. But fuchsias don’t scent sweet like hot
primroses. ’
‘ And therefore they are not so good ? ’ her
father suggested, greatly interested in the child’s
attempt to express herself. ‘ They say that the
scent is the soul of the flower.’
‘The scent is the soul of the flower,’Beth re-
peated several times ; then heaved a deep sigh of
satisfaction. ‘I want to sing it,’ she said. ‘I
always want to sing things like that.’
* » * *
‘What other “things like that” do you
know, Beth 1 ’ asks her father.
1 The song of the sea in the shell.
The swish of the grassftn the hreexe,
The sound of a far-away bell,
The whispering leaves on the trees,’
Beth burst out instantly.
‘ Who taught you that, Beth ? ’ her father asked.
Oh, no one taught me, papa,’ she answered.
' just came to me—like this, you know. I used
I>o listen to the sea in that shell in the sitting-
room, and I tried and tried to find a name for the
sound, and all at once song came into my head—
The song of the sea in the shell. Then I was lying
cut here on the grass when it was long, before you
cut it to make hay, and you came out and said,
“There’s a stiff breeze blowing.” And it blew
hard and then stopped, and then it came again ;
and every time it came the grass went—swish-h-h !
The swish of the grass in the breeze. Then you
know that bell that rings a long way off, you can
only just hear it out here— The sound of a far-
away bell. Then the leaves—it was a long time
before anything came that I could sing about
them. I used to try and think it, but you can’t
■ing a thing you think. It’s when a thing comes,
you can sing it. I was always listening to the
leaves, and I always felt they were doing some-
thing ; then all at once it came one day. Of course
they were whispering— The whispering leaves on
the trees. That was how they came, papa.’
* « • •
Does this not picture admirably the
dreamy fancies of a clever child ? And Beth
goes on—
-1 At first I used to sing them by themselves;
but now I sing them all together. You can sing
them three different ways—the way I did first,
you know, then you can put breeze first—
‘ The swish of the grass in the breeze,
The whispering leaves on the trees,
The song of the sea in the shell,
The sound of a far-away bell.’
Or you oan sing—
‘ The sound of a far-away bell,
The whispering leaves on the trees,
The swish of the grass in the breeze,
The song of the sea in the shell.’
Which way do you think the nicest?’ She had
rattled all this off as fast as she could speak, look-
ing and pointing towards the various things she
mentioned as she proceeded—the sea, the grass,
the trees, the distance : now she looked up to her
father for an answer. He was looking at her so
queerly, she was filled with alarm. ‘Am I
naughty, papa ? ’ she exclaimed.
‘ Oh, no,’ he said, with a smile that reassured
her. ‘ I was just thinking. I like to hear how
“ things come ’’ to you. You must always tell
me—when new things come. By the way, who
told you that fuchsia was salmon-colored ? ’
‘I saw it was,’ she said, surprised that he needed
to ask such a question. ‘ I saw 7 it one day when
we had boiled salmon for dinner. Isn’t it nice
when you see that one thing’s like another? I
have a pebble, and it’s just the shape of a pear—
now you know what shape it is, don’t you ? ’ He
nodded. ‘But if I said it’s thick at one end and
thin at another, you wouldn’t know what shape it
is a bit, would you ? ’
‘No, I should nob,’ he answered, beginning to
prune again, thoughtfully. ‘ Beth,’ he said pre-
sently, 1 1 should like to see you grow up.’
* * * ♦
‘ The Beth Book’s ’ sub-title is ‘ a Study
from the Life of Elizabeth Caldwell Maclure,
a Woman of Genius’ —and this looks like a
child of genius. But Beth’s sex is against
her. The book is her life-story, and it is
singular how the brightness goes from it at
her epoch of puberty—just as it goes from
the average girl-child. Probably Sarah’s
Man made things worse; but there is no
need to saddle him with all the blame.
After page 324 or so there is little but rant
and hysteria—devoid alike of truth and use-
fulness, and not even amusing. Beth the
natural girl is admirable; but Beth the
didactic and unenlightened woman is abomin-
able. Several good descriptive passages and
much crude force of statement do not alter
this judgment. Says Sarah (p. 152)- —
It was the next day that Aunt Victoria left
them to make room for Mildred and the boys.
Beth went with her mother to see the old lady off
to the station. On account of their connections
the little party attracted attention, and Mrs.
Caldwell, feeling her importance, expected the
officials to be obsequious, which they were; and,
in return, she also expect! d Aunt Victoria to
make proper acknowledgment of their attentions.
She considered that sixpence at least was neces-
sary to uphold the dignity of the family on such
occasions ; but, to her horror, when the moment
came, Aunt Victoria, after an exciting fumble,'
drew from her reticule a tract entitled ‘The Man
on the Slant,’ and, in the face of everybody,
handed it to the expectant porter.
Sarah doesn’t know it ; but she acts pre-
cisely as Aunt Victoria. We come to her
deserving and expecting Literature, 7 and she
fumbles in her marital Chamber of Horrors
and gives us 4 The Man on the Slant.’
* » * *
One of the pieces of verse printed on this
page, 4 A Lullaby, ’ was a gem in Palgrave's
first 4 Golden Treasury ’ ; but the freakish,
Blakish other—quite a modern 4 find ’ —does
not seem to have been included by antho-
logy-makers before Mrs. Meynell. That
lady’s new collection, 4 The Flower of the
Mind,’ naturally reflects her personal prefer-
ence for the quaintly-happy and curiously-
carven. Her own verse is simple enough ;
but in the verse of others she seems to
desire the rather stiff 1 conceits which make
subtle her prose Thus she clings to
Crashaw (justly), and chooses from Herbert
and Herrick rather the allusive than the
obvious in phrase and sentiment. A. T.
Quiller-Couch, in ‘The Golden Pomp,’
seems closer to the lyric spirit—he
is more alive, more romantic, and more
responsive to a breath of emotion. Mrs.
Meynell’s taste in beautiful verses seems
rather virtuoso’s than lover’s : she is in tune
with religious feeling, but where religion is
not concerned she is dispassionate, does not
palpitate enough, is too intellectual, too far
from her youth. Qoilier Couch’s head is not
so clear, but his heart pulses more quickly,
and with hotter blood. Ohurton Collins, in
his ‘Treasury of Minor British Poetry,’
seems wider and mellower than either. He
is warmed by passion and charmed by
scholarship : his point of view seems more
like Palgrave’s, but he includes humour as
well as sentiment, and the recherche as well
as the oi’dinary. And Collins has a male
appreciation of the value of ideas.
* * * *
Mrs. Meynell, by the way, seems to have
transfeiTed to Francis Thompson the en-
thusiasm which in his life she nourished
for Coventry Patmore. Patmore, Thompson,
and Meynell are, or were, all of the Roman
Catholic religion ; and maybe this spiritual
bond strengthened the intellectual one.
Anyway, Mrs. Meynell, who ‘ selected ’ Pat-
more, considered him ‘ the divinest voice of
our time;’ and Patmore held Mrs. Meynell
unique as English essayist. Now Patmore
is dead, Mrs. Meynell’s adjectives go to
show that Thompson is ‘the divinest,’
etc.; while Thompson’s eulogies of Meynell
are as undiscriminating as Patmore’s. In
truth, clever and cultured though she be,
Mrs. Meynell’s reputation will not last
beyond her day and her coterie. Her verse
is interesting and lacking force : her affected
and ornamented prose, often brilliant and
suggestive, is too aloof and ‘ precious .to
live beyond the hothouse of contemporai'y
friendship. (Mrs. Meynell has in Louise
Imogen Guiney an American imitator who
pushes her sophisticated style to the verge
of frenzy.)
• * • •
Palgrave’s second ‘Golden Treasury’ (of
this-century verse) is about as incapably col-
lected as it could be. (Palgrave, of course,
was on the brink of the grave ; and his brain
had softened or solidified). The host of
minor poets is all but unrepresented ; —the
book's staple is Browning, Arnold, and
Tennyson—and some examples of the first
and second and many of the last are unneces-
sary or odious—fancy including ‘ The
Northern Farmer’ and ‘Locksley Hall’
among ‘ Songs and Lyrics' ; —and certain of
the editor’s favorites are monstrously unde-
serving of favor. O’Shaughnessy, indeed,
nearly always justifies preference, though
not the disproportionate preference he
enjoys ; but W. Barnes and Tennyson-Tur-
ner might very well be dispensed with alto-
gether. And anyone who wants to see what
Palgrave has omitted may look up two or
three volumes in Miles’s ‘ Poets of the
Century ’ series (particularly the volume of
‘ Contemporary Poets ’). The obvious fact
is that Palgrave quite incompetent to
undertake the task set him (or which he set
himself). He had no knowledge of the
modern-verse field ; and his old-age at once
disinclined him to gather knowledge and in-
capacitated him from using it. So he made
his garland, for the most part, from his
favorites in youth and middle-age ; narrowed
his scope to his limited reading ; and often
mistook his-own familiarity and pai’tiality for
writers’ merit. The second ‘ Golden Trea-
sury ’ is practically worthless.
* * *
Mark Twain was Mark Twain : now he is
M.T. And old, sick, tired, he still plods
round the weary literary whim-path—has to,
it seems ; for a creditor’s lash drives him.
“ More Tramps Abroad ” is a long, slow,
dull book—like a railway journey from any-
where to nowhere and round back by every-
where. A kind of encyclopaedia, too, of the
things that struck Mark as he tramped abroad
with his old-age, and his sickness, and his
fatigue—very informing—as much informa-
tion to the square inch as a dictionary,
almost; but the average person isn’t
used to connecting the ideas of encyclo-
paedia and dictionary with the idea
of Mark Twain. And Mark must
try his hand at aphorisms, too, in the Billings
manner—things he never could do. Also,
he rides to death his hobby-delusion of how
a humorous story should be told—as ex-
pounded in ‘Tom Sawyer, Detective,’ &c.,
forgetting that after all his theory pertains
to speech, and speech isn’t writing. Occa-
sionally, as in the dog-deliverance at p. 285,
the old Mark flashes —to make the surround-
ing darkness more obvious. Yet a reader
who wall throttle' preconceptions and content
simself with diffused interest can find plenty
of ponder-matter in ‘ More Tramps Abroad.’
It’s not tense enough for the city ; but it’s
an Education for the man-with-lots-of-time
in the Bush. And the volcano was in its day
a good volcano, anyway ; and if of the ancient
fire there now remain only ashes well,
sale to the ashes !
LULLABY.
Weep you no more, sad fountains)
What need you flow so fast ?
Look how the snowy mountains
Heaven’s sun does gently wastes
But my sun’s heavenly eyes
View not your weeping,
That now lies sleeping
Softly, now softly lies
Sleeping.
Sleep is a reconciling,
A rest that peace begets ;
Doth not the sun rise smiling
When fair at eve he sets ?
Rest you, then, rest, sad eyes,
Melt not in weeping,
While she lies sleeping
Softly, now softly lies
Sleeping.
Anon.
TOM O’ BEDLAM.
The morn’s my constant mistress,
And the lovely owl my marrow ;
The flaming drake
And the night-crow make
Me music to my sorrow.
I know more than A polio ;
For oft when he lies sleeping
I behold the stars
At mortal wars,
And the rounded welkin weeping.
The moon embraces her shepherd,
And the Queen of Love her warrior;
While the first does horn
The stars of the morn,
And the next the heavenly farrier.
With a heart of furious fancies,
Whereof I am commander :
With a burning spear,
And a horse of air,
To the wilderness I wander.
With a Knight of ghosts and shadows,
I summoned am to Tourney :
Ten leagues beyond
The wide world’s end ;
Methinks it is no journey.
Anon.

Various Verses .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE CHILDLESS WIDOW
She sat behind her curtained window-pane,
And fed her yearning, hungry, angry eyes
On men and women who passed up and down.
On one side lay the busy, bustling town,
And on the other, under smoke-dyed skies,
A jet of bay behind a mist of rain.
She- thought of Love (O Love, thou’rt every-
where !)
Of melting looks and tender, subtle sighs,
And lingering hand-in-hand, and amorous words
I hat probed the soul of sense like steel-bright
swords,
pressure of hot lips, and rhapsodies
Of thought-drunk brains in days and nights that
were.
But soon her eyes saw, in a startled stare,
A stalwart man beside his mother walk
Down towards the misty bay. His well-poised
head,
Broad chest and massive frame and measured
tread,
And gentle deference to the woman’s talk,
Bespoke the son all mothers yearn to bear.
Against the window-pane her face she pressed,
a wa ' :c^ him with an aching, hungry heart:
And then with angry hate scowled on the pride
lhat filled the mother at her big son’s side ;
And clenched her fists and, with her lips apart,
Hissed curses on the womb which Hate had
blessed.
“ These breasts,” she cried, and smote her hollow
chest,
" Had fed as fine as he ; to-day he’d be
As brave, as gracious-featured, noble-souled.
But here I am—alone, forlorn and old !
While there is she—ah, happy mother, she !
Twain women—this one curst and that o e blest!”
And then her eyes flew upward towards the town,
And, smitten, rested on two women who
Walked slowly on beneath Affection’s spell;
A daughter and her mother, all might tell;
A regal girl in garb of azure blue
Which lighted up her mother’s sombre brown.
The childless widow screamed and drove a brood
Of bony fingers into palms of skin,
And cried, *• O God ! ” and rent the filmy vest
That hung upon her sunken, withered chest.
“ P God ! ” she cried again, “ what nameless sin
Hath cursed me at the fount of motherhood ? ”
“ The bitter years have come and gone again ;
They trod upon me with their iron feet
Till all my shameless frame was beaten flat,
And I called Death to slay me where I sat.
But Death came not, and here I sit and eat
My soul away behind this window-pane!
" Oh, but to hear the voice a mother hears,
And see a new-born self rise from my breast,
In man or woman, nourished with the blood
That filled my being in tumultuous flood !
What have I done that others should be blest,
And I accurst to barren, lonely years ? ”
C.C.
THE BREATH OF LIFE.
(History is the soul of a landscape).
O, dark, inexplicable mystery
Of human life ! So frail, so poor, so trite.
It fills the universe with warmth and light,
And bids the uncouth giant worlds to be.
The very granite ranges yonder, dead,
Dead and forgotten since the dawn of Time,
Will live, and harbor dreams, and murmur rhyme,
Repeating softly what some lips have said.
If man were by some chance to pass along,
To build his cottage in a hidden vale,
To laugh and weep and die—his flimsy trail
Would turn those iron walls to gates of song.
The sightless eyes of sombre desert meres
Would gleam with tender consciousness of life
If love, or hate, some bygone human strife
Had hallowed them with laughter or with tears.
God made the world. But mankind bids it live,
And, laying hands upon a stone, perchance
May fill it with the glamor of romance,
Suffuse it with that soul no God can give.
VON KOTZR.
“NIGHT."
Night in mid-summer ! An enchanted Night
Treading with soft steps on the robes of Day ;
Sending pale shadows in the west to play
Amongst the splendors of the sunset light.
A silver crescent on the eastern height
Sails slowly up the star-enjewelled way,
And on the river trembling moonbeams lay
A charm scarce ripple-broken—clear and bright.
Hail to the Night! To her pale Sorrow brings
Her wrongs for comfort, and the eyes that weep
Through smiling Day are hushed in silent sleep.
The inarticulate moans and murmurings
That thrill the sky are but the passing wings
Of Life and Death, who whisper as they sweep,
“ I sow the seed,” and “ I the harvest reap.”
ALIOE M'KINNON.
MILKING-TIME.
Click ! clack ! clicketty clack !
Down the river and up the track !
Molly and me !
Me and Molly !
Peggy and Pansy, Daisy and Dolly,
Up to the milking-yard and back
With a click ! clack ! clicketty clack !
I’m a king and Molly’s a queen—
Here in the milking-yard, I mean.
Me and Molly !
Molly and me !
Over the ridge by the quandong-tree,
Round by the panel beside the stack
With a click ! clack ! clicketty clack !
Ponk ! Pank ! Ponkety-pank !
Through the stubble and round the tank !
Molly and me !
Me and Molly !
Wisdom’s ours ! all else is folly !
While hobbles jingle and cow-bells clank
With a slink ! chink ! ponketv pank !
Queens gets tired, and so do kings,
Doing nothing, and lots of things.
Me and Molly !
Molly and me !
Hot and tired and ready for tea !
Down the river and up the track,
With a ponk ! pank ! clicketty clack !

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
* Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 15, 1898.
The Third Convention.
Next week the third Federal Convention
meets for another attempt at the making of
an Australian nation. If it fails this time
the public will probably be so weary of the
whole futile subject that the very name of
Federation will hardly be mentioned for
years to come. And there is, unfortunately,
much reason to fear that it will fail, unless
the miserably selfish and parochial-minded
politicians who have so far blocked the way
develop a larger soul and a better spirit
of patriotism. The Adelaide Convention
did something, though not very much,
and what it did was done none too well.
The second Convention did less than the first
one. Its principal achievement was that it
practically burst up the financial settlement
arrived at in Adelaide, without putting any-
thing in its place. And the third Convention
starts under worse auspices than the second.
In the interval, Queensland, after much
wobbling and many broken promises, has
decided, definitely it would seem, to stand
out and lead its piebald existence all alone.
New South Wales has broken the Federal
pact by demanding an affirmative vote of
80,000 to ensure the acceptance of the Federal
Constitution, instead of the 50,000 previously
agreed upon. As a sign of the heavy apathy
which overshadows the whole question, the
Federal Finance Committee, which was to
have a scheme drafted for presentation
to the Convention, hasn’t even met, and
Premier Turner, of Victoria, is trying, with
very little prospect of success, to get it to-
gether in time to do something, or to look as
if it were doing something. Hardly any
prominent politician talks Federation now.
The subject has almost dropped out of the
daily press, and the little transient enthu-
siasm of a year ago has apparently fallen
down very dead indeed.
Yet there is every reason why the enthu-
siasm should have grown stronger instead of
weakening. While Australia has been
almost torn asunder by fierce controversy
over the alleged throwing of the ball by
Jones, apd while our butterfly race has been
shaken off its moral base by the downfall of
Bilmartin, three Great Powers have de-
veloped an unexpected agreement for the
partition of China, and powerful enemies
(for all three are the enemies of Britain, and
the enemies of Britain are, unfortunately,
also ours) are growing up at our doors. The
one thing of which Australia may be as cer-
tain as it is of the sunrise is that some day,
soon or late, it will have to fight for its very
existence, and to play a lone hand in the
struggle. This prophecy, which is a very
easy one at the best, is based on nothing in
particular, save the fact that every nation has
to fight for its life sooner or later—-unless it
knocks under without fighting ; and unless
its existence is very short indeed it has to do
it not once,, but many times. Also every
nation, except the very newest, has, some time
or other, had to face stupendous odds alone,
and every one will, almost to a certainty’
have to do it again and again, or else be
blotted out. Australia, in this respect, has
no reason to hope that it won’t share the
common lot. The British connection is no
safeguard. Britain's good luck can no more
last for ever than the luck of any other
country, and the fact that it is 600 years
since Britain.Jms had to fight for its existence
against an invader on its own soil only shows
that the next occasion of the kind is 600
years nearer. Some day Britain will be too
busy hustling in its own defence to look after
this country, and it will be left with some of
Britain’s robust enemies on its hands and
nothing but its own resources to rely upon.
In view of this ever-present possibility, as
well as for many other reasons, Federation is
an absolute necessity. Australia’s six baby
armies, with their six different commanders,
different systems, different weapons and
ammunition, are a howling mockery. If
they were all combined as one force under
one head they wouldn’t amount to very
much ; as six straggling atoms they amount
to nothing. They couldn’t even move easily
to each other’s aid ; if the railway systein of
one province was disorganised by invasion,
and it couldn’t send its own rolling-stock to
the borders to bring reinforcements along,
the break of gauge would prevent the others
sending assistance. And it is abundantly
evident that nothing save Federation—in-
cluding a Federal railway system—will
settle the break of gauge difficulty. Also,
there is no guarantee, in view of provincial
jealousies and divided control, and the panic
and confusion which would naturally arise
when a community whose main desire is to
make 740 runs struck an entirely new danger,
quite different from the customary danger of
getting its leg before wicket, that there would
even be an attempt at combined action.
* * ♦ *
The alleged difficulties in the way of
Federation are so miserably small and futile,
and the need for union is so urgent, that the
threatened collapse of the national cause is
all the more melancholy. The injustice of
equal State representation in the Senate
hardly amounts to a row of pins ; Australia
has a century behind it and possibly a thousand
centuries in front, and before two of these
centuries are past the inequality may have
redressed itself and new inequalities have
arisen, and the balance of population may
have shifted half-a-dozen times, and the
“large” provinces of to-day may have
become small ones and then grown into
large ones again. If there was any possible
reason to believe that the one province
would always have the largest population
and the other one the smallest, the dis-
crepancy would be worth considering, but this
country has hardly even begun to take
definite shape yet. The tariff difficulty is a
bogey of the same order. It is believed,
upon the strength of elaborate calculations,
that with a uniform tariff Westralia would
pay far more than its fair share per head
to-day. If Federation had come along yes-
terday it would have been New South Wales
which would have paid the largest amount
per head. A few years further back Yictoria
would have been the principal loser; and,
judging by present appearances, a few years
lienee it will be Queensland which will have
most reason to object on the same ground.
There never will be a time when some pro-
vince can’t plead that it will probably pay
more under a uniform tariff than some other
province ; but, as every province gets into
that position in turn, one inequality about
balances another. Just now Westralia has
the best-paying railway system, but S.A.,
Yictoria, and New South Wales have all been
in the same situation in turn. Probably
they will all be there in turn again. If the
railways were handed over bodily to the
Federal Government, undoubtedly the pro-
vinces which had for the moment the most
profitable systems would lose something, but
as the same province never has the most
profitable system for seven consecutive years,
and there are a few thousand consecutive years
to reckon with, this difficulty also amounts
to really nothing. The alleged obstacles in
the way of Federal river-control are practi-
cally a myth. Most of the rivers, apart
from the infinitesimal quantity of them
which is drunk when the scanty adja-
cent population can’t get beer, and the
other infinitesimal quantity of them which
the adjacent population occasionally washes
itself with, are mainly used in poetry as the
place that the man who died of thirst was
trying to reach but didn’t. And the lone
swagman in the poem could die trying to
reach them just as easily if they were under
Federal control as the other way.
* * * *
There is not one of all the alleged insuper-
able obstacles which would really amount to
anything, provided there was a genuine
desire to make a strong and useful national
Government. When Australian politicians
fall over these straws and call them lions in
the path they only advertise their miserable
lack of resource. If the federating provinces
would throw into one common stock their
public debts, railways, posts and telegraphs,
customs duties and educational and military
systems, without any elaborate attempt
whatever at the adjustment of accounts,
there would be so very little loss on any side
that the discrepancy wouldn’t be worth argu-
ing about. • It is the struggle after a mean
and soulless accuracy in the matter of the
odd halfpenny—which is only an alleged
halfpenny after all—that makes the situation
so hopeless, and more money has already
been spent in reporting the Convention de-
bates over the financial discrepancy than the
financial discrepancy amounts to.
* * * *
And, meanwhile, the biggest opportunity
in history is being fooled away. This is the
one occasion in all the world's record, so far
as we know it, when there has been a chance
to take peaceful possession of a continent
and place it under one Government, and
under the control of one nation, with a
common language, with no mediaeval tradi-
tions to live down, no serious internal feuds
to eradicate, and no long, horrible, blood-
stained Past to rise up in judgment against
it. Washington and his contemporaries
had no such opportunity in the United
States ; neither had Bolivar in South
America, nor Hidalgo in Mexico ; nor the
makers of the Swiss republic, nor the creators
of new Italy, nor any of the other nation-
builders that we know of. And no one
else will ever have such another oppor-
tunity while the world remains as it
now is, for there are no more continents
to be occupied, and there is no room
to build another new nation anywhere,
unless an old one is first butchered out of the
road to make room for it. Probably the
last men who had such a chance were Cain
and Siiem, and it is a far cry from Shem to
George Reid, and Tozer, and Forrest.
There is something wildly ironical in the idea
that the biggest opportunity in a few thousand
years of history should have been put in the
way of Henry Dobson, and Tozer, and
Nelson, and Sunday Brunker, and Reid,
and Holder, and Lyne, and the other small,
ordinary potterers, who are busy fooling it
away about a small matter of twopence in the
accounts. But lift is mostly made up of
such ironies.
Reid’s Latest Facts.
Unless the two Sydney morning papers have
both been shamefully misreporting Premier
Reid’s recent speech at Mudgee, that truly
awful person must have lost the last remnants
of his accuracy —what he ever had of it. For
instance, the Telegraph represents him as
saying (and the Herald and Evening News
give exactly the same figures and assertions in
slightly different words) :
He desired, by a few figures, to show that these state-
ments were entirely false. Since the Government came
into office it had reduced the expenditure per head of
population from £2 18s. sd. to £1 17s. lid. (Loud ap-
plause.) This represented £1,024,000 less than the figures
for 1892, and £835,000 less than the limited expenditure
of the panic .year of 1893.
Now, first of all, the expenditure per head
isn’t £2 18s. sd. or £1 17s. lid., or anything
like these figures ; for the last financial year
(ended 30th June, 1897) it was £7 os. lid. per
head, which is a vast discrepancy. And if the
expenditure on the actual process of govern-
ment (apart from interest and sinking-funds in
connection with the public debt, and railway,
post and telegraph expenses) is taken, the
amount for last year was not £1 17s. lid., but
about £3 55., which is another huge dis-
crepancy. These figures are taken from
that reliable Government publication, the
“ Statistical Register of N.S. Wales, 1597,”
page 787. Further, according to the same
page of the same official publication, the Reid
Government hasn’t reduced the expenditure by
£1 os. 6d. per head, or anything like it. The
total expenditure in 1894, the last year when
the province was working under Dibbs’ esti-
mates, was £7 Bs. 4d. per head ; in 1897, the
last year under Reid’s estimates, it was £7 os.
lid., which shows a saving of 7s. sd. per inhabi-
tant instead of £1 os. fid. And the cost of govern-
ment— apart from the payments on account of
the public debt and the railway and postal ex-
penditure—was £3 9s. 9d. per head in Dibbs’
last year against £3 ss. in Reid’s, which makes
the saving 4s. 9d. instead of £1 os. 6d.
* * * *
The alleged expenditure of £1 17s. lid. per
head would make the total outlay of N.S. Wales
about £2,000,000 a year instead of over
£9,000,000 as it really is, but seemingly Pre-
mier Reid, whose figures look as if they had
been drinking horribly, couldn’t see this.
Again a saving of £1 os. 6d. per head would have
been a gross saving of over £1,200,000, whereas
Premier Reid represents it as £835,000, and
then again as £1,024,000. Apparently l hat in-
credible fat man has no idea what the population
of his own province amounts to, or any thing even
dimly, resembling it. The wildest humor of all,
however, is that the figures quoted are not
expenditure at all, but represent the revenue
derived from taxation. On the previous page
(786) of the “ Statistical Register for 1897 ” it
is set forth that the receipts from taxation in
1892 (Premier Reid got the wrong year also,
for the last year of his enemy’s administration
was ’94) were £2 18s. 5d., and by 1897 they
fell off to £1 17s. lid. Apparently lie had
simply grabbed at the wrong leaf, and thought
the slump in the revenue was the saving in ex-
penditure, and to all appearance it never struck
him that N.S.W. is run at a cost of over £7
per head per annum instead of less than £2.
And (according to the “Statistical Register,”
page 764) the expenditure for ’93 was
.£9,816,875 and that for 1897 was .£9,439,820,
and as the difference between these is £377,000,
where Premier Reid gets his alleged saving of
£835,000 from is a mystery known only to the
gods who are very tight in Valhalla.
* • * »
Again, Premier Reid is reported as saying re
his predecessor, Dibbs :
The late Colonial Treasurer put £BOO,OOO more taxation
on the backs of the people.
According to the “ Statistical Register ” (page
786), the taxation for 1890, the last complete
year of the Parkes Freetrade tariff, was
.£2,748,339. The first complete year of the
Dibbs Protectionist tariff was 1892, and it and

Another hunk of G. Reid, from the S.M.
Herald report :
It had been asked what had the Government done?
In a nutshell, it had done this : It had taken £BOO,OOO
ayear taxation off the people of the country through
the customs. It had reappraised the rents in the
Western district to something 1 like a fair amount, losing
£lOO,OOO a year by the process. The Government had
taken those loads off, and had only put on a new load
of £420,000 a year. How was it, the Government would
be asked, that they could give up £900,000 a year of
money and yet carry on the work of the country ?’ Well,
they had saved a large amount by Civil Service reform
and by other economies.
A much simpler explanation would be that the
present N.S.W. Government never gave up
.£900,000 a year, or anything like it. In 1893
(according to page 786 of the “Statistical
Register ”), the Dibbs Govt, pulled through
with a revenue of £9,499,252; and, in ’94 with
£9,300,743. In 1897, the Reid Government
had a revenue of £9,107,496, so it sacrificed
£193,000 instead of £900,000. Also, its revenue
from taxation shows a decline of less than
£300,000, as compared with Dibbs’ last year,
so again the statement that it sacrificed £900,000
a year is a glaring cram.
* • * *
Also (this is a quotation from the Telegraph’s
report):—
Again, they had been abused for the fact that the ex-
penditure had been extravagantly placed to loan accounts,
but this was not so. From 1883 to January 1, 1895, the
average expenditure from loans on public works was
£2,900,000 per year, and during the time the Government
had been in office it had spent loan money on public
works at the rate only of £1,340,000. (Applause.)
It is a small matter, no doubt, but still, if there
is any accuracy at all in the figures supplied by
the Government’s own official publication
(“ Wealth and Progress of N.S.W., 1897,” page
888), both these assertions are untrue. The
average amount of loan money spent by Reid’s
predecessors for the 12 years quoted pans out,
according to the official figures, fully £113,000 a
year lesn than he alleges, and his own loan ex-
penditure pans out £54,000 a year more than he
alleges. (This is taking the average up to
June, ’97. If it is taken up to December,
’97, Reid’s loan expenditure is still higher.)
And, as both alterations help the Premier to
make good his own case and slander his op-
ponents, it is difficult to believe that they are
accidental. On the other hand, the ghastly
hog-meanness of such misrepresentation and
the shameless lying it would involve make it
difficult to imagine that it is intentional. Any-
how, as a brick to throw at his political op-
ponents, it it very much out of place. Most of
the expenditure from 1883 to 1895 was the ex-
penditure of Reid’s own political crowd. And
the Dibbs Govt, cut down the loan expenditure
continuously; in its last year, when it had
finished most of the works left uncompleted on
its hands by that tremendous builder and con-
tractor, Parkks, it had reduced the year’s loan
outlay to £1,330,000, which is £60,000 below
Reid’s real yearly average. Moreover, the worst
complaint against Reid is not that he spends
loan money but that he spends most of it on
wholly unproductive works which were formerly
paid, in great measure, out of revenue; which
should be paid wholly out of revenue ; and
which are only charged to loans to make a bogus
surplus, and a lying budget, and a sham
economy. And the N.S.W. Premier brings
forward no evidence whatsoever on this head.

The Telegraph also represents Reid as alleg-
ing that he had
Governed the people of the country for a less amount
per head than was ever known in the history of the
country.
If the financier of great circumference did make
that assertion, and it wasn’t pure ignorance and
blundering, then it was something quite out of
the common in the way of hard lying. So far
as the State Statistician’s figures show, 1877
was the first year when the N.S.W. expendi-
ture per head got up to Reid’s level, and there
were many years when the total expenditure
per head was little more than half Reid’s
figure. And, by way of a sudden diversion,
the N.S.W. Premier’s account of his “cash
basis ” system is interesting :
They had now a new system of accounts, under which
the 12 months’ operations are clearly shown on a cash
basis at the end of the year, and every human being in
the country had only to balance the two sides to find out
the exact position of the countiy.
Which is just where the trouble invariably
comes in. In two out of the three complete
balance-sheets which Reid has presented, every
human being w ho balanced the tw o sides of the
account found a huge deficit. And each time
Reid, with his own figures bleaching before
him in the fierce sun of criticism, denied that
there was any deficit. Also, he keeps on deny-
ing it to this day.

The Westralian Slave System.
It is the street harridan who shrieks loudest
when her reputation is questioned. Similarly,
when, as occasionally happens, some example
of brutality to natives, such as the Anderson
horror, comes to light in Westralik, the local
press is at much pains to persuade the outside
world that such a case had never been heard of
before, and that the local nig., under the wing
of the local squatter, spends an ideal existence.
The Anderson case is only singular in that the
perpetrators of the outrage did not go scot-free.
The facts of the case were that several natives
belonging—that is, indentured—to the Ander-
son brothers (Ernest Anderson, aged 21, and
Alexander, aged 18) ran away and were cap-
tured by the two brothers and brought back 24
miles to Bhendhu station, being denied a drink
during the journey and after their arri-
val. The two brothers tied the natives
up and had tea,after which they took turns
in thrashing them with a three-quarter
inch rope, the victims including two girls,
Louie, aged 12, and Minnie, aged 8, whose
backs were found, several days after the affair,
to have “large festering wounds” extending
from the neck to the lower part of the body.
One male and two of the female victims died
three hours after the flogging. A complaisant
coroner’s jury, fearful of lightly interfering with
the customs of the North, found that the natives
died from drinking too much water on their re-
turn to the station, and they censured the
Anderson brothers for the manner in which
they had thrashed the natives. Nevertheless,
the Government ruthlessly preferred a charge
of murder against the Andersons— the local
police having thought that one of manslaughter
would suffice. The younger Anderson subse-
quently died of typhoid in gaol, and the trial
of the other has just been concluded. Four
broken shoulder-blades were produced in court
to show that the nigs, had not died from want
of water. The jury found a verdict of man-
slaughter, and the Chief Justice imposed a sen-
tence of imprisonment for life, the heaviest
which the verdict would allow
* * * *
The local press pleads that the rarity of such
cases is proof that the relations between the
natives and the squatters are satisfactory. The
Bishop of Perth (Anglican) lately visited the
north, and reported, in a despatch to the
Governor on the aborigines-control clause in
the Westralian Constitution, that the treat-
ment of the natives in the north in some cases
was cruel and brutal in the extreme. And
since the Anderson case Chief Justice Alex-
ander Onslow, from his seat on the Bench, has
said he could not forget that he had, 18
months before, tried a case at Geraldton when
a man was charged with flogging a native
to death. The circumstances were “ equally
revolting with those in the Anderson case ” ;
it was “ distinctly proved that the black
had been tied by the hands, lashed to a post,
and there mercilessly flogged to death by two
persons, one taking up the task when the other
became tired.” The defence that the native
had died of some disease of the stomach was an
“ impudent one,” and “no counsel who had
the slightest self-respect, or respect for the
cause of justice and humanity, would venture to
put forward such a defence in a community
whose feeling upon such questions was sound.”
The defence, however, was effective. One man
on the jury, the Chief Justice stated, had the
courage of his convictions, and the jury refused
to find a verdict. And the Chief Justice pro-
ceeds :
I came back to Perth, and asked the Attorney-Gene-
ral to see me in regard to the case. I said to him
that they would never be able to secure a conviction
at Geraldton, and he seemed to agree with me. I then
said that it was the duty of the Government to have
the case tried at Perth, and not at Geraldton. Mr.
Burt replied that the Government would not dare to do
anything- which would be likely to slight or insult their
supporters in that district.
The Chief Justice continues that he makes these
remarks because he is “ convinced that the
public sense has not been properly awakened to
the horrible brutality which has been displayed
towards the aboriginals of this colony.”
* * * *
The Chief Justice and his brother Justices on
the Supreme Court Bench are the only State
servants in Westralia whose utterances are not
subject to the revision of the almighty Knight
of Bunbury. The Chief Justice could not be
peremptorily called to order, and John Forrest
had to descend to the expedient of answering
the allegations. He did it in characteristically
ingenious fashion. He first secured from Sept,
Burt, retired Attorney-General, the statement
that the Chief Justice must have misunderstood
him, and this disclaimer is incorporated as the
chief point of his “ reply ” to the Chief Justice.
The rest of it is mostly bald assertion. Gerald-
ton is 250 miles from the scene of the
alleged Thompson outrage, and therefore the
people there were not more likely to be pre-
judiced than the inhabitants of Perth ; the
Chief Justice had experience only of the few
cases which had come before him, and supposed
that they were typical of the treatment of the
natives ; and he might have “ an inclination to
form erroneous conclusions if he is not very
careful.” The Chief Justice had commented
on the admission of a Justice of the Peace in the
case that he had frequently flogged natives, and
of this the Premier of Westralia says that
If a Justice of the Peach did honestly admit that he
' ad chastised natives, there is nothing discreditable in
i..s saying- so, for it is not reasonable chastisement for
that is to be guarded against, but " horrible
brutality.”
That is to say, the back-blocks beak
may wallop his nigger witli the sanction
of the Govt., and may judge of whether
the walloping is “reasonable chastisement”
or not! Finally, Premier Forrest points
to the report of the police and magis-
trates—the same magistrates who may wallop
their own niggers, and who did their best to let
the Andersons oil'—to prove that the nig. is
well treated. And he both begins and ends his
statement with the assertion that the Chief
Justice must have been misreported—which the
C.J. denies.
* * * *
The spectacle of a Government charged with
being afraid to prosecute the slayer of a nigger
for fear of “slighting or insulting its sup-
porters” is less melancholy than disgraceful.
It suggests first the demoralising influence of
the control of a servile race on a community,
and through the nigger-drivers on their rulers,
who in this case preferred to allow the slayer
to go free to the risk of losing the
support of a few hundred nigger-driving
voters. Fortunately—or unfortunately—the
Australian native promises to disappear shortly,
and the risk of his conversion into a permanent
servile section of the population, like the
American negro, is non-existent. But the affair
contains many morals for Australia, nibbling at ■
various cheap-labor bails. The fact that Wes-
tralia possesses a full-blown slave-system, by
which nigs, are owned under the pretence that
they have “indentured” themselves; that they
are flayed to death, often with impunity ; that
they are flayed “ reasonably ” by magistrates
with the expressed approval of the Premier of
the province ; and that the Government prefers
—rather than risk “ slighting or insulting ” its
supporters by taking proceedings—to let mur-
derers go free, are merely local circumstances.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Honest Auditor.
The law of N.S. Wales gives a mighty lot of
encouragement to the municipal auditor who
tries to do his work fairly and honestly. Re-
cently the borough of Rookwood, near Sydney,
elected John Groves as auditor. John Groves
panned out quite differently from the usual
slummocky individual who audits municipal
accounts, and certifies to their correctness year
after year, until it is found out that the money
had been regularly embezzled for a-quarter of a
century or so, and who attests to the correct-
ness of books, some of which were never
added up, or were never shown to him,
or never existed. In the first place,
he refused to sign the abstract of accounts
until he had gone through the books
and ascertained that the abstract was correct.
Also, he refused to go on with the audit unless
all the books and accounts were placed at his
disposal as provided by the Act, and the audit
was suspended for a considerable time, and
there were long arguments and appeals to the
Colonial Secretary before this difficulty was
got over. Then this quite unusual auditor
proceeded to actually audit the accounts. In
his report he pointed out that many receipts
were unstamped, that many documents were not
properly signed, and that a multitude of
small discrepancies made the accounts confused
and incomprehensible. He also pointed out
that some of the general rates were wrongly
appropriated to pay for the site of a new town-
hall ; that the cost of Jubilee decorations was
improperly taken out of the money for road
repairs, &c. ; that there was a wholly unlawful
payment for law-expenses, the item not having
been sanctioned by the Council; that certain
small sums were not properly accounted for ;
that the bank-book and cash-book didn’t agree
in some respects; that the purchase of a
piano on credit was a wrongful expenditure
of ratepayers’ money ; that 34 pages of
one account-book were wrongly added; that
the Council had a wholly illegal overdraft ;
and many other things. And this surprising
auditor, John Groves, actually refused to
certify that the accounts were correct when, in
his opinion, they were a long way from that
condition, though apparently a co - auditor
was willing to sign in the usual perfunctory
fashion. The overdraft matter is especially
notable. About half the municipalities in
N.S.W. have illegal overdrafts, and their audi-
tors — except John Groves— always state that
everything is right ; and the Government, in-
stead of enforcing the law' and making the
aldermen personally responsible for their
grossly unlawful conduct, passes every year a
retrospective Act to protect them against any
legal consequences of their wrongdoing. Any-
how, the Rookwood Council summoned its
auditor before Parramatta police court, under
an Act which is said to have been repealed. A
Government auditor, called as a witness for
the prosecution, admitted, according to
the printed report of the Cumberland
Times, that there was an error in the
balance - sheet, and he himself wouldn’t
have signed it in its present condition,
and Groves’ co-auditor admitted, according to
the same paper’s report, that he had signed the
incorrect balance-sheet and had his attention
drawn to its incorrectness afterwards. And
then the bench fined John Groves £2 and
£2 2s. costs for “ neglecting to sign and certify
to the correctness of the balance-sheet,” which
apparently wasn’t correct according to the pro-
secution’s own witnesses. This is good, solid
encouragement for the Honest Auditor.

The Clean (or Unclean) New-Chum
Writes “Bath Bun”:—
Dear Bulletin, —In common with several thousand
other adherents of your “valuable paper,*’ I am more
disgusted than amused by your frequent reviling of
Englishmen (I don’t say Britishers, for Tiie Bulletin
would claim a superiority for the Scotch and Irish even in
this particular) on the score of their personal dislike to
soap and water. Your latest is a foolish fabrication re
the discovery of a decayed corpse among the cobwebs of
a London hotel bathroom. Doubtless the untravelled
Australian expectorator who imagined that incident for
you got his cue from sundry previous yarns which have
entered Tiie Bulletin columns in the ordinary way of anti-
Hinglish business, and which, seeing' that The Bulletin
is the bushman’s Bible, have found many honest believers
in the backblocks I put some straight questions. Do you
really believe that the average English emigrant to Aus-
tralia is a dirty-bodied fellow, who begets “colonial”
children of a cleanlier disposition ? What is your explan-
ation of the alleged fact? Is the ordinary Australian
selector a more washed person than the English farmer ?
Are Stoddaut’s Hinglish Eleven less clean than the Aus-
tralian ditto, and why does the native-born mosquito,
likewise the Australian girl, show such a marked prefer-
ence for new-chums?
The Bulletin replies, more in sorrow than in
blankv, to the above reviling as follows : In
the first place, the man who was solely re-
sponsible for the appearance of the par. com-
plained of isßritish himself. His prejudice on the
subject of English cleanliness probably arose first
through the fact that he lived ten years—from
two to twelve years old—in an English manu-
facturing town of 16,000 population (Kidder-
minster, where they make the shoddy carpets),
in which town, so far as he knew—and in a
place like that almost everybody had some slight
knowledge of everybody else—there wasn’t one
house possessing a bath. He is prepared to lay
his hand on his heart and swear that he
didn’t know of one. He knew pretty nearly
every boy in the extensive grammar-school, and
pervaded the houses of many of them, to the
great discomfort of their parents, and he never
came across such a thing as a bathroom among
the lot. Presumably, everybody washed occa-
sionally in a tub in the laundry, or somewhere,
but apparently they didn’t do it often enough
to miss the bathroom. The first time he met
with such a place was when he removed to
Glasgow, and found three-roomed tenement
houses supplied with it. The same individual,
in his old, unprosperous days, came out steerage
per sailing ship, from London—a three months’
voyage. One night, in the tropics, a spasm of
washing came over the male passengers, and
they had a great, unanimous pumping upon
each other by the forecastle, but the madness
was never repeated. Perhaps that intensified
the prejudice. The writer does reckon that
the British arrival begets children who are a
little cleaner than himself; the visible reason
why they are cleaner is that washing in this
country is more of a luxury and less of a duty.
But the rule doesn’t always hold good. For
instance, the other day he learned that a
young society lady, the daughter of an
English professional man of good standing now
in Sydney, hadn’t washed her head for eight
months. He has the statement on her own
authority, though it wasn’t meant for his hear-
ing. How often the daughter of an Australian
professional man usually washes her head he
doesn’t know, or want to know. She might
wash her head so seldom that he would never
enjoy meeting her again. Anyhow, the writer,
after spending most of 19 years in Maoriland
(both islands), Queensland, Victoria, N.S. W.,
and Tasmania, only saw two men fired out of
the many boardinghouses and hotels he has in-
fested on the ground that they were too
grimy to associate with and they were
both English. One—ejected in Dunedin—
was the mate of an English ship; the
other—in Melbourne—was an English ex-editor.
The ex-editor didn’t take it at all kindly, and
demanded through his lawyer a definite state-
ment in writing whether the old lady who ran
the show did or did not charge him with being
grimy beyond reasonable limits. For the rest,
the responsible person above alluded to doesn’t
know whether the Australian selector or the
English farmer washes oftenest ; he never
attempted to wash either of them. He doesn’t
know how clean the English Eleven is. He has
no idea whether the mosquito bites the new-
chum oftener than the native—probably the
only difference is that the new-chum, not being
accustomed to it, feels it more. If it does
bite him oftener, it may be because of
his extra dirtiness, or again it may not.
And if the Australian girl does show a
marked preference for new-chums, which this
scribe has never noticed (when he was a new-
chum no girl showed any preference for him,
anyhow), that isn’t evidence. One girl, as a
rule, only gets a chance to marry one new-
chum, so she has no experience to euide her.
The new-chum, as a general thing, arrives here
with his clothes on, so that his condition is
rather a mystery. It isn’t the girl who is
going to marry the new-chum, but the woman
who has married him who is an authority.
Does the young Australian widow, who has
just buried her new-chum, generally make haste
to wed another new-chum ? Some information
on this head would be interesting.
The Shiftless Ghostly Comforter.
In a recent lecture, R.C. Archbishop Carr
(Melb.) suggested that the souls of infants who
die unbaptised are possibly kept in reserve to
animate the bodies of future inhabitants of this
world, when the hoary, sin-laden and utterly-
tired old planet shall have been cleaned up and
generally swept and garnished for a perfectly
new experiment. Truly the church has a hard
row to hoe nowadays when it goes into specula-
tions like these. It used to have a compara-
tively easy time of it. In earlier days man
wasn’t so much worried about details as he is
now ; he merely asked for large, robust asser-
tion, He only knew that he came from some-
where that was so large and dark and vague
that he felt scared when he thought of it, and
that he went somewhere which was so emin-
ently uncertain that his hair bristled up at the
prospect. Also, he knew that he was a very
short time here, and a very long time away,
and, feeling a desire for some consolation, he in-
vented a man in long gay rags to tell him (for a
consideration) pleasant things about the place
he was going to. In those days he was so des-
perately anxious to hear something pleasant
to alleviate his horrid dreams about the place
he was going to that he was willing to pay
almost any money for the information, and he
was so anxious not to burst up his only
consolation that he never asked where the
hired ghostly-comforter got his information
from. Now, alas ! he wants so many details
for his money that the spectral supporter has
hard times supplying the information. In the
simple-minded long ago everything that was
superfluous, or in the road, or that couldn’t be
otherwise accounted for, could be disposed of
in one act by saying that it would be damned ;
but this cheerful, off-hand, wholesale mode of
getting rid of uncomfortable problems is getting
out of date now. The community pays a great
deal less money than it used to do, and wants a
great deal more value for it. Where it used to
put Is. in the plate in return for the statement
that the unbaptised infant would be Lost, it
now parts with 3d., and demands that that in-
fant shall have another show. And the church,
in a clumsy, hesitating, tentative fashion, is
trying to give that infant a fresh deal accord-
ingly.
The uncertainty of the validity of legal pro-
ceedings when a Court, without Statute autho-
rity, sits in camera, or what amounts to that,
is an older Australian question than the N.S. W.
Divorce Law itself. The legality of the Nor-
folk Island executions of sixty and seventy years
ago has been impugned by numbers of lawyers,
especially in England, on the ground that the
trials of the accused parties were conducted on
an island which was then a gaol, and conse-
quently, not “ open to the public ” in the sense
that free people could pass in and out of court
when they chose. The general contention
was that the Norfolk Island accused should
have been transferred to Sydney for trial
there, according to the spirit and letter
of British law, and so strong were the
arguments to that effect that if the
accused had been few in number trials in open
court in Sydney would have been accorded
them. But the accused were counted by the
hundred, and the Botany Bay authorities,
realising that it was cheaper to send a Judge,
a prosecutor, and a hangman to the island, than
to fetch the prisoners to the mainland, tried,
and, when convicted, executed them on the
island, practically in camera and therefore
illegally, according to the majority of opinions.
Judicial murders in camera for reasons of thrift
preceded, therefore, by many years, judicial
divorces in camera for reasons of social com-
placence or political favoritism, and, in time to
come, the legal validity of the latter will be
placed on the same basis as that of the former.
♦
Some Q. Ms.P. have been sounded as to their
willingness to extend the term of the present
Parliament. It was known to The Bulletin
12 months ago that a small movement was to
be attempted in favor of a reversion to the
quinquennial system. But to apply to this
Parliament! Audacity out-cheeked ! Of course
the point is arguable as to the term of Parlia-
ments, since any number of years, from 1 to
x is purely arbitrary, but the tendency of
paid democratic Parliaments should be, and
must be, to shorten and shorten till the last
convenient limit is reached. Consider the
power for evil thrown into the hands of a paid
seven-years’ Parliament ! Shortly put: If long
Parliaments, then a clause by which one con-
stituency, or more, or all the constituencies
could compel its or their representative or
representatives to resign at any moment.
Otherwise, the Parliament will ride the people
with foot-long spurs, drawing blood and groans
at every stride. The easiest way being best,
however, short Parliaments should remain.
On the subject of the wretched girls who go as
missionaries to China, “A Man Who Knows”
writes The Bulletin :
I was born at one of the treaty ports; spoke Chinese
from childhood ; and know better than most people what
the natives really think on the subject of girl missionaries.
In a country where females are held cheap, and where
female children are actually killed among the poor as
being in the way, how' can alien females hope to be
treated with even passable decency when living away
from the treaty ports and masquerading in native dress'?
It is necessary for them to know the language well, so
that there is no getting away from the foulness of the
epithets openly levelled against them. They have a silly
idea of influencing the men through the women ; but (hey
forget that the women have absolutely no power in this
way. They are merely bought chattels, and for one pur-
pose only.
If any persons deserve flogging now-a-days it is the
men w’ho—themselves staying in comfortable safety—
cantingly induce girls to undergo this horror. Possibly
they act from good motives, according to their wretched
lights ; hut their crime is black, nevertheless. It is bad:
enough for a man to be a missionary ; for a woman it is
hideous. Ask at any of the treaty ports who is the worst
native, and you will be told a Christianised one. This is
a solid fact. It is curious, too, that the charge of vile-
ness of living we level here at the Chinese is returned
with interest by them as regards the missions, especially
where a man and his w'ife and several single girls live
together in one house, as they often do.
At Suffield’s trial his soft, smooth coat was,
on the suggestion of the Judge, handed to the
prosecutrix. “ Now ! ” said counsel for the
defence, “is that a rough or a smooth coat ?
Remember that a man’s life hangs on your an-
swer.” The injured one felt, hesitated, felt
again. “ Rough,” she faltered. “ Hand it to
the jury,” suggested Suffield in his shirt-
sleeves. The jury felt it—and sniggered.
“But,” summed up the Judge, “you mustn’t
take any notice of the discrepancy of the coat.
That may be accounted for by the difference
between the touch of a man and the touch of a
woman. What is smooth to a: man would be
rough to a woman.” How careful he was to'
remove from the jury’s minds the least impres-
sion in Suffield’s favor ! For the woman had
sworn that the coat worn by her assailant was
like a particular coat which she had described
as “ rough and shaggy.” And Suffield wears
the convict’s rough coat for eight merry Christ-
mases !
“ T D.” on “A Rabid Englishman’s” recent
cursory criticism of the Maoriland woman in
The Bulletin :
It is no use telling the self-reliant- young Maorilandress
that she is too practical, when circumstances constrain
her to forage round for bread and butter. The trouble
originates in the infrequency of marriage; due to the
fact that the Maorilander remains a boy and receives
boy’s wages half through life. Where the Australian
frequently sets up house on £1 a week the average
Maorilander is too Scottish and prudent to do anything
of the sort ; consequently everybody in the province is
acquainted with scores of good-looking girls who are
helplessly drifting into old maidenhood.' But, if the
M.L. maiden is lucky enough to visit Sydney or Mel-
bourne, she can be depended upon to return to "her native'
heath with a husband in tow “Rabid Englishman’s"
other assertion that in M.L. girls are better educated
than boys should not pass unchallenged. Rather is the
education of both sexes —so far as numbers go—in advance
of that in other parts of Australia The community is
already surcharged with lawyers and medicoes ; yet they •
keep on coming, and a Maoriland Napoleon would point
somewhat apprehensively to the fact that every.schoolboy
carries an operating-knife in his lunch-bag. The number
of M.A.’s and LL.B.’s one comes across is simply astonish-
ing, but (with all the necessary apologies to your corres.
pondent) these educated persons are not all girls. —T.D.
“The age of miracles is past,”, said ex-
Premier Dibbs at a recent public, dinner. And
yet there, only a few yards from him, sat Jacob
Garrard as Minister for Education.

SUNDRY SHOWS.
