The third week of “ Charley’s Aunt ” is
now nearly through, and that healthy comedy
fills Sydney Criterion so well that it looks as
if the management might put up another record
with it —supposing it wants a record. Consider-
ing that the “ Aunt ” has already seen much hard
service in Sydney, the present boom may be
fairly set down as something unusual, and if
Frank Thornton ever does suffer from swelled
head, this seems to be the right time for his head
to bloat considerably.
** * #
“Babes in the Wood” goes on its be-
jewelled way at Her Majesty’s, with great success
so far. It isn’t an orthodox pantomime, for which
the public has every reason to be grateful. Most
of it is plain prose instead of the usual badly-
recited long metre which drags so heavily, and in
the main it may be classed as very clever and gor-
geously decorated variety show, with a streak of
the regulation panto, element running through it.
There is a lot of genuine fun in it, which is also
unusual. That walking distillery, Uncle, sup-
plies a good share of the mirth, and Cole-
man and Burke furnish most of the balance.
The shindy between these two unprincipled
characters, in the course of which Burke spits
out nearly 100 teeth, takes well; and the public
yearning for a layer of pathetic prettiness is sup-
plied by the spectacle of Lily Titheradge and
Carrie Moore in bed, with a wilderness of beauti-
ful frill on their nightdresses. And, after a lot of
beauty and some ballet, the story ends suddenly
by Coleman hanging a soiled bag on a nail, and
the transformation-scene is brought on without
any attempt at weary explanation. This also
seems a good idea. The attempt to make a
coherent end to a panto, never results in anything,
and the best way is to give the public just as much
loveliness and fun and dazzle and bird of Para-
dise as it wants, and then stop short at the right
moment by hanging a soiled bag on a nail.
** * *
In the Sydney Royal pantomime the topi-
cal element grows a little stronger as time goes on.
Some remarks about the cricket-match, and the
way Britain will wipe the floor if necessary with
the great Powers in China, have been inserted
here and there, and meet with a gratifying recep-
tion. Otherwise, no unexpected change has fallen
on the gorgeous spectacle, it has a terrific amount
of wardrobe in it—in fact, wardrobe and ballet
are its strong points—and Story Gofton is the
only pantomime giant this paper has ever seen
who looks as if he was all in one piece. Apparently
he has neither stilts at one end of him nor a
purely artifical head at the other, and his arms
sprout out at the right place. There is a lot of
glittersome dance in Rickards’ production and
some clever people, including Miss Lottie Moore,
whose somersault in skirts is a source of perennial
joy. Miss Lottie only throws her feet over her
well-curled ringlet once every evening, but the
audience would gladly have her do it ten times if
possible. Some of the jokes at the Royal are
getting old and spectacled, notably that of
Lauri, when he snatches Addie Conyers’
top note; and they have a way now of
making the same joke do duty three times in suc-
cession, which device may possibly be overdone,
hirst A tells it to B, the joke artfully leading up
to a point at which it is necessary for him to
illustrate something by bashing B hard on the
stomach, and then walking away. When B has
done holding his anguished innards he tells it to
C and bashes him on the stomach, and walks
away. Then C starts telling it to A, not knowing
that he is the inventor of the great idea, but A,
just at the moment when his stomach should be
struck, turns round and hits C instead, and the
wild hilarionsness of the joke lies in the fact that
C gets welted on the stomach twice over. Still,
even with these little drawbacks, the Royal panto,
is a fine affair, and Rickards deserves all the luck
that can possibly come in his road.
** * *
Nothing has happened at Sydney Lyceum
since last report to alleviate the melancholy of
Hobson and Jobson ; and if Dobson had been put
in as the natural representative of Tasmania he
would probably have been just as cheerless as
either of the others. The spectacle of two Pre-
miers drawing a cart like ancient Israelites and
making remarks about the dead and gone Jubilee
is not especially mirthful, and when they conceal
themselves, clothed in a newspaper apiece, inside
two big packing-cases, and the Cat comes and
looks in at them for a great length of time, this
paper’s feelings towards Hobson and Jobson
become murderous. The only thing on this earth
that it really objects to more than Hobson is Job-
son. The Cat, like pantomime cats in general, is
a yellowish, monkey-like animal of no zoological
character worth mentioning. “ Dick Whitting-
ton ” is incoherent as a pantomime has to be,
but it isn’t cheerfully incoherent. There is not
one notably humorous individual in it; and the
costumes are not striking; and much of the
scenery is only moderate; and there isn’t a
single episode which makes the audience sit up
and say “O ” quite suddenly. The Little Boy at
Manly is a crushed and spiritless mite, who does
nothing—in fact, barring Daisy Chard, whom this
anS fr T fc t h prenume > whole-souled enthusiasm,
ana the transformation-scene, and some bits of
scenery, there is little in “Whittington. 55 If
be , Bpre j d T T U , over the Piece, as men-
tioned last week, and Hobson and Jobson fatally
dropped a great distance on to something hard,
lere might be hope. But no pantomime could
bear up against the deep melancholy of Jobson
and Hobson, and against the desolation that would
pervade Dobson if he were there.
** * *
Sydney Tivoli still provides Harry Rickards
with a reliable income wherewith to maintain his
glossy top-hat and his wonderful fur-coat, and his
other glories. It remains a recognised fact that
bydney can t long support all the shows now
raging in its midst, but which show will go under
is no more clear than it was two weeks ago. Amid
all the speculation on this point, however, the idea
° f , th ®. K°>. n K under is never mentioned,
lhe iivoli is an institution now, and one as soon
expects to hear of the sinkage of the Post Office
tower.
** * *
Fitzgerald’s Mammoth Circus and Hagen-
beok’s Menagerie, which goes along with the
circus without any extra charge, have been further
strengthened by a remarkable Japanese troupe of
jugglers. Also, a pony and a riding tiger are to
arrive shortly as companion attractions to the
hiking lion and elephant. The circus continues at
the usual address at Redfern station, close to the
cemetery, and the Benevolent Asylum, and the
Babor Bureau where the aged unemployed go to
look for sand-shifting, and other cheerful haunts.
** * *
Some preliminary arrangements are being
made to get up a benefit in Sydney to that charm-
ing young actress. Miss Elsie Landor (Mrs. Free-
man Kitchen). Miss Landor was just commencing
a dramatic season in Brisbane, she and her late
husband having embarked all their means in the
venture, when the tragic death of Mr. Kitchen
led to the breaking up of the enterprise, and
the young widow is left for the time being in a
position where a handsome benefit would come in
as an unmixed blessing. The Bulletin trusts
that a pleasure-loving city, which likes to combine
an act of kindness with a gaudy night’s entertain-
ment, will move in the matter.
** * *
“The Manxman,” staged at Melbourne
Princess’s on Saturday, has the charm which
Claudian ’lacked. The spectacular tragedy was
mostly turgid nonsense. “ The Manxman ” is a
well-knit story of human nature, strong and quite
convincing except when author-actor Barrett ekes
out his pathetic scenes with a line or two of old,
familiar bathos. The excellence of the production
as a whole makes these defects very noticeable.
Tinsel threads among the gold ; they ought to be
pulled out by the roots.
* * • »
“ The Manxman ” is a great sexual truth,
uncompromisingly stated. Pretty Kate Cregeen,
the inn-keeper’s daughter, falls captive to a well-
educated young man of the “ nice ” type, whilst
her other adorer, all heart and no style, and never
intended by Providence to win the fancy of a
sentimental spinster, flatters himself that Kitty
will gladly hug a “rough-and-ready chap” in
matrimony as soon as he makes a “nest” for her.
Honest noisy chaps will continue to make the
same error, as regards Kitty, until the crack of
doom. Novelists and dramatists encourage it by
seeming to ascribe special virtues to ruggedness,
and dirty nails, and other unavoidable class dis-
tinctions. Nobody emphasises the fact that mil-
lions of uncomplaining female lives are blighted
by the bad manners of the good man at home.
The fine, rough fellow revels in his unloveliness,
though the rooster in the back-yard is for ever
trying to teach him how to impress the fair sex.
Toney Philip Christian hasn’t the grit to marry
his woman, yet he does her no more wrong than
husband Pete, who is too noble and manly and
selfish to make himself attractive to his more deli-
cate mate.
• • • •
“ The Manxman ” dramatised strikes a
false note in the climax, out of respect for some
people’s prejudices. Husband Pete goes his way
to get a decree nisi. He takes a reasonable view
of the rights and wrongs of all three sides in the
domestic triangle, and even wishes the other fellow
good luck. Then the wife notifies the troubled
seducer that he, too, must retire, leaving her to
eat her heart out, in the society of their mutual
offspring, under the paternal roof ! Her father, a
mangy professor of beer and the Bible, tells the
sinner to go inside and brew herself a cup of weak
tea. The curtain drops just here, but it is gener-
ally expected that Philip will formally marry Kate
and properly assert his part ownership in the baby
when her repentance has cooled down. Why not?
* * * *
Of several highly-effective acting charac-
ters, Kate is the finest, and Miss Maud Jeffries
plays it well. She might be rather more easy in
her emotion at times, but it would be hard for any
artist to achieve a better average success. Wilson
Barrett’s performance of Pete is sound and for-
cible, but not fascinating. The Philip of T. Wig-
ney Percyval is good enough—a rather stilted re-
minder of Titheradge; and Ambrose Manning, who
didn’t figure in the “Claudian ” cast, takes first-
class honors as Caesar Cregreen, the loathsome
dealer in beer and Holy Spirit. Also Miss Lillah
M‘Carthy, Horace Hodges, and other members of
the co. earn favorable mention as minor charac-
ters, and Wilson Barrett’s imported setting of the
play justifies the cost of shipping it. “ The
Manxman ” should run to big business in Mel-
bourne, despite unsympathetic comments from
the soulless individual who only wants a good larf
when he goes to the theatre.
** * *
Melbourne Royal show continues to prosper
because it covers a lot of ground and looks im-
portant, whereas the brighter assortment of pan-
tomime talent at the Bijou has nothing solid to
fall back upon. There is not much room for fall-
ing back on the Bijou stage anyway, and yet
“ Robinson Crusoe ” makes a respectable attempt
to seem like a full-sized Xmas annual. In the un-
equal struggle between “Crusoe” and “Dick
Whittington ” The Bulletin gives a moral sup-
port to both candidates and hopes the little ’un
may come up smiling for a few more rounds.
** * *
Dan Barry, as a parson, is the chief attrac-
tion at the Alexandra, since Saturday. Daniel is
a breezy sky-pilot that one waits anxiously
for “The Parson’s Oath,” fully expecting it to be
“d n,” or something even blankier, but the
swear, when it arrives, is merely a solemn promise
to reclaim the girl who bolted with her father’s
murderer. In due course, they destroy the man
of blood and recover the girl, though not until the
police have suffered severely. Haleck dramas are
always rough on the emblems of law’n’order, and
the villain has much forgiven him if he slaughters
a full sergeant in the fourth act.
** * *
The author of “ Annie Rooney ” joined
the Rickards co. at Melb. Opera House on Satur-
day, and was found to be a well-meaning Irish
comedian. Also, a new lady came along without
affecting the position of the Three Hegelmanns,
whose “ turn ” grows more astounding than ever
on further consideration. Meanwhile, Harms-
ston’s Circus and Zoologia has made a change of
programme, and the big maternal elephant is now
appearing eight times a week with the rest of the
company.
** * *
At Hobart Royal the Paulton-Stanley
crowd, preceded by a Firm reputation, lately
opened a nine-night season with “My Friend
from India,” followed by “A Night Out” and
“Niobe.” It was a case of standing-room only
for four nights, when Amy Sherwin swooped
down on her native village and knocked Royal
business endways. That tuneful songstress is
simply worshipped in the Land of Sleep and
especially in Slowbart. During her six nights’
run the back-wash from her doors was heart-
breaking to witness, while inside the building a
perspiring crowd held its breath so as not to miss
a single note. Deane, Kitty Grindley, Haywood
and Sczepanowski filled up the gaps in the per-
formance, but the multitude had eyes and ears for
their own island-bred “ nightingale ” alone. The
public reception given Madame on her arrival in
Hobart was an enthusiastic function, and she
simply knocked ’em silly by hoisting her little
daughter up in her arms for the crowd to look at,
and, arrayed in her dusty travelling dress, sing-
ing “ Home, Sweet Home,” like a heart-broken
prodigal daughter.
At Poverty Point.
Sydney, this Xmas, has had a much more expensive
assortment of shows than Melbourne. And yet managers
prefer Melbourne as a business-" pitch.” It has a rather
larger and more concentrated population, with fewer
outside distractions, and it puts up bigger records in
theatre-attendance.
Comino : “ What glorious heads of hair are displayed in
‘ Claudian,’ at Melb. Princess’s. Surely this kind of ex-
hibition is too much neglected on the stage, with the
everlasting forget-me-not topknots. Miss Jeffries, a
brunette, leads in the wild luxuriance.”
Ada Crossley’s New Year greeting to the “ boys of the
(Sydney) Liedertafel,” sent per medium of conductor
Delany, must surely be meant in a Pickwickian sense, as
most of the members are steady-going Benedicts, who are
all in early o’ nights.
“ The pruning and grafting process has made a mar-
vellous recovery of ‘The Scarlet Feather,’ and W. and M.
are beginning to hope for a long run,” writes Tub Bulle-
tin’s London correspondent. “The decadence of our
Nellie’s voice is partly recognised and will be more so
shortly. Up-to-date gag by Seabrook and Snazelle has
galvanised the dialogue, the latter being especially strong
in his Australian ‘sling-off.’ Swinging his trusty blade
the gaunt comedian asks, ‘ Where is the man who stole
Stoddart’s watch ?’and his ‘down-under’ allusions keep
Florrie Young and Decima Moore in a titter. Altogether
there is much hope of much coin.”
Dan Barry’s announcement that his Alexandra dramatic
co. would give a performance in aid of the Melb. “ sewer-
tunnel tragedy ” fund was followed by another announce-
ment, even more appalling. Melb.’s penny doggerel-bard
informed the ’Euald that he had written a poem on the
melancholy event, and would halve the proceeds of the
sale with the widows and the fatherless.
Wilson Barrett will play “ Virginius ”in Melb.—its first
production these 17 years. “ Virginius”—as The Bulletin
once explained in a naughty little yarn—is very much
“ up against ” the vile seducer who has booked seats for
himself and his giddy gyurl, in complete ignorance of the
awful moral lesson that awaits them. All Sunday-school
teachers should be compelled to attend at least one per-
formance of “ Virgi.” ere it is too late.
Queen Victoria was one of Mr. Thomas
A. Edison’s first constituents in England for
a Phonograph, and now she is to make
a record of her voice on the instrument for pre-
servation in the British Museum. Anyone who
purchases a “ Home ” Phonograph for Jl2 at the
Edison Electric Parlor, 162 Pitt-street, Sydney,
can make records of peoples’ voices ad lib., and
also secure a fine selection of musical and other
records of the highest quality.*

Much curiosity in theatrical circles as to whether the
diamonds lavishly displayed by a certain agent for a
melodramatic co. are genuine or otherwise. A well-
known “push” which congregates outside theatres at
closing-time seems to have its own opinion re the
“ jewellery ” —the agent has never yet been stuck-up.
Binoc: “Be your par. that female royalty seldom or
never sees a music-hall show. Quite recently an Aus-
tralian vice-regal party booked a box at a music-shop for
a panto. Through some mistake they were put on to a
big variety show instead. When they arrived at the
panto., a few lordly words exonerating the management
from all mistake set things right, and the ‘ surprise
party ’ was seated.”

lot of the pantomime-understudy. One ambitious come-
dian had to study practically every male character in one
of the Sydney pantos., and yet—so far as present indica-
tions go—his name will probably get no show throughout
the season of figuring on the playbill.

(log.) : “ Now, my dear, that is not quite the way to do

Melb.: “ The matinee seems to be just discovered. On
Wednesdays we have four : Circus, Royal, Bijou, Opera
House, and, on Saturdays, three, while “ The Manxman ”
is to be added to the list next week. Well, if you can only
manage it, surely this is the pleasantest time to take your
nobbier of theatre.”
No truly loyal person should miss the Biographe’s pic-
ture of the garden-party where the sacred Royal Family
can be seen moving about and eating buns just like
ordinary beings. The most pathetic touch of all is kept
with true artistic perception till the end, when Wales
reaches down and pats a dog on the head. The spectacle
of Tummy leaning over with difficulty to perform this
good office is capable of drawing tears from the eye of a
potato. The dawg seems to feel his position keenly.
. “ Whittington,” at Melb. Royal, claims, with justice, to
give the baldheads more tights for their money than any
previous panto, in the Presbyterian city.
The Firm’s first comic opera venture (“ Scarlet
Feather”) was to be followed—according to the original
plan of campaign—by a revival of “ Tambour Major.”
That sounds promising. “Tambour Major” owed all its
enormous success in Australia—as George Musgrove is
probably aware—to “ dear Duke ” Harwood and John
“ Bambini ” Wallace, the one comedian walking slowly
and deliberately through his part, whilst the other pro-
vided an inanely active contrast. Snazelle is just the
right person to play the Duke on Harwood lines, and they
may find a Bambini of the Johnny Wallace type to play
up to him.
Snazelle is engaged by the National Sunday League to
give his “ Music, Song and Story ” entertainment at
sundry places around London every Sunday night
throughout the winter.
Bel Loel, one of Robin Hood’s band at Her Majesty’s
panto., intends to depart Londonwards soon to join her
sister, Mrs. Franks, formerly Julia Loel, a Melb. girl with
a voice, who is doing some hard study at the R.A.M. She
has won medals for singing, and appeared last month on
the concert platform at St. James’s Hall, London.
Another member of the chorus in the above piece is a
daughter of the late Master of Melbourne Mint, Anderson.
Mr. Anderson was the author of that bizarre poem, “ The
Agnostic.”

and Melba, with their next-of-kin noses, belong to the
Lost.”
Mme. Vanderveer Green has been fairly idolised in
South Africa. Guv’ment ’Ouse was at her disposal occa-
sionally, and the De Beers Co. worked up a native dance
in the contralto’s honor. Among many souvenirs she was
offered a water-bottle, knife, several assegais, and a neck-
lace of home manufacture.
Haresfoot: “What an impressive sermon is the physique
of a Wilson Barrett, compared with that of the familiar
clergyman, of his age—crampy, stewv, limp, round-
shouldered, pot-bellied, shuffly, seeming'as if he trod the
pavement on list slippers.”
Armes Beaumont, the veteran tenor, though almost
blind, is one of the quickest and best solo-whist players
in Melbourne. When he forms one of the party the
others call out the cards as they play them. Beaumont’s
retentive memory does the rest.
Haresfoot: “ Mr. J. C. Williamson’s advertisements
give a hint that an overpowering draw will necessitate
a rise of prices at the Melb. Princess’s some time in 1898.
This attraction must obviously be larger even than
Wilson Barrett. The murmur is that Henry Irving and
Ellen Terry are meant. Musgrove has long chalked out
the Irving and Terry Australian repertoire as ‘ The
Bells,’ ‘Merchant of Venice,’ ‘ Nance Oldfield,’ ‘Olivia,’
‘ Faust,’ ‘ Much Ado About Nothing,’ and ‘ Henry VIII.’ ”
No actor’s grave is so well and persistently looked after as
that of G. F. Cooke, English tragedian, who died in
Boston, U.S., 1812. Tablets show it was restored bv E.
Kean in 1821. C. Kean in 1847, Sothern in 1874. And
Cooke was the hardest drinker in the profession ; the
grave of the sober actor is generally left to take care of
itself.
A London notice of the first performance of “ Claudian ”
says the low-comedy business was hissed, not because it
was low or wicked, but because it was bad. Melbourne

The news that N.S.W. is floating a 3 per cent.
ioan for £1,500,000 in London bursts up a beautiful dream.
About three years ago Premier Reid announced that he
was m favor of borrowing locally instead of in London—
and then he floated a £4,000,000 loan in London as proof
of his sincerity. Said loan realised £96 18s. 3d. or
allowing for expenses, £95 9s. 6d., per cent. After
that the local investor was allowed a show, provided
he took up 3 per cent, stock at £lOO (2s. Bd. per
cent, expenses), so the cornstalk was required to yield
£4 7s. lOd. per cent, more than the price received from
Bull. He responded so liberally, even at this rate, that
the Reid Govt, felt it was necessary to discourage him,
and for a considerable time it refused to take his money
on any terms whatever. Recently it resumed the issue of
stock m Sydney at £lOO, but it is said that the sale goes
on more slowly than formerly, though no figures have
been published. Therefore, the Reid Government
goes to London and offers its loan there at £99
As expenses in London are £1 Bs. 9d. per cent,
against 2s. Bd. in Sydney, this really means that the
Britisher is offered stock at £97 11s. 3d., for which
miserable Australian is still expected to yield
u L 4d l Probably, if the Reid Government had
offered its stock locally at £99 it would have got all the
money it wanted, but it never tried the experiment. If
the beastly local investor won’t pay the full £lOO he must
stand out. Why he should always be expected to pay
more than Bull, the Lord only knows ; probably it is the
Reid fashion of encouraging local industry.
Something is said about the alleged prospective
scarcity of money in the province, but if the Government
is paying its way the flotation of a local loan wouldn’t
make things any worse in this respect. If the Govt,
borrows £1,500 °°° in N.S.W., and spends it on works in
N.S.W., then the money is still in N.S.W., and the opera-

The Mercantile Mutual Fire Insurance Do
r P ° rts prem ium income for 1897 was
£41,52°, compared with £41,050 for ’96, and the interest
mg d r Thp S *' ece , lved were £2021 as compared with £1847 for
and £B96s y fifr r ?«QS° flt -im f10,989 a F a mst £12,136 for 1896
£4is9 n “ o 6 f i 18 ( 5 - f he co. pays £8304 of this away—
£4ls2 as a bonus to policy-holders, and £4152 in paving a
dividend of 22 and a fraction per cent, per annum to
shareholders. This latter is an example of the awful
hunger after premature big dividends. The Mercantile
Mutual, though it is doing very well indeed, is still a
moderate-sized concern ; it has £26,250 paid-up capital,
£40,000 reserve, and £2913 profit and loss balance, but all
these together make less than £70,000. The British idea
of a really prosperous fire insurance co. is a concern with
a reserve of about £600,000, and, if the Mercantile Mutual
fixed its dividend at 15 per cent., which is a sufficiently
glorious figure, and pegged away a little harder at building
up a really big institution, it would be better for it in the
end.
* * * *
Indemnity Fire and Marine Insurance Co.
(Melbourne), a weak concern which has had bad luck of
late, is selling out as the result of the big Melbourne fire.
Its marine business is under offer, and its fire business,
the most profitable part of its connection in recent years,
has been sold to an English co.—the Norwich Union,
u ls V' a , ; s lt! second Australian fire-insurance business
absorbed by British companies inside two months, and
the process goes on continuously. The Argus sets the
collapse of Australian fire-insurance co.'s down partly to
the fact that their field of operation is too circumscribed
—this is evidently meant as a side argument to prove the
impossibility of State fire-insurance—but some of the worst
disasters that happen to Australasian offices arise through
going out of their own circumscribed field. The once-
prosperous Bacific smashed through that, and the Stan-
dard of M.L., some 16 years ago, came within an ace of
disaster through the same cause, and only grew prosperous
again by drawing in its horns. The Argus also ascribes
the steady absorption to the fact that Australian com-
pames hunger too much after dividends, and build up
insufficient reserves—which is true. Yet some of the
absorbed concerns had very solid reserves, and some of
the British companies which do a good business here cer-
tainly haven t. The preference of the unpatriotic Aus-
tralian for foreign institutions seems to be as much res-
ponsible as anything else.
* * * *
The Australian Widows’ Fund, an institution
with a very good reputation for soundness, issues a new
form of policy under which payment of premiums alto-
gether ceases, without in any way impairing the insur-
ance, while the insured is unable to pursue his occupation
through accident or mental or physical illness. This is
an exceedingly valuable idea, seeing that the man who
dies after a long illness, during which his funds ran out,
P’kcn lives just long enough to let his policy lapse through
j P a y premiums, and leaves his family unpro-
vided for after all.
* * • •
Query: Should directors and managers of
public companies be allowed to vote at the election of
auditors ? The business of an auditor is to find out if the
director or manager is a fool, or a knave, or both, or
neither. He represents the shareholders and creditors as
against the directors. If there is anything wrong, as
there very often is, the directors naturally vote for the
easy, incompetent fool auditor who isn’t likely to find it
out. If the auditor does find out anything, the fact that
he owes his position largely, or perhaps wholly, to the
directors is a reason why he should say little; and the
tact that he will owe his position to them quite as largely
next year is a reason why he should say nothing at all. In
many companies the directors, by their votes and proxies,
are solely responsible for the election of auditors, which
is the same as letting a suspected person elect the Judge
and jury which is to try him.

• * • •
A Sydney syndicate is big with a scheme, which
may be described as a fuel economiser, but which is really
?h 1 ?! 2c /! anica ' ev ’ ce f° r increasing the power of machinery.
With the mechanism affixed, it is claimed that 25 percent,
more power can be got from any kind of steam machinery,
or the same amount of power with 25 percent, less fuel.
The scheme is in process of being patented all over the
world, and has, The Bulletin is told, been favorably re-
ported on, and even enthused over, by some very well-
known Australian engineers. The inventor is a marine
engineer on one of the coastal steamers, and he has had
associated with him an eminent C.E. The principle
of the invention is said to be practically something
altogether new and undreamed-of in engineering, and up-
sets the most cherished of all theories of applied mechanics
regarding the length of the piston determining the throw
of the crank.

H.: “Ae the traffic in return-tickets on the
Vic. railways. While Railway Commissioner Mathieson
cannot fail of success in putting down this trade, he will
have to agree with the N.S.W. Commissioner about some
rebate on unused return-tickets. Else the public con-
science will never be persuaded that the tourist agent is
other than a boon to the community. A Melbourner who
cannot use his £6 return-ticket from Sydney must perforce
feel he has been done out of £2. Now let the Department
give that back to him, and the agent’s occupation is
gone. Then the ticket is destroyed, and the Department
gets £4 instead of nothing out of the party who nowbuvs
from the agent.”

A Chinese dealer recently asked a firm of
Southern merchants to increase his credit from £3OO to
£650. The amount being considered rather high en-
quiries were made, and, to the firm’s horror, it was found
that the Chow was on the point of a bad insolvency. It
was recognised that if he knew his solvency was suspected
the firm would get nothing, so when he came in again the
boss received him affably, and entreated him tobuv £lOOO
worth of goods right there. The heathc n was delighted
but somewhat taken aback a little later when it was
stipulated that the little amount owing, £3OO, should be
paid up, “soas to keep accounts square.” Still it looked
too good a chance to miss, so Shoo Fli borrowed the £3OO
from a fellow-conspirator, and came back with it promptly.
But there was a lingering sediment of suspicion in his
mind, and he wouldn’t actually part until the £lOOO
worth was put upon the vans, and the vans started off to his
place. His disgust was indescribable when, on his way
home with the receipt, he met the procession of vehicles
and goods coming back. They had simply driven as far
as his shop door, and the driver had disrespectfully
taken a lunar at the Chow in charge, and then turned
back. Next minute there was a distracted Chinaman
gabbling to the merchant—“ Whaffor ? whaffor v Why
you bling ’urn back tea? You belly well damn,” and
other remarks. The rest of the story was told in a
Chinese schedule filed shortly afterwards : Liabilities
£2556 ; assets, £lO. ’

. B.H. Proprietary, whose parsimony in reward-
ing the men who risked their lives (three actually lost
theirs) and spoiled their Sunday clothes at the Block 12
fire has been universally condemned, distributed among
the men who; have worked in the fire region during the
last few months a Christmas-box of a week’s wages each.
Of course that is a great deal better than the co. need
have done ; still, it’s mighty poor payment for the men—
so.'l'.e , them, anyway. Very few have worked there
'Y 1 !lmf?ly ! many who have done it have lost shift after
shift through sickness—for which shifts they weren’t paid.
B-H- Proprietary calls for “rough miners”;
even Creer’s Labor Bureau was moved in the matter.
Plenty of workmen are hanging about the mine; but
they buck against working in the neighborhood of the
w mch, though “practically extinct” months ago,
still makes fumes enough to sicken the average stomach.

Beetaloo reservoir, which supplies northern
bouth Aus., including the Yorke’s Peninsula towns and
Port 1 me, is running so low that prices have been put up
to check consumption. Among those to feel this are the
B.H. companies which smelt at Port Pine—the B.H.
Prop. co. m particular. There has been talk of shutting
down to force the Govt.’s hand; meantime an arrange-
ment has been patched up. Curiously, the cos. never had
to suspend operations in droughty Broken Hill for want
of water.

* * » •
Frank Coffee, of Sydney, has visited the Cripple
Creek mines, U.S., in the interests of tellurium, there
known as sylvanite. Compared with these mines, he
says, Kalgoorlie shows are much over-capitalised. Cripple
Creek mines are turning out a million dollars’ worth of
gold a month. Miners’ wages, 21 dollars a week of seven
days, eight hours a day. Coffee is assured that tellurium
is just as likely to go down as anything else.
* * * *
More men arrested at the Boulder for gold-
stealing, which is becoming a common practice there.
Tellurides offer much encouragement in that direction
they contain not only a very heavy percentage of gold,
but the thieves soon found out a way of melting them into
nuggets, which, when rolled in the earth, present every
appearance of having been found there. Gold-stealing is
a more serious trouble at Kalgoorlie than English share-
holders imagine.

Absolutely the best monthly circular dealing
witKWestraiian mines is that issued by Clarke and Co.,
of Adelaide. A few items from the Jan. number. With-
out a boom, the market-value of the six leading Kalgoorlie
mines has advanced £4,225,500. The Ivanhoe sale assisted
the mine’s market-value from £400,000 to £1,250,000.
Hu ring the year, upwards of 50 Adelaide co’s have gone
into liquidation, but, being the wildest of wild cats, they
are not missed. Fifteen to 20 more are practically dead.
Adelaide has put up £400,000 in calls and £460,000 in re-
™ctions for year. The total divs. amount to
e va,ue of the gold output is estimated at
£2 /00,000. Kalgoorlie is furnishing more than half the
gold and three-quarters of the divs. Flotations have been
tew and far between, but the year has been marked by
the wholesale slaughtering of “ wild cats ” and reconstruc-
tion of exhausted co’s.

The fall in copper, which lately knocked on the
head some copper-prospecting shows, followed naturally
on an increased output, which was the quick response to
a slight advance m price. Australian shipments for Sept,
totalled 1665 tons in advance of those for Sept., ’96 ; and
® Vjcrease was mostly due to composite mines—Lyell
and the like. The British engineers’ strike is likely to be
a drag on consumption for many months to come.

Croydon and Etheridge miners carry a big
handicap m the extortionate price charged for dynamite
and gelignite—£s 10s. a case. One big firm has a practical
monopoly of these articles throughout the Gulf • and
when a Croydon storekeeper started to sell at £4 10s. his
supply was cut off until he agreed not to sell under the
standard price. Also, the banks charge 2s. 2d. an ounce
tor , the gold, which is often as low in value as 30s
and 20s. They charge the 2s. 2d. on the silver contents
as well and as this is more than the seller gets for it the
banks have the silver for nothing—which in gold of poor
quality amounts to a considerable item. Average value
of Croydon gold is under £2 10s., so that the banks charge
something like 5 per cent., and on the field’s last-year out-
Pin non ° m they must have cleared £BOOO to
4>1u,000 profit for the mere handling 1 .

Sydney E. News society item :
Mr. Harry Yates, of the Postal department, Tocumwal,
wno left yesterday for Inverell, ori promotion, was, before
leaving, the recipient of two testimonials of the esteem in
which he was held. He was entertained by the ladies at
a social, and presented with a handsome counterpane,
silk and plush crazy-work, having nearly 200 autograph
signatures worked thereon in silk, the centre being hand-
painted ; and, in the evening, a number of leading resi-
dents entertained him at Burbury's Hotel, and presented
him with a purse of 30 sovs.
Of course, Mr. H. Yates is a bachelor—presumably
young, and with a silky moustache. Just think
of the joy of smoking in bed while covered with a
counterpane signed by 200 women ! Probably no
man who wasn’t a curate (or a shah, or a Mormon
elder) ever before had such a blissful experience in
all the history of the world.
* » * *
The latest instance of the curious oc-
casional results of the practice of naming vessels
after women is Sydney D.T.’s sub-heading re the
Balmain ferry-steamer fire —“The Lady Hampden
Gutted.” About the most startling effect of the
kind that The Bulletin remembers was produced
man y y ear s ago by the announcement in a
Melbourne press shipping-column that the
Viscountess Canterbury had been taken into
such-and-such a dock to be cleaned and coated
with anti-fouling composition—the part to be thus
dealt with being gravely specified without any
apparent consciousness of its paralysing apropos.
m . ri 9 ■ * ,
He Sydney Transit Commissioners’ recent
wholesale condemnation of antique and defective
cabs, The Bulletin suggests that the Cominis-
sioners should go a step further and permit the
licensing of no cab that is not sliding-windowed at
the sides and ventilated at the back, or that is
doored in such a way that the occupant, once
closed in, is the cabman’s absolute prisoner. Too
many of the existing vehicles are, in these respects,
either heat-traps or death traps, or both, and the
shding-door arrangement, besides rendering the
passenger helpless in.case of accident, is freight
with other very undesirable possibilities.

Again the much-maligned climate of
Bananaland. During the recent heat-wave no less
thnn 35 deaths from heat-apoplexy were reported
from Victoria. Meantime the' Q. death-rate
didn’t rise anything above the usual, and a great
portion of that sultry land rejoiced in a tempera-
ture seldom rising over 85deg. in the shade,
Torrid Rockhampton, with its Sheolic reputation
is, contrary to general repute, a most healthy
city—its death-rate being considerably below that
of Melbourne and other Southern cities. But
better die at Melbourne than live at Rockhamp-

* * * *
In torrid Croydon (N.Q.), a woman,
charged with “ langwidge,” said she had done it
under the provocation of a counter-irritant mus-
tard-plaster about her loins. The plaster drew
out the langwidge, and, if his Worship’s loins had
been burnt up like hers, probably he would have
volcanoed at the mouth. If his Worship didn’t
believe her, she’d show the court the burns ! And
she leaped into the middle of the court-room and
started to undress. Three policemen muzzled her,
and she was handed over to a local medico for
examination. The doc. declared that she was
frightfully blistered, but the well-oiled beak ruled
that a mustard-plaster was no excuse for
langwidge.
* * * *
Menzies (W.A.) is evidently the ideal
place to be gaoled at. judging from the recovery
from the nearest pub., on Xmas Day last, of a
“prisoner ” with a bottle of whisky and two pick-
locks in his possession, and from the assertion by
the police-sergeant that the recent contraction by
the culprit of a certain disease showed that he
must have been roaming in and out of limbo
pretty much at his own sweet will.
* * * *
The Australian Jack Sheppard seems to
have turned up in a fellow who broke out of
Perth gaol, and has just done the same at Ade-
laide. The strangest Australian case of prison-
breaking is that of Anne Miniver Davis. About
20 years ago she was convicted of the manslaughter
of her employer, at Brighton, Melb., by poison,
and there was much indignation at her getting off
so easily. Though her sentence was not long, she
took the trouble to escape. Being employed in
the household of a former Melb. gaol governor,
she managed to walk away. The authorities were
so ashamed that they gave no information to the
press. Indeed, they seem to have resolved to let
her go altogether on the quiet. However, after
she had been at large some months, a policeman
concerned in the case saw her, and, most unwel-
comely, took her back to gaol, knowing her
period was not nearly up. This time it did get
into the press, with a vengeance. A prisoner once
escaped from Melb. gaol by the novelistic device
of the strips of blanket. The other day there was
a bush case of the prisoner locking the constable
up and taking an unobtrusive departure.

The “respectable” press of Sydney
started off by calling the Bennett divorce-case a
“Society Divorce Case,” and then, as the squalid
aspects of the affair developed themselves in
| columns of avid type in the evening papers, the
captions of the morning papers dropped the
“Society” and labelled the suit “A Dentist’s
Divorce Case.”
* ♦ * «
During the hearing, at Sydney, of the
Bennett divorce-case, which extended over the
Christmas holidays, a sympathetic outsider invited
Judge and jury, counsel, and all the parties to the
suit, including witnesses, to a launch-picnic on
Boxing Day. This unique and well-meant kind-
ness did not, however, “ come off.”

The locals kick against the frequent refer-
ences to Mildura as a town where liquor-prohibition
has failed. It never was a prohibition settlement,
they say. In the Mildura (official) Red Book, 10
years old, it was set out that, “ while there is to
be no promiscuous and enticing vending of intoxi-
cating drinks, there will, of course, be no inter-
ference with personal liberty as regards the private
consumption of wines and spirits in any way what-

* * * * .
There was a great rush of Greeks to wit-
ness the ceremony at the grave of Greek Comino,
Sydney's chief oyster-purveyor, and the first of
his countrymen locally buried according to the
rites of the Greek Church in Australia. The
priest, a recent arrival in Sydney, made his spec-
tacular debut on this occasion, wearing a black
silk gown “relieved” with a scarf of pink and
yellow, and on his head the long black cylinder of
his faith. The office of priest in the Greek Church
is, on such occasions, no sinecure, as he must walk
to the ground ahead of the defunct, do most of
the singing himself, throw the dust into the grave
with his hands, and afterwards go round among
the friends and relations with cake and wine. At
one time during the ceremony a riot seemed im-
minent, the officials refusing to allow the deceased
(who was buried in full evening dress and all his
jewellery) to be interred with his feet towards the
sunset, as his religion requires. The disturbance
was quelled, however, by the priest undertaking
to alter the position of the remains on a future
occasion.

The longest criminal trial in N.S.W.
annals is the just-concluded libel-case, Carruthers
v. Lonsdale and Norton. The venue was Armi-
ar^est fee ever paid a barrister in
JN. o. vv., 2000g5., was earned by Dailey, for an
unsuccessful defence of a gilded cattle-stealer,
tried at Armidale —which thus holds two legal
records. Armidale is a quiet, easy-going little
place, remarkable for nothing much beyond its
churches, schools, and pubs., and the stolid con-
servatism of its population. It is just the sort of
baby city wherein Mark Twain might have con
ceived his champion idea of “ restful Australia.”
* • * »
Acting-Justice Backhouse will no doubt
receive a vacation spell for the period spent in
harness over the Norton-Carruthers case. But
why should not the courts sit all the year round?
Justice needs vindication in hot months as well
as in cold. In the early days of Australia, Judges
divided the year among them, and the courts
were open every month.
* * * *
The Norton libel record is mild reading in
comparison with some of those made in the early
days. Take that of E. S. Hall, editor of the Syd-
ney Monitor. Hall was found guilty of libelling
Gov. Darling, 15th Sept., 1829. Sentence 12months’
imprisonment. On Dec. 21, same year, he was
found guilty of libelling the same Governor, and,
two days latter, was found guilty of libelling
James Laidley and F. A. Healey. On December
,?. ne later still, he was found guilty of
libelling Alex. Macleay. These are cases in which
verdicts of guilty were returned. The peppery
officials of the early days went for a newspaper
man with the club of criminal libel in both hands
if he happened to print their name with “ Esq ”
or some similar tag or title at the end.
* * * *
The point taken by John Norton at the
opening of the recent big libel action— “that Judge
Backhouse should not sit because lie had once
been attacked by Truth,” was rather naive, as
the Judge who has never had an open letter
written to him by J.N. has yet to be born.
Judge “ First Offender ” Backhouse got even with
John, however, when he explained that he was
not aware that Truth had ever attacked him.
No Judge “ evah reads the papahs.”
The gigantic M ‘Sharry v. N.S.W. Bail way
Commissioners arbitration case will, according to
one of the interested parties, extend well into
August next. As the arbitrator and advocates are
politicians as well as lawyers, provision is to be
made for Federal and electoral business, whenever
either intervenes.

WHEN COMES THE CHANGE.
(A Rondeav.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When comes the change from blazing heat
And south winds fan the gasping street,
W e find in Life the old-time zest,
And take the thunder to our breast,
And face the storm with willing feet.
And when hard-up, stone-broke, dead-beat,
How joyously that day we greet,
How burns the bullion in our vest.
When comes the “ change ! ”
But when Life’s past its last conceit
And Death comes nigh on pinions fleet,
Say, shall we welcome it as Rest
From days we cursed and days we blest?
Will failing lips form “ Sad ! or “ Sweet! ”
When comes The Change ?
H.C.C.

Miss Graham, the humble nurse who started
the-tremendous-Adelaide Hospital rows which for
three years'have been turning S. A. upside-down
and influencing every political and municipal
election, was appointed superintendent of nurses
the other day, whereupon five other nurses imme-
diately resigned. If the Hospital rows, still as
bitter as ever, last much longer, Margaret Graham
will probably take on the Premiership herself.
* * * «
Charters Towers calendar of unpunished
crime for the past year includes murder, dynamite
explosion, arson and mutilation, to say nothing of
a crowd of comparatively trivial offences. The
Towers is the most prosperous city in Bananaland
—perhaps in Australia. Hence—beer, booze,
sport, speculation, and crimp. You can’t confine
your full-fed, full-blooded man in the same con-
ventional traces as the starveling of a city slum.
The most law-abiding man on earth is the
withered, wizened Chow from Hongkong or
Canton.
• * * *
“ Always see that your husband’s shirt-but-
tons are sewn on,” says one of the artless cacklers
of Sydney Sunday Times. The up-to-date woman
doesn’t sew buttons on her husband’s shirts ; she
abolishes buttons and provides the old man with
studs.
* * * *
Melb. : “ I was not aware, until to-day,
that a beginning had been made with the Vic.
Women’s and Children’s Hospital, for the treat-
ment of patients solely by lady doctors, of whom
eight appear on the board, including three sisters
Stone. The premises are next to the Welsh
Chapel, Latrobe-street. There are just upon a
dozen thoroughly qualified lady doctors practising
in Melb. Probably the number will equal that of
the men early next century. The strange thing is
that Bishopess Goe, Dowager Lady Clarke, and
the other influential ladies of the regular Women’s
Hospital, are able to keep down, or out, the lady-
doctoring there. The senior men doctors are not
such opponents as the junior ones, represented by
those who tried to make life not worth living for
the ladies at Melb. Hospital. But a little
thought will show that the juniors are the most
affected.”
♦ * * *
Dear Bulletin, — “H.” mourns the alleged
barbarism which deprived his brain of interna-
tional cable news at his Christmas breakfast-table,
but seemingly fails to appreciate the feelings of
over-driven pressmen on the point. Heaven post-
pone the time when the proprietaries of Australian
dailies decide to bring out their journals 365 days
in the year—the first Sabbath issue should have
turned column-rules to mark the pressman’s Black
Sunday.—T.D.

Whenever St. Bedstead, of the Review of
Reviews, refers to Queen Vic. —and that’s often—
he always wants to bring in his pet anecdote re
how the morning after her marriage Her Gracious
diaried that Albert was “ the dearest, purest of
creatures.”
The hysteri-
cal young wo-
man with the
curdling story
about a man
with a rough
coat has turned
up, with varia-
tions —and these
variations fur-
ther illustrate
the length to
which the hallu-
cination, or
whatever it is,
will carry the
young woman,
and the cautious
manner in which
her evidence
should therefore
be sniffed at.
This young wo-
man with the
variations ap-
peared about
sunrise the other
morning at the
Broken Hill
police- station,
hereyes red with
crying and her
general appear-
ance that of a
penitent who
had been wrest-
1i n g mightily
with conscience
for 48 hours,
meal - times in-
cluded. Having
made sure that
she was in the
right shop, site
quietly but reso-
lutely demanded
to be taken into
custody on the
charge of mur-
dering her in-
fant. In the three days since it had been buried,
she said, she had had no peace of mind. The
officer in charge smelt her breath, and that passed
muster ; but (probably because he happened to be
very Scotch) he made some inquiries and
then told her to go home and reduce the
confession to order, and after porridge he’d
see about it. However, the vvoman’s sister
presently came along with incriminating let-
ters ; witnesses swore, too, that they saw the
youngster alive and kicking one hour and
found it dead the next. At that point the
young woman wouldn’t have had much diffi-
culty in getting locked up for 10 years or so ;
still, on the off-chance, the body was dug up
when a doctor gave his verdict that death arose
solely through convulsions. By this time the
hysterical mother had got over her desire to be
hanged, and withdrew her statements. But if
the remains had been so far decomposed that the
cause of death couldn’t be ascertained, her posi-
tion would have been precarious. Now, if a
hysterical female can’t be fully trusted even when
she asks the law to hang herself, there must be
considerable space for suspicion when she asks it
to hang somebody else.
THE ROAD TO VIRTUE.
STATE SCHOOL-TEACHER : “ They are going to send
me up to a place in the bush. I’m afraid it
will be so awfully lonely.”
HER FRIEND: “Never mind, dear; if you are
awfully lonely you will be sure to be awfully
good, and if you are awfully good you ought
to be awfully happy!”
Sydney’s own little Princess Alice disaster
will not happen at Circular Quay, but somewhere
near the Heads —perhaps when something goes
wrong with a heavily-laden boat in a big beam-sea,
far away from any chance of assistance. On the
last holiday I travelled by a ferry-steamer which.
—spite of Marine Board or Water Police—carried
far more than should have been allowed on board
her. When coming up the harbor, a capsized
dingy was encountered and the steamer tilted
dangerously as seven or eight hundred thoughtless
persons made a simultaneous' rush to starboard to
get a better view. Had this incident occurred ten
minutes earlier, when the -overloaded vessel was
struggling to cross the Heads, black crape would,
probably, have risen to a premium in Sydney
drapery stores.

The Manlyites have at last arisen to protest
that they don’t run The Village simply for the con-
venience of holiday-making ’Arries and Hemmas.
The local aldermen have decided to protest to the
Board of Health against the continuance of that
most hideous of all modern instruments of torture,
a steam merry-go-round, which lately set up on a
vacant piece of land in the main street.
* ♦ • *
In every ease where adultery is made the
ground of divorce, the Argus prudishly substi-
tutes the word “misconduct.” Which is morally
and legally false, as “ misconduct ” is not neces-
sarily a ground of divorce. By the way, the
Argus, in writing of a recent industrial disaster,
alluded to the laborers concerned as “ Mr.” Smith,
“Mr.” Jones, and so on. The transparency of
which bid for horny-handed support is too
ludicrous.
A burnt-out Tas. settler tells Hobart
Mercury :
The water in Mountain River, where the fire crossed, is
full of roasted fish, many hundreds lying about, the water
being very low.
Now, fish-liars !
* • * *
The Tas. bush-fire recalls the fact that on
the Victorian Black Thursday—lBsl—cinders and
burning twigs from the blazing Gippsland forests
were blown right across the Straits on to the beach
of Northern Tasmania.
* • * *
Six lives were lost in the Tasmanian bush-
fire oh New Year’s eve. Hundred of acres of
fruit-gardens were scorched to cinders, and 50 fami-
lies rendered homeless. Never since 1841, says the
oldest inhabitant, has a Tas. bush-fire moved so
fast, and then ’twas on the midland plains. The
latest holocaust happened in the big scrub of a
damp coastal district.
• * * *
Henry Dobson’s pretty little chalet, which
nestles up among the fern-trees on the foothills of
Mount Wellington (Tas.), and was much fre-
quented of local sassiety, was the other day totally
destroyed by a bush-fire. All the furniture and
fittings went up in smoke. ‘ We have lost every-
thing in the place,” almost wept poor Viin
Dobson. “We saved nothing of value, except my
Federation papers ! ” Talk of anti-climax !
* * * *
A certain M.P., when travelling with the
Q. Land Commission, would drink nothing but
champagne, and nothing but a particular brand
of cham., at the expense of his bleeding country.
The coach by which the Commission was travel-
ling, in the back areas, came to a township the
next stage from which would cover two whole
days before champagne could be had. The M.P.,
equal to the occasion, took aboard a case of his
own particular brand for his own particular use.
He chose the inside of the coach, seemingly in
order to guard his treasure. His fellow-commis-
sioners were on top. A dry, fearful day. The
M.P. fell asleep. One by one the bottles in his
case were withdrawn, the corks carefully manipu-
lated, the contents drunk, the bottles refilled from
the water-bags, and the corks replaced so as to
resemble as nearly as possible the real thing.
“The poor M.P. got none!” At the next halt
he had his case taken carefully into his room at
the accommodation-house. What he discovered
there, what he felt, and what he said are “ best
known to hisself by gum ! ”
* * * «
Dear Bulletin, —On the authority of poet
Byron and innumerable others, it is generally
assumed that immorality is mainly a question of
temperature. Which dictum writer cheerfully
disputes and propounds one of his own, viz. :
illegitimacy is mainly a question of Piety. The
illegitimacy-rate of torrid Italia is not appalling,
while that of Scotia is the highest but one in
Europe. Proportion of extra-conjugal offspring
is not much above average in godless Cooktown,
but holy Ipswich, sanctum sanctorum of Banana-
land snufflebusters, heads the bastard list of its
sun burnt province. . . . However, Ipswich is
both hot and pious and the combination is
simply irresistible. —P.L.

A.S.R. writes from Melbourne :
Dear Bulletin, —A gruesome speculator rambles in
Bulletin re false teeth being bought and worked up
again by dentists. Well, some crazy men may do it, but
as the best artificial tooth (in any color, shape or size)
costs the dentist only about 6d. straight from the manu-
facturer’s porcelain furnace, why should “second-hand”
teeth be used? The odds are 10 to 1 also that the porce-
lain face cracks in attempting to remove a tooth from an
old plate. It’s not the teeth, but the gold in the plate,
that the pawnbroker is after.
This is no doubt quite true. Gold from false
teeth taken from the mouths of the dead is worked
up into wedding-rings, and new tooth - plates.
This is an unsentimental and utilitarian age.

The sublime intelligence of the prison
authorities. Two letters were lately returned
from a N.S.W. gaol endorsed by the prison
officials : “ Diseased ” and “ Gone to liberty ”
respectively.

In the Adelaide hospital tender-list this
year the line relating to the “ burial of the desti-
tute ” was put after that referring to the disposal
of “ kitchen refuse.” The old story: “Rattlehis
bones.”
N.S.W. country journalism gains, and
Sydney D.T. loses appreciably, through the
secession of the vigorous and resourceful night
sub-editor Westlake, and G. W. Todd, one of
the most efficient of Australian reporters, from
the staff of the city daily. The pair leave to
become proprietors of the long-established Wagga
Express, and hope to more than renew its pris-
tine vigor, in which venture they have the cordial
sympathy of their many press friends.
A few days back a long, lean vagrant ap-
peared before Melbourne autocrat Pan ton, P.M.
His jaws were moving with the deliberate grind
of a ruminating cow. “ What is this person eat-
ing?” thundered the modern Jove. “’Backer,
yer worship ! ” replied the deadbeat. “ Swallow it
at once,” roared the P.M. The ’beat’s jaw dropped
and he glared. “Swallow it! ” the P.M. roared
again. The prisoner gulped and the quid fell into
the inner darkness. And now-—under what pos-
sible law does Panton sentence unconvicted and
untried prisoners to the poisonous and horribly
indigestible punishment of swallowing tobacco ?

In a recent Ascot Vale (Vic.) mainte-
nance-case, the plaintiff (aged 19) alleged that
defendant, a police constable, went out for a walk
with her, then handcuffed her, and “ took ad-
vantage of her.” The Court decided in favor of
the girl, so presumably it believed this story, or
part of it. Of course, a man might handcuff his
best girl, when he went out walking with her,
from the most honorable motives - if she always
clawed him when he tried to make a formal de-
claration of love, for instance, it might be neces-
sary to thus secure her before stating his tale of
love_ at full length. Still, it seems a painfully
official proceeding, even apart from the allegation
that the policeman seduced her in that tied-up
condition.
Mr. C. R. Wilton writes from Adelaide :
Dear Bulletin, — In issue of Jan. 1 you write re the sug-
gestion that Dr. Cockburn may be chosen to succeed Mr
Playford as S.A. Agent General : “ His departure from
S.A., if it really happens, will be another result of the
Adelaide Hospital row, which has been the cause of nearly
everything that has happened in S.A. for two years past.
As a member of the Kingston Government, Cockburn is
practically ostracised by the profession which takes off
your leg for a consideration, and, as a consequence, his
practice is, probably, hardly worth keeping now.” This
is incorrect. Dr. Cockburn, who is an M.D. (gold-medal-
list) of London University, relinquished a most remunera-
tive practice shortly after his first election to Parliament
in ’B4 and for more than ten years he has not accepted a
fee for seeing a patient. He has never attempted even to
establish a consulting practice. On the other hand, Dr.
Leith Napier, Dr. Rogers, Dr. Hynes and some others’who
have remained true to the Govt, in the Hospital row
enjoy very large practices, while the juniors who braved
the British Medical Association have certainly gained
rather than lost popularity and patients. If Dr. Cock-
burn does go to England the Adelaide Hospital will have
nothing to do with the case one way or the other. Unless,
indeed, the influence of the Hospital dispute in solidifying
the Kingston Govt, support may have kept if, in office
long enough to appoint two Agent.s-General in succession.
A NEW YEAR’S EVE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The year is going;
You and I,
With Love once glowing,
Say Good-bye.
Once fired with passion,
Heart in heart—
We’re in the fashion—
Now we part ;
Still this “ old Adam ” cannot grieve,
He’ll find ere long a New Year’s Eve !
ADONIS.

Storekeepers and hotel proprietors in
country towns of N.S.W. that can be reached by
rail will have an. opportunity of seeing the
National Cash Register Till. Mr. W. A. Ling-
ham, the general manager of the co. in Australasia,
has just despatched a representative with a set of
sample machines. The traveller is now at Goul-
burn, and he will go right through the province.
Country business people who would like to inspect
this marvellous “money saver” should send a
post-card to the 00. at 393 George-st., Sydney, and
the traveller will not fail to oall.*
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, January 11, 1898.
Mr Dear Myfe—
The color-line is a flexible boundary in England.
Lady Anne Coventry (sister of Australia’s Lord
Deerhurst), one of the most aristocratic of the
aristocrats, is announced to be about to marry the
Maharajah Dhuleep Singh. What would the
Four Hundred of New York think if one of its
beauties were to contract an alliance with Pete
Johnsing—or, to bring it near home, how would
the historic families of Mossvale or the Western
Districts take to King Billy as a son-in-law?
One of the scientists who have come to Sydney
for the scientific corroboree, now going on at the
’Varsity, is Prof. Kernot, presiding deity of
cycling in Melb. He shed the radiance of cul-
tured patronage on the wheel at the time when it
was considered undignified for fogies in the
learned professions to mount a jigger. The
“Prof.” takes an expert’s delight in the scientific
principles of ’cycle-building, and sticks to the
original and correct pronunciation of the word-
talks of “bi-cycles,” not a “6f-cycle.” Theoreti-
cally, everybody endorses the first pronunciation,
and practically, everybody uses the second.
The appalling lengthiness of the titles of papers
read at the scientific camp-meeting now in full
swing doesn’t seem to frighten ambitious women
who wish to be known as cultured ; they turn up
all the same at the lectures with their little blue
leather membership tickets pinned on their chests.
At Mrs. Russell’s afternoon tea-drinking, at the
Observatory grounds last Friday, most of the
visitors appeared with cool, easy clothes and the
unworldly, dreamy eye of the man-with-a-hobby.
The company roamed over the buildings and
peered curiously at a big telescope and other
mysteries. The Victorian weather - fictionist
seemed interested in the room where the meteoro-
logical photos, are kept and our local weather
manufactured, but Wragge and all his storms were
conspicuously absent from the rival weather-man’s
tea-party.
At the circus there’s a good cartoon suggestion
going begging in the attitude of the elephant seen
sideways as he sits at table, which is distinctly
like the contour of Premier Reid as he sits in the
’Ouse. One night last week the trick pony, which
is guided by the fairy in hand-painted satin skirts,
did its act of letting off pistols and unfurling flags
with a slight variation—the pistol which releases
the Australian flag didn’t pop, probably from a
desire to economise powder, in view of the D.T.’s
scare about the lack of ammunition in N.S.W.
The crimson-habited girl is a horsewoman who is
good to look at, and is guyed by an inflated clown
with a pneumatic tyre on his chest. If he were
jabbed suddenly in the waistcoat with a pin, his
humor would expire through puncture with a loud
hiss.
Some of the pictures rejected by the judges for
the Walker Exhibition of Australian works of art
are gladsome bits of color, and original enough to
make a local show of “ Masterpieces that haven’t
gone to England,” an event that would brighten
up this dull mouth. An appreciative public would
gladly push its silver coin through the pay-hole to
see that large yellow “ sunrise ” that went the
rounds of all last year’s shows, and was unani-
mously crowded into oblivion by all the hanging-
committees. Competitors, in a few cases, sent in
explanatory notes with their canvasses. One con-
scientious worker staked his hopes on an idyll of
bush life —a damsel plucking wild flowers in a
shady nook near a creek, his explanation being,
“ The lady is supposed to be one of a picnic-party,
and has strayed from her friends gathering flowers ;
you cannot see the rest of the picnickers, as they
are all on the other side of the creek.”
The Ladies’ Club entertained the scientists in
an original manner, on Monday night, by giving
an “at home” on the Phillip-street tennis-court,
next door to the club-house, and beginning the
revel at the latesome hour of 9.30. The guests
entered through the advt. hoarding fencing off the
ground, and crowds of well-dressed people insert-
ing themselves into the party at a late hour
through a glaring notice of Somebody’s Gin had
an odd effect. The place was festooned with few-
and-far-betVveen paper-lanterns, some tables and
chairs were set about, and a string band lurked
somewhere in the gloom and buzzed like an en-
raged mosquito : but the shadowy forms prowling
about in the dark eyed each other nervously and
furtively. The entertainment didn’t hum much ;
the shivoo was late, the moon was later ; but the
scientists were the latest of all. Somewhere
towards midnight a search expedition with dark
lanterns was proposed, but a contingent of morn-
ing-dressed men and high-necked women were
dimly seen to be the expected visitors. Some of
the women were in full evening rig, and others in
a prudent compromise, but all cats are grey in the
dark, and all looked equally shadowy. The men
were mostly hatless and seemed to be dismally
conscious of coming colds in the head in conse-
quence. One man with a boxer was excitedly
trying to conceal it all the time lest it should re-
veal the damaging fact that it wasn’t a crush-hat.
The party drifted away in distressful sections
shortly before the cold gloom of midnight settled
down over the asphalt-court once more.
, The wedding of Miss Thorne, of Potts Point,
affd Mr. J. Brunton, at St. John’s, Darlinghurst,
last Wednesday, united two well-gilt families of
Sydney and Melbourne. The bridegroom is eldest
son of Hon. T Brunton, of Vic. Leg. Council.
The weather did its best to wreck all the “ pretti-
ness ” of the ceremony, as the bride had to struggle
into church against a strong head wind. The
white satin bodice was a study in tuckery, the
lines of this fashionable mode of trimming all
running diagonally across the back ; she changed
her dress for a travelling-gown in blue and snuff-
color with leather fixings. The same high wind
thinned the ranks of farewellihg guests at the
final shivoo on the departing flagship the same
afternoon. The trip over the bobbing waves gave
a malaisey feeling to many of the guests which
waltzing on board the Orlando didn’t help to
smother. As souvenirs of the time-expired flag-
ship the officers handed round little red-white-and-
blue coekades. The “crimson thread of kinship ”
has been strengthened several times during the
Orlando’s stay in Australian waters. The retir-
ing Admiral’s secretary was married (when in
England) to a Melbourne girl, daughter of Widow
Menzies, of Menzies’ Hotel. A Hobart lady,
Miss Mac Hardy, a universal social favorite, was
the latest annexation : she married Lieut. Grea-
torex, and goes per mail-steamer to rejoin her
husband at the voyage end.
The Premier’s progress in the Mudgee district
last week was shared by Mrs. Reid, and at
different towns on the tour prominent citizenesses
lined up to greet the lady with the orthodox hand-
fuls of flowers. Part of the trip per buggy was
glorified by a mounted escort, who sat about with
drawn swords. Mrs. Reid’s jaunty and beflowered
hat was exchanged for a comfortable sun-bonnet
en route, and at the first sight of the cotton head-
gear the escort sheathed its weapons as though all
thesmartnessof the expedition were quenched. The
female deputationists waiting at Gulgong craned
their necks expectantly for the Premieress, quite
unconscious that the lady under the large bonnet
was the expected distinguished visitor. But after
a while Mrs. R. appeared topped off once more by
her smart millinery and best veil, and the whole
party pulled down its vest and felt that it had
some style imparted to it.
His Ex. of Victoria and Lady Brassey are tour-
ing in this province. The family yacht is still
moored in Farm Cove out of commission, but the
owners paid the craft a visit before leaving for the
Blue Mountains, which they are beholding “ on
their own,” as the suite is enjoying himself in Syd-
ney. The suite had his day out at Canterbury
Park and the theatre at night, thereby causing a
little flutter among the chorus-girls, who are all
interested in the above suite.
A daily paper lately wanted an interview with
the three lady-doctors about whose non-appoint-
ment to the Sydney Hospital there has been so
much discussion in the papers. The tactful chief-
of-staff wisely picked out for the job a young and
nice-looking reporter noted for his gleaming collars
and cuffs, and by arrangement the scribe met the
three at the house of another lady doc. He was
shown upstairs into a pretty little sitting-room,
and the four medicals all sat with their backs to
the light, gave the pressman all the information
he wanted, and fed him with afternoon-tea and
fresh fruit-salad, “ which I made myself,” said the
hostess artfully. So the interview was entirely
favorable to the girls’ professional aspirations.
The “ Babes in the Wood ” continued their
plaint about “ their mother in Heaven ” to a
crowded and grown-up audience on Saturday
night. That wearisome, infantile wailing about
the Babes’ dead parent is making even the stage
props feel tired, as the cot in the bedroom-scene
promptly shut up with a babe inside it. J. J.
Burke’s new song caused one vast blush to spread
itself over the d. c., which was mostly occupied
by country visitors. The high-toned Saturday-
nigh ters are all “out of town,” or pretend they
are, although the family funds may not permit of
a more expensive jaunt than a trip to Botany and
back per tram. A box was adorned by the Vic-
torian Aide, Shaftesbury, who is soon to take his
departure for ’Ome. He will long be remembered
here for his enthusiastic stage friendships and will
be missed as Australia’s only specimen of the
stage-struck British nobleman.
Woman is not getting her fair share of the con-
solations of religion at St. James’s Church, where
a strong ritualistic feeling has set in and the parish
is worked by celibate curates. Lady members of
the congregation feel hurt at being ignored by
these St. Anthonies in all the attractive side-
shows, which are now rigorously kept for men
only. Smoke-concerts and walking-tours have
been going on for some time, and all the share the
snubbed sisterhood have in them is to look en-
viously on at their brethren having a good time.
The latest effort to keep alive the spiritual flame
was a grand Xmas dinner of roast beef and plum-
pudding for over two dozen men. But the church-
wardens don’t fear a female exodus, for they
know that if the church is popular with men the
women are bound to follow.
The religious and devotional variety of drawing-
room ballad, which owes its rise to the popularity
gained for itself by “The Holy City,” is very
much on the increase. No one is surprised now-
adays when the evening-party tenor uprises and
pours forth a full confession of his most sacred
beliefs in song. “The Vision Divine,” by Joseph
Adams (Ricordi and Co.), is the latest composition
of this type, and it is of so emphatically sacred a
nature that the words seem to have strayed
aimlessly out of some hymnal. The music is al-
most as holy as the words. The same publishers
send out a new waltz, “ The Bohemian,” by
Bucalossi, from Puccini’s up-to-date opera. The
cover is embellished with a most fascinating
maiden, early Victorian as to garb but modern
French as to expression. If the waltz weren’t
half as good as it is, the cover-girl would make up
the money’s value.
Paling’s Piano Album is a lavish shilling’s-worth.
Among the new pieces which it contains are one
or two musical chestnuts which a much-vexed
public would gratefully consign to oblivion. The
once “ Merry Peasant ” has lost much of her
mirth since he cast in his lot with the “easy”
classics in the piano instruction-books, and
Weber’s Last Waltz suggests nothing now but
early morning “practice.” Emily D. Paterson
has prettily set the words of “The Banjo’s”
poem, “ Black Swans,” to music (Paling and Co.).
The accompaniment runs in dreamily rippling
arpeggios which suit the lines most effectively. A
serenade by “ Hinemoa ” Hill is bound to become
popular. It bears the somewhat confusing com-
mand to “ Come beloved one to wake not, but
hear me ” by way of title. Signor de Beaupuis
has sent out to the musical public a snappy little
Prdlude for a Xmas composition.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ The adoration of the celestial Prince Ranjit-
sinhji broke out at the conclusion of the inter-
national match when the dusky potentate was
mobbed at the gates of the M.C.C. Ground
by a fatuous‘push,’ mainly females. Here and
there a red, hysterical person in skirts hurled
garden stuff at the gritted Indian, and all made
audible little comments about the ‘darlingprince,’
and his ‘expressive eyes, dear,’ and fought, and
gasped, and giggled about God’s image in coffee
until the princelet shrieked for a cab, or a balloon,
or something, and was borne away, trembling,
whilst his admirers reached for scraps of his ward-
robte, and got off with a shred or a button
or two. Ranji is having a patent boiler-iron
suit fitted up in order to obviate the possibility of
his having to race to his hotel one of these days in
a tattered umbrella and a pair of braces. Heaven,
or the Firm, send us a beauty actor at once to
divert female Melbourne from this chocolate hero!
“Lord William Nevill, who has been achieving
such evil distinction in the English law-courts, is
the same pallid young man who toured Australia
when this stricken country was infested with Deer-
hurst and many other titled personages. Nevill
at that time created big interest by publicly “going
over to Rome” (he was at. the time courting a
daughter of the then “ big ” banking house o
Murrietta). The ceremony took place at St
Ignatius Church, and the priest responsible for
the precious convert was the well-known Father
Kennedy. At the time there were great hopes of
Nevill joining the priesthood, but the unwhole-
some-looking young man seems to have got less
spiritual-minded since he left here, for he couldn’t
have been much of an anchorite on the .£15,000 he
beat the Jews for. There was great joy among
the R.C. ranks at the time of the conversion; now
it is the other camp’s turn to smile.
“Besides the particular transactions in which
he obtained the signatures of a confiding Life-
Guard juggins, through holes in the paper cover-
ing what Nevill alleged were documents concerning
a strictly private family matter, this ornament of
society appears for years to have been trading
upon the ill-placed confidence of young men of
means—his dealings in this way with the money-
lender who sued Nevill’s latest dupe having
amounted to £BO,OOO.
“ A handsome Melb. society-girl came out of a
shop to find an admiring young man standing on
the kerb beside her bike. She took painful care
to avoid catching the young fellow’s eye, but, as
she seized the machine by the handle, he raised his
hat and stepped forward, so that his presence
could no longer be ignored. Accordingly she
raised her eyes, gave him a killing glance, and
turned her back, wheeling the machine into the
middle of the road. But the apparent Don Juan
was not to be denied. * I beg your pardon,’ he
began, following the lady into the street, ‘ but I— ’
‘ Sir! ’ she cried, in a frenzied tone, ‘if you persist
in annoying me, I will give you in charge.’ ‘But— ’
E leaded the youth. But it was too late; to escape
im, the lady made a hurried jump into the saddle,
and the next moment was floundering in the road,
and giving the hated rou4 a bigger display of limb
and lingerie than he could have got in ten ballets.
As the blushing girl vainly endeavored to disen-
gage her dress from the angles of the machine the
young man came up and helped her to rise. ‘ This
wouldn’t have happened if you’d stopped to listen
to me,’ he said, sternly. ‘You know you can’t
ride a diamond-frame with skirts on. You see,
that’s my machine; yours is over there, on the
kerb.’
“ Rightly or not, one doesn’t associate fashion
and fine feathers with a district labelled the ‘ Isle
of Man,’ so the simple dressing of the ‘Manx-
man ’ characters comes as no surprise. All the
same, Maud Jeffries, as the Landlord’s lovely
daughter, apparently owns a plentiful supply of
fresh cotton frocks in pinks and blues ! In warm
weather what more can a girl want ? It’s not so
easy for poorish people to maintain high-water
dress level in winter, for furs and things costly
enough to look cosy come expensive. But most
summer sartorial sins can be laved away in the
laundry.
“ Up North, where washing wear is perennially
possible, women are fond of accusing impecunious
belles of possessing only one frock and ‘ doing it
up ’ nightly. Wretches, as if it wasn’t absolutely
heroic for a heat-worn girl to swelter over
nocturnal box-irons and boiled starch, so as to put
her best foot foremost by day.
“To go back to stage-land. When the cruel
Manx Fairy ‘ falls upon the thorns of life ’ she
keeps on curling her hair just as in brighter days,
and breasts the waters of tribulation in the bright
blue Eton suit and cream vest she happened to be
wearing when the deluge overtook her.
“On the boards, as a rule, she-victims smile
through their tears in tight-fitting black or grey
gowns and turned-down white linen collars and
cuffs. But in this work-a-day world, even when
the whole universe seems to be going smash, suf-
ferers like Maud Jeffries generally just go on wear-
ing what they have on hand, always supposing, of
course, there’s no funeral in the case.
“So far, Melbourne girls have obstinately
nourished regrets for a vanished stage idol, and
Claudian in all his glory could not blot out
memories of a shapely predecessor casting off a
misty blue mantle to stand revealed in glove-fit
white. But the ‘ Manxman ’ is going to change
all that. Already women are chortling over the
delightfulness of Wilson Barrett in Pete’s rough
blue woollens and unveiled braces as they
never did when he wore braces and had
fillets in his hair. Probably an instance
of ‘ beauty unadorned, adorned the most! ’
Yes, that frightfully ill-used person, Mr. Peter
Quilliam, is bound to be popular. Worst of it is,
that Barrett’s interpretation of his character, even
more than Hall Caine’s conception, makes one re-
gret more bitterly than ever that in the last scene
the much-tried hero desists from his axe policy,
instead of righteously making mince-meat of the
whole boiling, false friend, pitiless wife, snuffling
pa-in-law and babs included.
“ Gov. Brassey always makes a practice of read-
ing the lessons whenever he goes to church in the
country. Visitors to a fashionable mountain re-
sort had this treat the other Sunday before the
vice-regal clan left for Sydney, and, what with
the long drive to the tabernacle, and the heat and
flies, and his Excellency’s slumbrous style of elo-
cution, the congregation simply couldn’t keep
awake. The reader’s own belongings kept their
eyes open somehow. ‘Forewarned is forearmed,’
and, in preparation for what was coming, they
had probably steeled themselves to the ordeal by a
jorum of strong black coffee. Anyway, they sat
up like ramrods and smacked their lips over the
responses as if they liked it. Sister-worshippers
were glad of their company, for speculations re the
way the visitors did their hair helped to drive dull
sleep away. The coiffures were so high at the
back that hats could never have sat on the erec-
tion had not a sort of division-path been left for
the centre rear rim to fit snugly down into.
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.
