Suffield is still in his living grave, and the
N.S.W. Justice Department is jumping on him
with all its feet to make him fit that grave.
Suffield is a victim not only of Windeyerism, but
of Government by Contradiction. Both N.S.W.
political gangs, the ins and the outs, are interested
in justifying the actions of their respective Minis-
ters of Justice. A pamphlet fully dealing with
this monstrous wrong has just been published.
Post-free from The Bulletin office for 7d. in
stamps.
Blotting-paper towels are the latest English
novelty, and are made like a suit of pyjamas.
You climb into the paper suit, and in a second or
two are dry as a bone.
Dear Bulletin, — In your Xmas issue ap
pears a par. relating to my late brother, Fred.
Croft, which is only partly correct. He was of
English, not Irish, parentage. Also the line,
“ He loved wit and whisky better than work, and
so went under,” seems to convey an idea that his
end was in some way owing to drink, which it
certainly was not.—Yours, &c., Herbert Croft.
The one spark of humor during the recent
Melb. heat-wave was the spectacle of Mayor
M‘Eacharn sitting, throughout a “big” day at
the City Court, amid the heavy odor of vagrants,
and enveloped in his huge official ’possum-rug
robe. The heroism of a Highland piper or a
Bendigo miner is as nought compared with that
of the civic dignitary who must needs look
picturesque in a police-court.
Writes John Shaw, Parramatta :—
The Suffield case raises the question whether the
Goulds, the Slattervs, and the O’Connors can possess the
slightest conception of the true meaning of the terms
“ responsibility ” and “justice.” Each has confused
Justice with legal Formalism, and Ministerial Responsi-
bility with obsequious compliance with precedent-hedged
routine. “Minister of Justice” is a big phrase and
should mean much in the way of executing discrimination
and righteousness. A Minister of Justice, who, Gould-like,
shirks the real responsibilities of office, consenting to be
the tame follower of narrow precedents, the servile regis-
trar of departmental decrees based on traditionary syco-
phancy towards judicial procedure, is an official to be
publicly despised and abhorred. The wav in which a
Minister deals with a Suffield case proves whether he be
a statesman capable of precedent-creation in the spirit of
sagacious discernment, or just such a soulless, mechanical
organ-grinder as Gould by his acts has writ himself
down.
J. B. Castieau, in the essay on Australian
Literature which gained first prize in the Vic.
A.N.A. competition some months ago, took the
trouble to complain about a Westralian story pub-
lished in The Bulletin— and suggested that “ the
sickening performance ” of murdering a black-
gin, there described, gave an untrue impression of
certain Australian facts. That particular sketch
happened to be true to the life; but this paper
didn’t think it necessary to say so, because Cas-
tieau is a well-meaning young Australian who is
getting older and wiser every day—and Westra-
lians knew all about the truth of the picture, any-
way. It occurs to The Bulletin, however, to
recommend Mr. Castieau to read Chief Justice
Onslow’s remarks on the Westralian slave-system,
apropos of the Anderson murder case; and to
suggest that he should thank the writer whose
dramatic art succeeded in disgusting him, and
(but for Mr. Castieau’s passing youthful bumptious-
ness) might have succeeded in enlisting his aid to
wipe out a black stain on the Australian name
and nation.
“ Drover” to The Bulletin :—
If Sleath, M.P., would ask the N.S.W. Watering-Places
people a few questions about the leasing of bores and
tanks to stations on this road, he would add to his chances
of re-election. This Wanaaring-Milparinka road is one
of the worst in N.S.W. for feed and water at any time,
but by leasing Osaca bore to Urisini station, and Buri-
goolpa tank to Salisbury Downs, the Govt, helps to make
it a good deal worse. There is Clifton bore running to
waste, two stations—Salisbury and Yantara—getting the
benefit of the water for nothing. At the other two places
mentioned the stations are running thousands of sheep,
and the travelling public can only get a drink (of course,
by paying), and then have to go miles to look for a bit of
feed. I am told the squatters only pay a few pounds
annually for the use of them. [How’much do they pay?
—Ed. B.] Then again, from Osaca bore, we have to
travel stock nearly 30 miles to the next bore, Cullabulla,
unless we can beg a drink from Urisini; while at the
91-Mile, between the two places, there is a bore shut down
with a splendid pumping supply. The least the Govt,
should do at these places is to fence in the reserves, so
that the travelling public can feed a horse or two of a
night when they can get water. As it is, the country is
eaten out for miles around the bores. Lessees of bores
are only supposed to water so many head of large and
small stock —I think it is 30 head of large and 100 sheep.
[How many is it ?—Ed. B.]
A Bananaland political prophecy :—
At least one of the present Cabinet will have to white-
wash owing to Mt. Morgan boom and land blight. Thynne
will anon seek to win Tozer’s electorate when the latter
resigns to become Agent-General. Tomjoe Byrnes will
be Premier vice Nelson, retired to the Leg. Council,
Palmer getting a satisfactory solatium. Bumptious Pat
Leahy will figure as Minister for Lands, and, as a sop to
Cerberus, the billet of Minister for Mines will be offered
to (and probably grabbed by) Andy Dawson, the bright
particular star of the Labor-party. Dawson has more
genuine followers than Glassey, and his championship of
Byrnes last session was a coming-event sore of a proceed-
ing. Anyhow, Dawson does not hesitate to avow his
partiality for Tomjoe. Dawson can command at least
eight followers, and this would break the back of the
Labor-party and make Tomjoe “ good iron ” with the
ultra R.C. Labor crowd. Which may sound like moon-
shine, but there have been bigger somersaults up this way
ere now —witness the Griffilwraith coalition, the admis-
sion of Powers to the M'llwraith Ministry, and Donaldson
do. do. The Q. electors may have a very pretty problem
set them at next election. Perhaps, after all, Diokson
knew a bit when he came into the betting early.
Sands’ Sydney and Suburban Directory
for 1898 is just out, and is, as usual, a publi-
cation indispensable to any business man who
knows the value of the time he will otherwise take
in hunting up things. In addition to the names
and addresses and occupations of every resident in
Sydney and suburbs who doesn’t change his lodg-
ings every fortnight or so, the directory contains
an index to N.S.W. squattages, with holders’ and
station names —a very useful item—and a variety
of other useful information which only a system
born of 38 years experience is capable of amassing:

Personal Items.
Another king has gone under— poor old
Jerry, of Mannum, S.A. A Primitive Methodist
parson preached at his grave.
The weather has been hot in Tasmania,
but it will be cool now. Iceberg Gould, N.S.W.
Minister for Justice, is in Hobart.

“Rubbish, rubbish. Your speeches are all froth.”
Haynes : “ First time you ever objected to any-
thing with froth on it.”
By a strange coincidence the two ladies
most prominently mentioned in a recent “big”
divorce-suit are daughters of old-time Sydney
police-court solicitors, contemporaries.
Brisbane Worker finds remarkableness in
the fact that ex-Gov. Norman should resign the
Q. General-Agency just after the Q.N.B. sewage
had been stirred. The clean-souled old soldier
couldn’t stand the stink.
D. D. Gazette (Toowoomba, Q.) says that
Boss Tozer “has > tact which might well be the
envy of Beelzebuv. himself.” But the Devil is a
gentleman and no lawyer, or he wouldn’t have
lost his landed estate in heaven.
Of the four N.S.W. baronets, two (Cooper
and Samuel) are English - born, one (Nicholson)
Sootch, and one (Read) Irish. Nicholson has
started into his 90th birth-year ; but all appear to
have given the Psalmist the slip.
Some birthdays in January—Edmund
Barton, 49 on the 18th ; Jenkin Coles, Speaker of
B.A. Assembly, 56 ; and David Gaunson 51 on the
19th ; Henry Britton, of the Australasian, 51 on
the 24th ; Judge Holroyd, 70 ; and Stanbury, the
rower, 29 on the 25th.
Before prosecuting anyone criminally for
libel, always remember this : It takes 12 jurymen
to justify the prosecutor, whereas, no matter what
the merits of the case, one dissenting juryman can
justify the defendant in the eyes of the population.
Nothing fails like failure.

When Parkes visited the sporting district
of Coleraine (Yic.) a number of young men met
him with a drag and entertained him with
athletics. After viewing the sports the old man
■aid that the local youths might beat him at run-
ning and jumping, but in one line he claimed the
ohampionship. “At courting a pretty girl I can
lick the lot of you.”
The official heads of Sydney Roman Catho-
licism usher in 1898 as publicly-announced figure-
heads of newspaper and wall-poster advertisers’
tonics. Card. Moran stands forth as patron of a
particular wine, Monsignor O’Brien as the pan-
jandrum of a Choctaw - named indigestion cure,
and Dr. O’Haran as solemn voucher for a certain
brain restorative and nerve tonic.
Gov. M‘Gregor, whom they talk of send-
ing to West Africa, has made no secret of it for a
long time past that he expected an appointment
in civilisation after his ten years in New Guinea.
In fact, one of the Australias was almost promised
him ! What a life has been his —and what a liver
he must have ! Fiji, New Guinea—and the Gold
Coast ahead ! Scotchman—a man whom you
oan’t scotch.
Hon. Geo. Thorn, one time Q. Premier,
writes advertising testimonials for patent medi-
oines, in return for which favor the proprietors
describe George as “ex-Premier and veteran
parliamentarian.” Thorn’s career is further de-
scribed as “brilliant.” To anyone who remembers
the series of fiascos which resulted in the above-
mentioned Premiership, these little flights are
indeed humorous.
Dr. Walter Brown, who lately died at Par-
ramatta, was a steady smoker in his early days.
Nearly half a century ago, however, he visited his
brother, at the latter’s station in Queensland, and,
in pursuit of nicotine enjoyment, accidentally
dropped a lighted match in the tinder-dry grass of
the run. When the conflagration was suppressed,
or burnt itself out, the doctor vowed that he would
never smoke again, and he stuck to the resolution
till death.
The resignation of Dr. Norton Manning
as N.S.W. Inspector-General of the Insane may
be due, as stated, to ill-health ; but it is probable
that the very scurvy treatment extended to the
doctor by the authorities has also much to do
with it.
The Bulletin was in error in setting
down Capt. Samuel as heir to the newly-created
Samuel baronetcy. It is Mr. E. L. Samuel, well-
known in Sydney as a mining-operator, who is
Sir Saul s eldest son. Mr. Samuel is married to
a daughter of the gilded house of Cohen (Sydney
and Tamworth).

Delicious explanation from Sydney D.T. :
Hr. A. C. Shadier, who was a witness in the Norton
libel-case, was referred to by the presiding Judge as a
person unworthy of credit. Mr. Shadier complains that
this observation has been misunderstood by many per-
sons, who have assumed that it was an attack upon his
commercial credit. As a matter of fact, it alluded to the
credibility of his evidence only, and not in any way to his
commercial standing.

H. : “I note a reference to the testi-
monial heartily given by the employed at Book
Arcadian Cole’s, Melb., on the return of Mrs. and
Miss Cole from Europe. About 20 years ago
Cole advertised for a wife in a Melb. daily,
devoting a column to a full description of the sort
of lady required. This was generally taken as an
advertising joke, but Miss Jordan, of St. Kilda,
answered the advt., and Cole married her. Also,
they lived happily ever after.”

trial, applied to the Judge for police assistance to
help him trace the sender to him of a telegram in
French: “Salutation after the downfall; come
to our home in Hell.—W. Freeman Kitchen.”
(A pet aversion of Norton’s who lately suicided.)
The Bulletin fails to see why Norton should
object so vigorously to this cheerful offer of hospi-
tality. _ The theory (apart from the Calvinistic
belief in the damnation of unbaptised infants) is
that no one need go to Hell unless he chooses.
Of interest to a numerous Australian
family. Per last census the great clan Smith
numbered 410,000 in England and Wales —or one
out of every 79 of the whole population. Scotland
numbered 60,000 of the same illustrious family,
Ireland 30,000. Total for the United Kingdom
half-a-million—outnumbering army and navy by
two to one. There are 19,300 widow Smiths ; the
Miss Smiths tot up to 30,000, and the married
Smiths —but stay . . . About 26 Smiths die every
day, and 42 are born every 24 hours, so that the
whole habitable earth will soou be the domicile of
this ravenous brood.
A-top of all N.S.W. Opposition-leader
Dyne's political trouble comes this printed testi-
monial to him from a Sydney pen-lady :
He spends in his home the time some other men in pub-
lic positions are said to occupy in running after strange
goddesses, and he has nothing to fear from anything that
may jump to light.
This emanates from the guileless scribess who
plaintively remarks, a few inches further up :
There comes a time in the life of every girl when she is
tired of being alone, and she feels she wants a husband to
love and a home of her own. No girl can deny that. The
fever commences about the age of 24 and lasts till 30,
when despair enters the heart and changes the nature. I
don't know whether the same longing, or sense of loneli-
ness comes over a man ; probably it does, but disappoint-
ment, in his case, does not bear the same fruit.
A M‘Gooey story about Parkes :
The late Henry Parkes, in his young days, could ride
a bit, and, while engaged post-and-rail fencing, in the
Penrith region, was given to borrowing horses. My
uncle once lent him an old fire-eater to ride out for
good splitting timber in the Regentville district. In
those days, bullock-bells were as much valued, and as
much spoken of and worshipped, as goats are at the
present day on worked-out diggings—and that’s saying
a lot. Parkes, in his hunt for splitting material, found
a bullock-bell, and tied it round the old horse’s neck.
Then he pointed the head towards home, and mounted.
Ding! dong! . . . The horse did the 14 miles home in
some few minutes. Parkes limped in, on the evening
of the third day, minus hat and left boot, which he
had left in the stirrup, and plus a sprained ankle and
skinned hip. The horse brought home the bullock-bell
only. My uncle sold the bell for 17s. 6d., and drank
the money, and Parkes had the price of the saddle and
bridle stopped out of his fencing-pay.
Amos Nicholson, of the West Coast (M.L.),
suicided in a business-like manner :
“ Reefton, Dec. 13, 1897.
“ To the Coroner.
“ Dear Sir,—l will be at a great disadvantage when we
meet to be introduced, as I will not be able to return the
hackneyed phrase of ‘ How happy,’ &c. Still, to save
trouble, I will give you and jury particulars that may
help to shorten proceedings :
“ Name—Amos Nicholson, carpenter, late of Reefton.
“ Age—Fifty-nine on Feb. 27, *9B.
“ Religion—Church of England.
“ Married —Yes.
“ Cause—Want of rest.
“ Revolver was bought at Bob Forsyth’s about five this
evening.
“ Height—6ft. 9in. Kindly check this, as I like to be
aocurate.
“ I cannot think of anything else you require to know,
so I have the honor to be yours truly,
“Amos Nicholson.”
Verdict —“Temporary Insanity!”
Cabled that Judge Boucaut (S.A.) has
been knighted. Boucaut was in his time a “ big ”
S.A. politician, and distinguished as much for his
ability as for his vitriolic temper. As a Judge, he
is not so popular with the lawyers as Way and
Bundey. A smart Adelaide practitioner once
remarked that Way breeds sheep, Boucaut
thoroughbred Arabs, and Bundey dispenses jus-
tice. Boucaut’s great hobby, besides his horse-
farm, is collecting first editions.

A retirement from N.S. W. Supreme Court
Bench is expected within the next three months,
thus giving Premier Reid one more opportunity
for the exercise of patronage. There is but one
Supreme Court ex-Judge on pension at present—a
fact which makes an addition to the list seem more
tolerable than it would have been some years ago.
W. K. Simms, the recently deceased S.A.
sporting - brewer - Brokenhillionaire, left behind
him about half-a-million. He made his first rise
out of a big brewery float, and then got into
Broken Hills at the start. Simms was an excel-
lent judge of a racehorse, although he never
raced, and made a pot over Carbine’s Melb. Cup.

—the thermometer ran up to 110'8, having on the

The woman concerned in the Hess-
Labouchere row is, it seems, Mrs. George Augus-
tus Sala No. 2, who sold to Hess a number of
letters long ago written by Labouchere to “her
beloved husband.” Hess incautiously paraded his
intention to publish these, whereupon Labouchere
promptly injunctioned him, it being clearly estab-
lished in law that the copyright of a letter vests
in the man who writes it, and that only the paper
on which it is written belongs to the recipient.

Baron von Mueller steered clear of small
details, such as worry ordinary folk. His thoughts
were always “in the skies.” The writer one day
met him riding a pony, without saddle, but bridled.
He led another animal (with a saddle on) by a
headstall. The unconventional character of this
arrangement was pointed out to him. He looked
around wistfully. “Aeh!” he said, “ der sattel
on der wrong horse ! It should be on der donkey,
mineselluf. ” He got off, and mounted the saddled
pony. But he never changed the headstall for the
bridle, but jogged off as anomalous as ever—hat,
like the Tower of Pisa, balanced at the back of his
honest head, and his pale blue eyes fixed on the
far-away horizon of abstract thought. There was
a lot of Don Quixote about the Baron.
Wahanui, who lately died in Maoriland,
was “ Premier ”to the late King Tawhiao. He
had a magnificent physique, and boasted a 56-inch
chest. Also, he was a famous trencherman. He
once told Edward Wakefield that he had eaten
half a sheep at a meal—no doubt a lie. Wahanui’s
brother, Kahu, was built on similar lines, and
weighed 18|st. The present writer met Kahu in
the Mokau country in ’B5 when travelling that
region under safe-conduct from Wetere Te Rerenga
—it was just after the amnesty granted by the
M.L. Govt, to all rebels against Kuine Wikatoria.
Wetere told off Kahu to obtain firing for the
writer’s party, and instead of passing the order on
to a person of meaner birth as would have hap-
pened in a European community, Kahu harnessed
himself to a bullock sled, and inside an hour
appeared at the camp sweating in the trace
and with six or seven cwt. of timber in tow.
A monetary recompense for Kahu’s exertion was
offered, but the giant replied : “ Kahore ! (No).
You teach ee me sing ‘Annie Laurie.’ That the
utu, by Ka’ris’! ”
AN.Q. policeman was asked: “How do
you like Parry - Okeden as Commissioner of
Police?” “Ugh, the divil take Parry-Okedin.”
“ Why ? what have you got against him ? ” “Be
jabers ! it wor Parry Okedin what foined poor,
dacint Consthable Y foive poundh for bein’ under
th’ inflooence.” “ I suppose he was very drunk ? ”
“Drunk ? Be L, he wasn’t dhrunk at all, he wor
marely under th’ inflooence. I putt it to you,
now, as a fair man, if a consthable was foined
foive pound ivery toime that he got under th’
inflooence, wid all his chances of gettin’ under th’
inflooence, he’d soon be a rooind man ! ”
On an Island blackbirder was a pet billy-
goat, and the other day, when the skipper had sur-
rounded more nigger-rum than he could carry, and
was lying helpless on deck, billy got on to “the
old man’s ’’ whiskers and chewed them clean off
on the port side. There was a first-class circus
when the skipper woke-up; but, after libating
copiously to the billy’s destruction, he got re-
pnmed and collapsed onoe more ; whereupon the
goat came along again and chewed off the star-
board whiskers. A week later the goat was killed,
and a day after that four of the crew were hove
overboard, amidst the mutterings of the rest about
their narrow escape.

Aboriginalities.
S.S. : “Be strange deaths—here is a Car-
digan (Ballarat) story: A man had undressed to
go in for a swim in one of the quarry-holes in a
paddock. Whilst standing on the high bank,
preparing for the first dive, he was butted by a
steer, and driven into the water. The shock dis-
located his spine. Black snakes were not uncom-
mon in the waters of those quarries on hot days,
apd we boys who went swimming there were
always careful to watch for a time before going
in. The man of my yarn was bitten by a snake
whilst in the water. He was also drowned. The
M.D. at the inquest said death would have
resulted from the spinal injury, and probably
from the bite of the snake, but it was really a case
of drowning. Verdict accordingly.”
* * * *
Bear Bulletin, — Another snake yarn,
cfltto a tight place. Jack Barber lived in the
usual stringy-bark hut with the usual slanting
E®@f. When he lay on his bunk his face was
about 2ft. below the lowest part of the roof.
One warm Sunday morning he woke rather
late with an idea that a cold hand was being
passed over his cheek. When he opened his eyes
he encountered a dark, indistinct object sus-
pended directly over his face. A second glance
convinced him it was a snake, a large snake, two-
thirds of the body of which hung from the divi-
sion between two sheets of bark on the roof. The
body came to within two inches of his lips, turned
upwards again, and the lean, deadly head, from
which the tongue darted, oscillated with a rhyth-
mical circular motion. Barber lay for a moment,
frozen in flesh and blood, and simply glared, ex-
pecting death, but the snake’s diamond eyes were
fixed upon the wall about six inches above his
head, and it continued its strange, methodical
movement. Then the power of action returned to
Barber’s limbs, and he simply flung himself from
the bunk and jumped for the iron fire-bar. When
he turned around the snake had not altered its
position, and still its head went slowly round and
round. The navvy’s watch hung open on a tack
in the wall, a ray of sunlight fell across the glass,
and it was about this shrine that the snake was
performing its quaint devotions. The snake con-
tinued its actions even when Barber approached
it, when a blow from the bar sent it squirming to
the floor with a broken back. Jack was sure the
brute had just looked in to see the time of day,
but I have since seen a snake performing some-
what similarly in the presence of a white glass
Miss MAUD JEFFRIES (leading lady in the Wilson
Barrett Co.).
Photo., Talma.
bottle standing in the sun.—Silas Snell.

Dear Bulletin, — To procure employment
in the bush difficult ?No name for it! A swaggie
lately asked a Riverina cockey for a job at har-
vesting. “ Pull up your pants ! ” sternly de-
manded the man of wheat, caterpillars and rust.
Swaggie, who wanted work badly, did. “H’m!
you’re no uster me. Waste too much time lacing
your boots. Why don’t yer wear ’lastio-sides ?
Besides, yer don’t wear socks. Men who don’t
wear socks tear up everything about the place for
‘ Royal Alberts ’ (foot-rags). A Cockney cx.ap I
had here onst used up a brand-new tarpaulin that
way. ” —Fact.
* * * •*
The dignity of the average shire council is
a fearful and wonderful thing. Two way-back
Vic. councils have recently been standing on their
dignity towards each other with respect to the
matter of a dead horse. It appears that the horse,
a disused and disowned neddy, wantonly died on
the ploughed line dividing the two shires, and
right in the track of worshippers attending the
little bethel that holds a monopoly in saving grace
thereabouts. Then the question of removing and
disposing of the offensive equine remains began to
agitate local politics, and each council resolved
that it was the other’s bounden duty to provide
decent sepulture for the dead horse, and a policy
of masterly inactivity was adopted on both sides,
until the stench from the remains awoke a distinct
echo in the hills, and the good people of Bingey
Bingey and Yalladorg were compelled to walk a
mile round when they wished to get religion. At
this stage a diplomatist suggested that it would
be wise to cross-cut the animal directly over the
border, and leave each shire to bury its own half.
But, unhappily, no man could be discovered who
would be hired to venture within a hundred yards
of the pestilent quadruped, and the dignity of the
councils hardened into bitter antagonism. One
morning it was found that the public nuisance
had been hauled bodily into Yalladorg territory,
but during the following night some person or
persons unknown went with chains and tackle and
a draught-horse, and towed the decayed animal
back into the shire of Bingey Bingey. This ex-
change of compliments continued for nearly a
week, when two towing parties met at midnight,
and a riot was fought out in the solitudes. Next
morning five councillors were found to be more or
less damaged and incapable, and the defunct horse
was scattered in malodorous fragments pretty well
all over both shires, just as if the opposing
factions had fought bitterly over the horse, and
then bombarded each other with junks of the
malignant remains. After this neither shire
council found it incompatible with its dignity to
gather up all the scraps within its jurisdiction and
burn them, and each subsequently indulged in a
lavish banquet in celebration of its triumph, but
Yalladorg and Bingey Bingey are still on their
dignity, and all diplomatic relations remain off.
For the next Australian diction-
ary “faker ” :
Swagsman : General term for person with
“ swag.”
Traveller : Term used by better class Aus-
tralians for genuine seeker after work and
wages. Mostly met on the long, bad stages
W. and N.W. Always clean, walks upright,
and travels 20 to 40 miles a day. Mostly
strange to track.
Swaggy or Swagger : Abbreviation of
“swaysman,” used by children and larrikins
of all classes.
Sundowner : Professional loafer with swag.
Carries three sets of ration-bags, and sticks
to short stages mostly in sheep country.
Etymology obvious. Mostly well-patched and
dirty ; knows the ropes. Travels five to
seven miles per day, and knows where he’s
going to camp.
Tramp: Tendegre.es below “sundowner,”
the height attained by broken clerks, coun-
ter-jumpers, waiters, civil-servants and larri-
kins. Keeps to towns and thickly-populated
districts, such as coast roads and railways.
Eloquent; wears dilapidated store-clothes,
and burns down shelters when leaving.
Smokes cigarettes. Travels one mile a week.
Lives well.
Whaler : Riverina sundowner (supposed to
have a lien on the local squatters and
farmers).
Hatter : One who travels alone and camps
alone. Either “ratty,” or has money, or
does it to avoid bad society, which is about
the only society going in the bush.
Squatter: Large landholder. (Combines
the offices of Providence and natural enemy
to swagsmen). National scapegoat.
Farmer: Expert agriculturist (mostly a
foreigner).
Cocky: Selector who plants things, which
sometimes grow. ( May develop into farmer,
but not likely.)
Selector: Ignoramus who takes up land
and clears it for a mortgagee. (Mostly a
“ broker.”)
Pumpkin-squatter: A little cocky with a
swelled-head.
Planter (pron. “ Plantoh ”): A five-acre
cane-grower, with two niggers, a buggy, and
a large opinion of himself. Sometimes' nomi-
nal shareholder in a central-mill, but in-
variably mortgaged up to the eyes.
Jackeroo or Silvertail: A “ haw-haw” per-
son pitchforked into an officer’s billet. Occa-
sionally turns out a white man, but not often.
Manager: Head jackeroo. (Overseer : Ac-
tual manager, with warrant-officer’s rating.
Western rivers : Sand or gravel indenta-
tions fringed with stunted timber. Mostly
dry.
Western trees: Shadeless, shelterless libels,
used as look-out stations for crows.
Western crows : Supreme owners and
pirates of the backblox. (Supposed to be
reincarnated swagsmen andsquatters.)
Blight flies : Supposed to have (migrated
from Egypt. Very intelligent and persevering. Always
attack the eyes.
Sandy blight .- Blindness caused by unrelieved gazing at
western “ grass.”
Barcoo rot: Sort of scurvy and blood-poison, combined
with every other disease.
Lizards : Northerners. Too dry to sweat. Skin rustles
like a lizard’s. “ They don’t die —they dry up and get
blown away.”
THE INEVITABLE COMPROMISE.
CUSTOMER (in Sydney restaurant , to waiter): “I’ll have pigeon-pic.”
WAITER: “ Pigeon-pie off.”
C.: “ All right ; I’ll have liver and bacon.”
W.: “Liver and bacon off.”
C.: “ Well, I’ll try rump-steak and tomatoes.”
W.: “ Tomatoes is off. Try steak and onions.”
C.: “Naw! I’m off!”
Maoriland philosophers have been much
troubled about some “ancient rock paintings”
found in Weka Pass, at the back of Canterbury.
The learned men of the south had declared these
paintings to be some 500 years old, and alleged
that they showed the rude art of the ancient
Maori. Whereupon an early settler wrote to
Christchurch Press :
I know two of the party who, in the early days of the
West Coast diggings, executed these paintings. They
were travelling with sheep and cattle, and were rained up
at the Pass for some days, and amused themselves by
making the rough sketches which have since so deeply
interested the savants of Mac-band and of Europe.
About 18 years ago one of these gentleman told me all
about it, and explained the real significance of some of
the designs. The “ serpent” was simply a stockwhip, the
helmet was a pith-helmet hat worn by one of the party,
and the plume was a store of flax crackers, twisted by the
owner, and stuck into a hole in the hat for convenience.
The gentlemen I have mentioned still reside in Nelson,
and I shall be glad to give their address to anyone who
wishes to follow up the subject.

The humors of Mongrelia : A Rockhamp-
ton Chinaman (Ah Poo) sued a Cingalese (De
Silva) for eighteen pence, value of a water-melon
supplied. Summons cost Ah Poo five shillings
and he also hired a lawyer. Defendant paid six-
pence into Court, alleging this was full value.
The P.M. thought Is. 6d. a fair thing, and gave
verdict accordingly, with ss. costs. Worth noting,
by the way, that the Chow is usually honest in
business transactions, but the Cingalese is nearly
always a rogue.
* * * •
The buffalo-season on the Alligator River,
Northern Territory has been productive of ter-
rible slaughter. Cahill, champion shooter, heads
the list with 1601 buffalo. Tingha follows, his
seven rifles fetching down 1250 head. Then
comes Cooper with 1100 —a total of about 6000.
At this rate the buffalo will soon be a thing of the
past in the Par North.
* * * •
Blacks who have been reared with whites
have exalted ideas of their own importance. The
greatest insult you oan offer one is to call him a
blackfellow. He simply abhors that race, and
wouldn’t be seen talking to any of it for a trifle.
He’s a blanky white man, with no spots on him.
The writer travelled with one Once on the road
with cattle. The man in charge was a skinflint,
and his tucker was rough. Said my colored white
mate : “ He reckons this tucker good enough for
us. Why, it's only fit for blacks to eat.”

Dear B.,—Re your recent par. about a
ram’s attempt to break up a cart. My father was
considered one of the strongest men in our dis-
trict, but was gone in one leg from rheumatism.
One day the. old man and myself had been
mending a fence, using a 281 b. hammer to drive
large stakes. Crossing the paddocks home, a ram
charged us, and I bolted for the nearest fence,
while Dad, who couldn’t run, prepared for action.
Dropping on one knee, he swung the 281 b. hammer,
and brought it smash upon the ram’s forehead.
The hit was just enough to steady the brute, and,
backing for about 15yds., he came again, meeting
another blow from the hammer, wielded by 15|in.
biceps. That ram kept the game up for 15 suc-
cessive charges, and then he wasn’t tired. —J.P.D.
[Next, please ! —Ed. P>.]

Bill—or perhaps it was Jim—had worked
on Run-dry Creek station for over two years, and
saved up a tidy little sum. “ I think I’ll have a
spell, after shearing,” he said one day. “Atrip
to Sydney or Melbourne ? ” “No ; I know a nice
little waterhole fourteen mile this side of Hay.
It must be a spring. I’ll do a camp on it for a
month or two.”
Another beautiful
instance of climatic un-
certainties in the West.
A few weeks ago a
Winton carrier drove
his horses down to
drink at a waterhole in
the bed of a creek.
As there had been a
long dry spell, the
place was boggy and
seven of the team were
soon stuek as fast as
flies in a treacle-pot.
But it happened to be
raining just then a few
miles up the creek, and
down the waters came
a banker. Before the
unfortunate carrier
could do anything he
had lost three of his
team by drowning.
This was in the middle
of a drought.

The Suffield Case.
He’s true to God who’s true to man
Wherever wrong is done,
To the humblest and the weakest
’Neath the all-beholding sun,
That wrong is also done to us ;
And they are slaves most base
Whose love of right is for themselves,
And not for all their race.
JAMES RUSSELL LOWELL.
PRESS AND OTHER COMMENTS.
Balclutha (M.L.) Fees Press (Dec. 17,1897): “ It looks
as if a grave miscarriage of justice had taken place.”
Grey River (M.L.) Argus, -in leading article (Dec. 17):
“ We have not the slightest hesitation in saying that we
believe Suffield did not commit the offence for which he
is now undergoing imprisonment, and that the evidence
he brought forward, and the only satisfactory and con-
vincing evidence submitted to the court, did not receive
that weight and consideration that it was fully entitled
to. It would appear as if the jury acted on that old
thqory known commonly as ‘splitting the difference.’
Between their awe of a stern, positive, and implacable
Judge, and their natural sympathy with a fellow-citizen
in a position of great peril, they doubtless thought they
were doing the best thing in the circumstances by
acquitting him on the more serious charge and finding
him guilty on the minor offence.”
Otago (M.L.) Daily Times (Dec. 25): “ A strong case is
made out for a revision of the trial, for a suspicion is
created that the penal laws of New South Wales are some-
times savagely administered, as well as obstinately inter-
preted, by officials enveloped in red tape.”
tg-Writes Dr. Leighton Kesteven, formerly Q. Govt. Medi-
cal Officer, Brisbane: “Hyde Park Terrace, Sydney.
I have read the medical evidence in the Suffield case,
and have no hesitation in saying that there is nothing
whatever in the symptoms described te warrant the con-
clusion that a rape had been committed, or even attempted.
Without definite evidence of the presence of spermatozoa
in the blood-stains, or on the plaintiff’s clothes, it is im-
possible to consider the act proved ; and the symptoms
described by Drs. Harris and Stapleton could have resulted
from several quite ordinary causes. The fact of profuse
menstruation having come on points to the strong likeli-
hood of the injuries having been caused by the violent
shock a bull would inflict.”

FROM THE HEART.
THAT IS THE WAY MINISTERS SPEAK.
No class of people in the community have a
deeper interest in the welfare'of the people than
ministers of the gospel. They feel great interest
m the welfare of the members of their congrega-
tion, study their wants, and are consulted on all
subjects. Of late years, pastors are becoming
well versed in those things which contribute to
the physical as well as spiritual comfort of their
people. Many pastors are also physicians, and all
seek earnestly for those things which they believe
to be worthy and valuable.
The following statement, bearing upon this im-
portant point, is most valuable. Rev. Wm.
Riding, Zeehan, Tas., writes : u X have pleasure
m saying that I have derived much benefit from
the use of Warner’s Safe Cure.”
lhe advent of the first sail on the
“ umbrella ” principle to the waters of Syd-
ney Harbor, and probably to those of the
Southern Hemisphere, created much interest
among the many ferry passengers between
Circular Quay and Lavender Bay on Sunday
week. Aquatic men who are up to date in
their reading will know what a stir has been
created in England by the claims made for a
new invention in the shape of a sail on the
umbrella principle. It is alleged for this
sail that the boat to which it is attached will
not heel over, and that she will carry at
least twice Ihe area of canvas. It was with
a view of testing the theory advanced by
the English inventor of the umbreJL.-sail
that Mr. Charles L. Garland designed the sail
and fitted it out on the little craft shown in
the accompanying photograph. Mr. Garland
is satisfied that, the theory is correct, that
there is a big future for the umbrella-sail,
and that and lasting 1 changes are ap-
proaching in the matter of constructing, ri<>--
gmg and sailing all small craft. The Vofa
is the name of the little boat, upon which Mr.
.Gailand has fitted his newly-designed sail.
She is 14ft. long, 15in. deep,' and has 30in.
beam -a rather ticklish craft whereon to try
anew design of sail, where the principle
involved demands a movable mast How-
ever, as the wind was light, there was little
risk in experimenting, arid Mr. Garland’s son
a boy of 12 years, worked the mast from
port to starboard, as required, without diffi-
culty. The Vola was tried on all winds, and
worked to windward with ease, whilst she
shovved excellent form in all points with the
wi.nd abaft the beam. No sitting-out was re-
quired, and the sail possesses great lifting
power There are no sheets to the sail, and
the only attachment to the boat is the foot
of the spar, which is fixed to the keelson by
a swivel. The mast is about 12ft. long, and
the sail encircles the mast about Bft. up.
standing out on all sides at right angles
and is stayed both above and below by
are’brouX'^ 8 ’, 128 •"* nu ™ ber ’ Sixty-four of the lines
ui ii Kh *u a ,1 , 0lnt and are attached to a halliard
which passes through a block at the mast-head and the
remaining 64 secure the 16 radiating battens which
spread the sail.to a ring secured to the lower portion of
the mast. These are the lines that have to bear an V
strain to which the sail is exposed, and their united
strength is equal to,any demand that a strong southerly
may make upon them. Although Mr. Garland ’has
adopted the principle involved in the construction of the
English invention, the make of the Vola’s sail is entirely
different The English sail folds horizontal! , and'the
Vola s vertically, round the mast.
The great heart of Charleville (Q.) is rent
by the discovery that the hospital-box at the Ideal
station had been stolen. For the year ’96 that
article contributed to hospital funds the sum of
fourteenpence and it is supposed that the rumor
thereof got abroad and attracted some of the
skilled cracksmen from Bananaland’s metropolis.

POLITICAL
POINTS
On all the Australian State railways
theatrical companies are allowed to go first-class
on second-class tickets. What say the Clergy to
this?
S.A. Labor-party nominates Hutchison for
M‘Pherson’s East Adelaide seat, despite the fact
that Hutchison isn’t a lucky man, having already
fought and lost three elections.
The N.S.W. Opposition having no policy,
is having one manufactured for it by its enemies.
And, as its enemies own all the daily papers—
well, what more remains to be said ?
Obit. : Bundaberg Leader. Tory money
and influence have told, and Labor-lead er
Glassey’s chances have been further lessened—
perhaps—for re-election at Kanakatown.
Q. Labor-leader Glassey ; “It is a deplor-
able state of affairs that eleven men who represent
nobody ” —meaning a majority vote in the Council
of 11 to 7 —“ should dictate to seventy-two men
who represent every corner of, and every interest
in, the province.”
Q. Leg. Council absolutely yawns for
Hugh Nelson. Stubborn old Bullocky Palmer,
however, blocks the way and, apparently, a pen-
sion is the only thing to make him abandon the
Presidentship. And the leading metropolitan
papers are calling for the retirement of every
ex-Q.N.B. director from the precincts of the
Council, while the Courier significantly reminds
the President that his place most of this session
has been filled by a substitute. Sooner or later
the old linguist will have to pack.
Dear Bulletin, The
W.A. sand-groping Forrest
Govt, has a great penchant for
importing second-hand Eng-
lish clerks. It takes them as
job lots. A draft of telegraph-
operators lately arrived at
£l9O per annum, with right
of a pension after five years’
service; and very recently
there arrived a large package
of English railway clerks,
picked up. during the Jubilee
festivities. I suspect they all
have a tinge of blue hlood.
Minister Wittenoom shows the
same desire to pitch foreigners
into good, fat billets. As an
instance the magistrate in the
town which I have the wretch-
edness to inhabit is a fat
Dutchman —one of many suou.
—Sandy Blight.
One hon. member illumi-
nated the dying moments of
the Q. session. Felt-hatted
and waving a “brolly,” he
sat on a back bench (the
House was waiting a message
from the Council) yelling in-
termittently, “Ashurn !” The
message came. Premier Nel-
son moved the adjournment.
The hon. member arose, and
swept the atmosphere with his
umbrella —-“ Misher Annear! ”
Speaker Cowley : “ Ordah !
Will the hon. membah please
remembah that the Speakah
is in the chair, and not the
Chairman of Committees.”
The member was sorry :
“ Misher Cowlish, I ’polo-
shise ” The Speaker
wailed : “Ordah ! Ordah ! ”
The member lost the thread
of his ideas, but amicably
waved his gamp, with his hat
on the end of it. “ Orright,
plent’ time,” he said, and put
the hat bn his head, and
shook the umbrella at Provi-
dence generally, and started
walking in circles on the floor.
Then Speaker Cowley called
out “ Ordah ! ” some more,
and the hon. member ran out
with his umbrella and his hat
in front of him.
Cabled that Thomas M‘H-
wraith is shortly coming back
to Brisbane, presumably to
fight things out over the Q.N.
Bank. And Thomas, even in his old age, is
a mighty fighting man from his boots to the
crown of his hat, so the chances are that a
good many people who have been saying nasty
things about him will apologise considerably.
M‘ll wraith should be able to wreck a good many
characters if he tells all he knows about that sin-
ful bank.
At the bye-election for Burke (Q.), in
1890, Hoolan, Browne, and Sim contested the seat
and finished in order named. To-day the three
represent three contiguous Gulf electorates —
Burke, Croydon, and Carpentaria. All three are
democrats—and bachelors (so far as the parson
knows, anyway).
It is true, as contended by Haynes, M.P.,
that the Minister for Justice should be a layman.
A lawyer Justice-Minister having his own private
practice and legal social status to consider, must
defer to the Judges, law-officials, and legal pro-
fession, and simply cannot act impartially between
lawyerdom and the public.
A member of N.S.W. Public Works Com-
mittee, totting up his items of income for a month
thus jotted : “M.L.A., .£2s(and oing nothing);
P.W.C., £5O (with travelling exes.); journalism,
£10; commissions at 2J per cent., £31 —t >tal,
£ll6, or at the rate of £1392 annually.” And
journalism was the only hard work on the list.
Brisbane (Q.) Worker is wisely sounding
the tocsin of Leg. Council destruction, in readi-
ness for next election. Every time in Austra-
lia that a Council has been swamped with new
members the swampees have begun to love the
Council in a week. In a month they have sworn
that it is a sacred institution. “And ever shall
be, Amen ! ”
Note how Australia is absorbed in a boys’
play-game of cricket, while such a momentous
affair as the division of China is on the board. In
short, this continent is not a whit English, except
when something is to be got out of England. If
Queen Victoria attempts to lean on a cornstalk —
enlarging this to all Australia —it will pierce her
hand right through.
When the Maoriland Leg. Council was
“ reformed ” it was agreed that the o'd life-mem-
bers should simply be allowed to die out, but all
new appointments should be for seven years only.
Only three of the seven-year crowd of democrats
sent up by Ballance and Seddon voted against the
Old Age Pension Bill, but they were all members
of the first batch appointed (in 1892) under the
new system. The longer a democrat holds office
as a nominee legislator, who is not responsible to
the people, the worse, as a rule, he gets; there-
fore the 1892 nominees are naturally the most
gamey of the new crowd.
TENTATIVE POLITICS.
“Did you hear that Reid is contemplating a bachelor tax?”
“ Yes; but if Lyne had nous enough to propose giving a bonus with
wives he’d beat him hollow!”
Adrian Knox, the only member of N.S.W.
Leg. Assembly who, at the opening of Parlia-
ment, directed in writing that his £25 per month
should be paid back into the Treasury, was pre-
sent at just 13 divisions and counts-out during
last session. Mr. Knox, who conscientiously
objects to payment of members, seems to as
strongly object to doing members’ work.
The veteran Douglas,
Govt. Resident of Thursday
Island, has been too plain-
spoken for the Boodlewraith
re the Japanese invasion of
the North, and the ruling
gang would gladly shunt him
to Port Moresby into Governor
M'Gregor’s billet. This may
account for Douglas’ recent
visit to Brisbane.
“In view of the fact
that 10 per cent, of the
Hawaiian race are affected
with leprosy, it becomes a
serious question as to what will
be the effect of the absorption
of this tainted population
upon the health of this (U. S. A.)
country,” says skin - expert
Prof. A. Morrow in the
North American Review.
He concludes that leprosy
is latent in the mass of the
Hawaiian people, and that in
a short time Molokai will
swallow up all of them.
The Victorian Alliance
has formed a “Civil Rights
Vigilance Committee,” the ob-
ject of which is to secure
“That no person shall be
disqualified from being ap-
pointed as a magistrate by
reason of his connection with
any temperance or religious
organisation.” The Alliance
is also passing around the
hat to pay for an appeal to the
Supreme Court against the
recent refusal of the Licen-
sing-court to entertain objec-
tions to Panton, P.M., adju-
dicating in wine cases. It
is noteworthy, however, that
though the Alliance demands
that the man who belongs to
a “ temperance or religious
association ” should be eligi-
ble, it doesn't ask that the
man who belongs to a licensed
victuallers’ association, or
anything of that kind, shall
have an equally good show.
B.J. : “ Why does The Bulletin object
so bitterly to Scripture reading in State-schools ?
Isn’t it wanted badly enough? If the soothing
word had been turned on earlier, perhaps 17-stone
Vic. Labor-member Trenwith would have asked
for the small Yarrabanker Fleming’s shirt instead
of snatching at it forcibly as he did at the Trades
Hall rumpus a few weeks ago.
One striking difference between Parkes and
Reid is that, when the former was challenged for
inconsistency, he always labored to prove either
that he wasn’t inconsistent, or that he was incon-
sistent only with the best motives, or something ;
whereas Reid, in similar circumstances, merely
fires-off a jibe and goes rejoicing on his way, as if
political honesty were really not a thing to be
worried about.
As the result of the bit! er stonewall kept
up all last session by the Maoriland Assembly
Opposition, and the wholesale chucking-out done
by the Council, about fifty measures were left un-
finished at the end of the session, besides those
actually rejected. Seddon’s new arrangement
whereby all these will be taken up next session
where they left off should save about two cubic
feet of Hansard.
Q. Attorney-General Tomjoe Byrnes used
to aver that politics were ruining him professionally
and monetarily. A Parliamentary return shows
that, besides his political salary of AlOOO a
year, Tomjoe has received from “ the Crown ”
£6491 7s. during the last seven years. Wanted to
know, moreover, how much of this consisted of
fees given out by the Attorney-General’s depart-
ment to practising barrister Byrnes ?
Premier Reid : ‘ ‘ The Local Government
Bill was lost under conditions well known.” Quite
so. The Reid Govt, wanted to give the property-
holder anything up to sixteen votes or so at muni-
cipal elections, but on the motion of the Labor-
party he was cut down to four votes. Whereupon
Premier Reid said something to the effect that he
would rather not have Local Government at all
than deprive the Fat Man of his 16 votes, and
withdrew the Bill.
The British ought to stay in Egypt—a
Professor Spratly has put that beyond doubt.
Having searched back 6000 years or so, Spratly
concludes that ancient Britain was colonised from
the banks of the Nile, that the British race has
the blood of the Pharaohs in its veins, and hence
—the “ hence” is the best part of it —hence “we
ought to retain a foothold in our ancestral acres.”
Spratly’s logic doesn’t prove that the Egyptian
should own Britain —only that the Briton should
own Egypt.
When Labor - leader Glassey tables a
motion in Queensland Assembly Ministerial and
“ Independent ” members don’t enquire what it is
or oonsider any of the arguments—they simply file
into the House when the question is put and vote
against Glassey. This is partly the fault of
Boodlewraith, and partly the fault of Glassey—
who is suoh an incapable and half-hearted cham-
pion that it’s a pleasure to oppose him on general
principles. Glassey is about the most melancholy
item in the most melanoholy Labor-party in Aus-
tralia.

MRS. MULLAREY : “I came up to tell yez, Mr. Peddy fog, that ye
mustn’t tache Latin to little Micky.”
PEDDYFOG : “It was only to teach him the derivation of the English ,

MRS. MULLAREY (hotly): “Faith, and it would be far better to

Dear Bulletin, —Why should the munici-
pal franchise be exercised by ratepayers only ? My
landlady rents a house at Bs. per week, and has
her name on the ratepayers’ roll, She lets rooms
unfurnished. There are four of us lodgers alto-
gether, and, combined, we more than pay the
rent. Her rates come out of our rent. In other
ways we contribute far more to the revenue (in-
cluding the municipal subsidy) than she does, for
she neither drinks nor smokes. She is a most
charming woman and votes most regularly— for
the local baker. But, seeing that each of us in-
directly pays his full share of the rates, why
shouldn’t we have a vote, too?—F.C.
The election for East Adelaide to fill
Labor-leader M'Pherson’s place is fixed for 22nd
Jan., when Premier Kingston and many other
prominent party chiefs will be absent at the
Melb. Convention. Despite the exhortation of
the dying leader to his followers: “Stick together,
boys ! ” there is a likelihood of a split in the
Labor ranks over the election. Hutchinson, one
of the proprietors of Quiz, is the chosen candidate,
but Salmon, who at the last three contests was
chairman of M‘Phersbn’s committee, thinks he
has a special claim on the district, and threatens
to oppose him. Salmon is a Charlestonite, which
means “ one of those damned neuters who in
their middle way of steering are neither fish, nor
flesh, nor good red herring.” The Conservatives
have never returned a member for E. Adelaide
since the resignation of Dutton Green in May, ’B6.
How the politicians do yearn after Feder-
ation ! The Convention is due on the 20th. The
finance committee, which was going to devote the
recess to the preparation of a carefully-thought-
out scheme of Federal finance, now thinks of
meeting on the 17th, so that it will have two
clear days to tackle the toughest and most com-
plex problem in Australasia. Fysh, of Tasmania,
however, wants the meeting postponed till the
19th, in which case the committee will have one
clear evening before it, and Turner implores the
committee by telegraph to brace up and be
there on the 17th. In fact, it seems that most
of the members of the finance committee are
so busy opening the pumpkin show at the town-
ship of Jones’s Dog that they really can’t arrive
in time.
At latest advices Wise, Reid, Neila and
other people were still writing to the papers at in-
tervals about the circumstances attending Reid’s
election as N.S.W. Foreign-trade leader, and con-
sidering that none of their stories at all resemble
each other, one begins to wonder if any human
being is really capable of remembering anything
accurately, and if any evidence or history is of
any value whatever. The following, from Reid’s
reply to Wise, is interesting :
He speaks of his having resisted a great temptation, as
if the leadership were offered to him. What occurred
was this : Names were being proposed, and someone pro-
posed Mr. Wise No human being got up to echo the
wish that he should be leader, but he writes as though the
position had actually been pressed on him, and he had
exercised heroic self-denial in declining it. I was absent
when my merits and demerits were being discussed, so I
am perfectly ignorant of what occurred.
This positive assurance on the part of the man
who also says that he is perfectly ignorant is very
beautiful.

Kerosene being used as gas for cooking is
now nothing new. The difficulty hitherto with
the old “.Primus ” stoves has been the objection-
able hissing noise they make whilst burning, and
this has been entirely overcome by a new kerosene
gas stove called the “ Jupiter,” which has later
improvements and burns silently. The gas has
simply to pass through a cylindrical burner before
being consumed, which subdues the noise and
makes the combustion more complete. The stoves,
with the cooking apparatus, can be seen working

the time above mentioned, and that persons failing to
furnish such Return are not relieved from liability to the
penalty by reason only that they may not have received a
notice to furnish the Return, or that the prescribed Form
may not have been delivered to them.
The attention of owners of taxable lands subject to mort-
gage is minted to the necessity for furnishing the requisite
particulars, in order that they may derive the benefit of
the deduction to which they are entitled in respect of the
Interest on the mortgage of their lands, including im-
provements thereon. Without these particulars the Com-
missioners will be unable to make the statutory deduction
from the tax on the unimproved values.
Forms of the prescribed Return may be obtained on
application at any of the abovementioned offices, and
should be furnished on or before the above date.

257 George-street, Sydney.*
John Norton, editor of Truth and re-
former of N.S.W. libel-law, has just emerged
victorious-in-a-manner-of-speakingfrom two heavy
actions. In one of them, where plaintiff claimed
■£2ooo, a 40s. verdict was given against Norton,
carrying some .£2OO or more in costs. In the
other, brought by Minister-for-Lands Carruthers
against Norton and Geo. Lonsdale (the alleged
writer of a letter to Truth calling in question
Carruthers’ lands administration), the Arinidale
jury failed to agree after a twelve-days’ trial; and
defendants were discharged under recognizances
to appear again for trial if called on. The
onus of the charges against Carruthers was
that he had improperly used his influence
in the matter of certain peculiar exchanges of land
—particularly one connected with the Salisbury
Court squattage, where Carruthers used to go
shooting. To buttress his general case against
Carruthers’ character, Norton produced Susan
Blackburn, a young woman who swore that Car-
ruthers used to have immoral relations with her,
and to whom Carruthers admitted having given
money—£lo or £20 —out of kindness of heart
and without any consideration. Carruthers de-
nied all the charges. The jury failed to agree.
As to what were the respective numbers for and
against acquittal, there are various printed state-
ments ; the defence avers that there were ten
jurors on its side, while the prosecution declares,
per medium of the Government morning organ,
that not more than two held out in favor
of defendant Norton (whom the Crown law-
office cordially detests, and against whom
the prosecution was mainly directed). During
the trial it transpired that Carruthers had
been semi-privately divorced by Judge Windeyer
at an unusual hour on Saturday morning (which is
not a regular sitting day), and that successful
efforts had been made (not, as Carruthers alleged,
by him) to keep press and public away. Interest-
ing question—Then who did instigate the secret
sitting? A noticeable feature of the libel-trial
was the extraordinary interest taken in the pro-
ceedings by the Crown Law Dept.—the in-
fluence of the police and detective force
being apparently utilised to a remarkable extent
on behalf of Carruthers, while a private letter was
read from Attorney-General Want to Carruthers
stating, in reference to a conversation with Nor-
ton, that the latter “ would find out when he was
doing two years for the libel.” The Crown-law
office gives it out that Norton is again to be tried
in April. From this The Bulletin infers that
Attorney-General Want intends to resign mean-
while, as it is altogether unlikely he would be
guilty of the indecency of personally filing a bill
against the man who seemingly is in his opinion
guilty and deserving of two years’ gaol. Both
Mr. Moss, counsel for Lonsdale, and Norton, who
defended himself, made very able addresses to the
jury—of the damaging character of which the very
meagre reports in the Sydney papers give no idea
whatsoever.

GREAT MECCA OF HEALTH.
THE SICK ARE CURED, AND THE
SCEPTICS LEFT TO WONDER.
In this nineteenth century science has made
rapid progress in treating with disease in every
form. Researches and study have brought to
light many remedies that are invaluable to suffer-
ing humanity, but, without doubt, the greatest
and grandest in the annals of medical science is
the accurate location of disease, which it would
seem from the great errors that are made could
not be acquired, but must be a direct" gift front
birth. That the POLYPATHS can, without
feeling the pulse, looking at the tongue, or asking
any questions of the patient, locate unerringly
each disease of which a patient may be suffering,
is a fact that can easily be tested at their Insti-
tute, 84 Collins-street, Melbourne. Never in the
history of Melbourne has any body of men created
such a sensation or been the object of such wide-
spread comment and universal praise as has been
accorded the POLYPATHS since their Institute
was founded in Melbourne. But it is as it should
be, and only goes to prove that the citizens of
Melbourne are fully competent to discriminate
between the good and the bad. Their success has
spread like wildfire far beyond the borders of Vic-
toria among those who had given up all hope of
ever being well again, and they are by hundreds
availing themselves of the Polypathic system in
both the diagnosis as well as the treatment of
chronic disease.
Here begins the very first starting point of van-
tage with the Polypathic school of practice, for its
chief exponents are men who from a natural and
overpowering sympathy with all forms of disease
and suffering are able to determine by quickened
sense, surely and with determination, the exact
cause of all that makes life a burden to thousands
all over the land. The highways of medical prac-
tice are strewn with the wrecks of wrong diagnosis,
with the going out of innumerable lives because of
improper location of disease, far more than from
the use of remedies of themselves hurtful. Here
Polypathy plants its foot firmly down and de-
clares that not alone the instinctive, keen sense
of its consulting chief should be taken as a finality,
but that what he determines is the real trouble,
with all the causes that led him to the clear
conclusion, shall be placed before the full faculty
of the Institute and his judgment meet their
approval.
Polypathy claims the freedom of accepting the
good and rejecting the bad in all systems ; it has
formulated special treatment for itself in all
chronic cases ; it prepares its own medicines, and
applies them in the light of nineteenth century
progress.
The success attending this new system is almost
miraculous, but it is in accordance with natural
and applied science, and wins its constant vic-
tories over disease because of the true following
out of that which natural ability and the highest
developments of Medical Science has shown to be
sure and true. All sufferers are welcome to the
fullest investigation of the POLYPATHIC
MEDICAL INSTITUTE, of 84 Collins Street,
MELBOURNE. Wm. HENRY GAZE, M.D.,
L.S.A. Lond., L.R.C.P. Lond., M.R.C.S. Eng.,
is Physician in charge.
Those who cannot call personally, can
write. All communications are strictly con-
fidential, and an opinion will be given in all
cases by return mail where the consultation
fee of Ios. is enclosed.
More capital invested, more physicians
employed, more cures made by the Poly-
pathic System, than by any other similar
Institution or body of Physicians in the
world.*

SPOUTING NOTIONS.
Partisans of either side can lay down various
fines of argument on the question of the cracked wicket
in the second test-match. An Australian barracker will
say that the wicket must have been all right for the Eng-
lishmen's first innings because two of the tail-end men
made 100 runs between them, and must have been equally
all right for the follow-on, seeing that Maclaren and
Ranji. took the score to 65 before the second wicket fell.
Why shouldn’t the super-excellence of Australian bowling
and fielding get full credit for the awful defeat of the
other fellows ?
Then the English barracker puts in his little
lot of statistics. The Australians, winning the toss,
scored 434 runs before the fifth wicket was taken, so the
state of the ground, which was then beginning to help
the bowlers, accounted for the rapid downfall of six men.
All out for 520. Then the visitors went in and scored 22
runs for 1 wicket on the Monday evening, 289 for 7
wickets next day, and only 155 for 12 wickets on the
fourth day. How would it have gone if Maclaren had
won the toss ? To all this the kangaroo barracker may
reply that the team which couldn’t get rid of more than
three batsmen during the first day deserved to be jolly
well beaten, anyhow. Which is The Bulletin’s argu-
ment. Sauce for the goose in the Sydney match was
sauce for the gander at Melbourne.
• * * *
The English captain’s policy of putting Richard-
son aside for a long spell when only 12 runs had been
registered against him was imitated by Trott in the case
of Trumble. The long Victorian who went to bed on the
Monday night with a record of one easy catch (Maclaren’s)
missed off him for six runs, out of a 22 total, didn’t re-
sume bowling on Tuesday until the Englishmen had put
on another 130. Trumble had 4 wickets for 54 at the end
of the innings.
* • * •
Rather noticeable that none of the Melbourne
Eapers commented on the Englishmen’s bad luck in
aving Hayward hors de combat just when his bowling
services were most needed.
* • • •
For versatility of talent in catching a cricket-
ball, straight from the bat or off the ground, the writer
would like to back bowler Jones against Ranjitsinjhi and
humanity in general. *
* * * •
An old dog for a hard road. The board showed
8 wickets for 224 when little Johnny Briggs marched out
to take his first innings in the second test, and the
“ veteran ” (Johnny is aged 35 or so) cast an eye over the
figures on his way to the wicket. At the drawing of the
stumps, an hour later, Johnny was not out with 45 to his
name —and didn’t he get a reception from the members’
reserve 1 A good many people, seemingly, had backed
the visitors to make 300. Briggs was their blessed salva-
tion.
* • • •
Maclaren is at a disadvantage on the M.C.C.
ground. Melbourne is the home of his bride-elect, and
the knowledge that the brightest angel in all the wcrld
will nearly break her little heart if he doesn’t make a big
score troubles Mac. considerably. Anyway, he has shown
some weak, nervous batting in Melbourne this trip.
• • * •
Judging from the rival first-innings scores in
the two test-matohes already played, the next will be
decided by the toss—given a hard wicket at the outset.
And the fourth match? Well, why not arrange for the
side that wins the toss in Adelaide to lose it in Melbourne
and let the fifth struggle be for the odd trick ? Then we
would have the question of supremacy settled with the
minimum of luck either way, and the maximum of gate-
money always.
* » • *
The mercury in the Giffen thermometer fell
from Hero to Zero last week.
• • • •
Iredale, after the Sydney match, predicted that
the Australians would do better on the faster Melbourne
wicket. When the Melbourne match fell due, all the
“ experts ” were astonished at the slowness of the
wicket, and the Australians won in a walk.
• • * *
When Jones had provided the sensation of the
second test-match by bowling a stump in twain, a cynical
question was raised—” Would the promoters deduct the
price of the stump from Jones’ account?”
• * • *
The lamentable inferiority of the Australian in
catching, referred to in a recent Bulletin, was further
shown in the Melbourne test-match. On the second day,
while the Englishmen were making 20 odd, three chances
were missed, and for one of these no less than three Aus-
tralians made a wild rush. The necessity of putting
“ Tracker ” Forbes, the boss high mark of the Vic. foot-
ball field, into the next test team becomes more apparent
every day.
* « * •
“The wicket in the second test-match looked
worse than it played, and was a big factor in the easy
defeat of be visitors,” says a Melbourne authority. In
other words, they funked it ?
• • * *
Revenge is sweet. The first man to wring
Noble's hand when he bowled Ranji was Jones.
• * * *
Ranji’s criticism of Noble, who clean beat and
bowled him, is distinctly good : “ Noble bowled very well,
but he had a certain amount of luck in having things
come his own way.” And then he goes on to say that
Noble had three chances missed off his bowling. This
looks much as if Ranji feels bound to feed the inter-
viewers with copy at any price, padding with tommy-rot
when he has played out his cricket sense.
.At last the public begin to wonder where the
idiotic rule about playing out a match on the one wicket
came from. For years past nobody has dared to question
the wisdom of the Marjdebone Club, and thousands of
cricketers all the world over have tamely submitted to a
set of rules made by a mere handful of men in England—
to the domination of the English cricket-Pope, in fact.
If sportsmen of all sorts were not the most pig-headed
and Conservative class extant, some of them would have
long since seen the absurdity of compelling perhaps
three-and-a-half innings to be played on a pitch like a
washing-board, or in prohibiting the erection of any pro-
tective covering after playing hours. By far the most
sensible course, and one obvious to all but those blinded
by fetish-worship and prejudice, would be to prepare, say,
four pitches on a ground and allow the batting team each
innings to have its pick. In case of rain the pitches
should also be covered. And what earthly reason is there
why all matches should not be played on matting as with
most first-class Australian junior teams? Matting would
provide a splendid surface and eliminate much of the
luck of the toss.
* » * •
The exceeding Hinglishness of Ranji’s walk and
general style makes one smile. The dark gentleman has
such an awfully jolly Johnbull air of don’tcareadam about
the bowling, bai jove 1 He is as English as Sydney’s
Chinese Anglican clergyman, Soo Hoo Ten—who,‘viewed
aft, is the most English-looking and gentlemanly member
of the N.S.W. Angiican “ cloth.”
* • • •
Talking about the no-balling of Jones, and the
question whether Richardson “ throws,” recalls the time
when Coningham was no-balled, and was alleged by most
of the Press to have thrown the ball at Stoddart in a rage.
The true in’ards of that affair may or may not have
escaped all print, but they certainly never appeared in the
big papers. Coningham, vexed, took the ball, and meant
to “ throw ”it on the ground. Seeing where it was going,
he said, even before the ball had time to hit, “ I beg your
pardon, Mr. Stoddart.” Stoddart replied, “It’s lucky
you apologised, or I’d. have punched your head.” Autho-
rity : Austin, who was at “ slip ” in that particular
match.
* * » •
The snobbish “Mr.” distinguishing the ama-
chaw and professional cricketers of the English teams
seems to be completely dead, and the daily press now
honors all members with the prefixing initials, hitherto
confined to the “gentlemen.” Among the latter K. S.
Ranjitsinhji figures as if he had been baptised Kumar
Shri. As a matter of fact these words signify “ His
Nibs,” or something of that sort, and as Ranji is no
longer a royal highness, but merely a claimant to the
throne of a kingdom about as important as Collingwood,
he should by rights figure as simple Ranjitsinhji.
.£7650 was put through the totalisator at the
Q.T.C. summer meeting.
• ■ * *
Jack Brewer’s lease of Rosella expired the other
day, and he sent the ruinous mare back to Bill Long with
many curses on her head. Brewer was going to make
millions out of Rosella —win the Caulfield Cup with her,
for instance—but she dropped his money every time.
• * * »
It looks as though Auraria will run again at
Flemington in the autumn, notwithstanding that she
shaped last Nov. like one gone wrong internally.
* • • *
Embrasure and Mischief, winners of the Stand-
ish and Bagot double, at Flemington on New Year’s Day,
came from the one stable, Mark Whitty’s. While the
gentle public was paralysed at seeing the unthought-of
and 25 to 1 Embrasure collar the Standish, the stable was
joyfully rubbing its hands at first leg of its double. Mis-
chief was the other.
• • * •
A Wallace that is not the Wallace has been
entered for the Newmarket Handicap. A two-year-old
Amberite is among the entries for the Caulfield Futurity
Stakes, and a Vigil, not the crack jumper, is in the same
race. Horse nomenclature is ever poor in new ideas.
• • • •
Entries for Caulfield Futurity Stakes, a ,£3OOO
experiment of the V.A.T.O. to be decided next month,
number 56 cracks, canterers, and crocks. People who
should know say that about a dozen will start, and that
the club will drop or alter the Futurity in future.
* * * *
Le Yar, winner of the Perth Cup, is owned by
A. G. Jenkins, ex-Attorney-General Burt’s son-in-law,
and himself the lawyer-Mayor of the goldfields’ centre.
The horse carried the money of the goldfields’ “toff-
push.” R. B. Pell, whose career on the Eastern turf was
meteoric, is—many will be glad to hear—among the heavy
winners.
* • • *
Wonderful the change wrought by the new S. A.
Gaming Act. Hundreds of people crowded Grenfell-street,
Adelaide, under the old system, and the Cohen sort of
tobacconist tote-shop flourished in every lane. Tote
books were made on every race in Australia from Melb.
Cup to Smith’s-Curse Plate. All this has vanished, and
the tote man now has to do his business down a sewer or
in a remote back alley, with the risk ever before him of a
heavy fine or six months.
* * * *
“ The American jockey Tod Sloan lost .£lOOO
by coming in second, on Keenan, in the November
Handicap,” writes Thb Bulletin’s London correspondent,
“ but, nevertheless, he closed the English racing season
amidst a bunch of laudatory rockets. Five mounts in
six races against the cream of jockeyhood, resulting in
four wins and a second, merited the thousands waiting
ankle-deep in half-frozen slush to cheer the popular
lightweight. Sloan holds half a million dollars, and is
miles ahead of his English confreres in intelligence,
education, and horse-sympathy. He says he lost Lord
William Beresford’s big wager to nothing simply owing
to the going being so ‘rotten,’ Asteric, with 6st. 61b.,
giving Keenan, at 7st. 31b., a bad beating. It is ex-
pected the Yank will return and settle here, where
there is big money for Chris. Moores and Jimmy Hayses
without unpleasant risks.”
• • • •
The League of Vic Wheelmen, in its judicial
oapacity, has bitten the very dust. Reduced to a cash
capital of £5OO odd, and with the risk of an unsuccessful
race-meeting staring it in the face, the league cast aside
its poor little bit of “ frill,” and made a desperate effort
to resuscitate Bilmartin. Bill, in days of yore, was a
never-failing attraction, so the League advertised that he
would come to the assistance of Saturday’s meeting in
the event of his disqualification being removed by the
S.A. League. Subsequently a Melbourne petition to that
end was despatched to Adelaide; also, the secretary of
the L.Y.W. sent a telegram announcing William’s readi-
ness to star at the local show if the S.A. authorities would
relent. Which telegram was a shocking waste of silver,
considering that the L.V.W. might just as well have posted
an old newspaper with Bill’s name looming large in the
ad. The iron-jawed plugger didn’t get his disqualification
annulled. Moreover, the Adelaide League dropped some
nasty remarks concerning the L.V.W.’s financial policy,
and as soon as the Melbourne people begin to grasp the
full significance of those remarks they will denounce
“ the governing body of cycling” as a vain thing. The
almighty dollar is the ruling power on bike-tracks. Cir-
cumstances, beyond the control of anybody in particular,
will always prevent man-racing from being bossed to the
same degree that horse-racing is made respectable. And
heaven knows the management of the Australian turf
doesn’t command all possible respect.
This was the Vic. League’s telegram to the
S.A. League : “Megson, Barden and Kellow disqualified
for a month from January 1. Places us awkward fix
owing to engagements at our meeting. Wire result
Martin’s appeal first thing, as he is entered in anticipa-
tion,” The awkwardness of the fix was mitigated by
quashing the local disqualification of Barden and Kellow,
and leaving Megson to work out his sentence.
* * • *
The Melb. crowd, which stayed away vigorously
from recent bike-shows on the Friendly track, showed
that it has not lost interest in the sport by rolling up at
the Exhibition last Saturday when Green and Walne met
to contest the Brassard which Meg-son forfeited by his
disqualification. The one-mile event was a splendid race
—the best, in the opinion of all followers of the game,
this_ season. The pacing was by singles, and the
brilliant dash of Barden for Green, and the steady, solid
plugging of Body for Walne 'was worth going miles to
see. The dash of Barden got Green a lead of five lengths,
which the Maorilander stolidly carried Walne over, leav-
ing them to enter the last lap level. There was no wait-
ing ; they raced all the way, and when Walne beat the
Englishman by half-a-wheel, the time showed the fifth of
the mile to have been turned off in 22 4-sth sec.
The second race, over the ten miles, was a
smellful-looking affair. Numerous accidents to the
pacing triplets took place, but there were still enough
left to act had they done what was expected of them.
Walne’s teams rode splendidly, but some of the English-
man’s pace was most suspicious-looking. It seems
incredible that a team of experienced pacers should not
be able to shift a triplet faster than Green could ride
sitting up, yet that is how the Englishman did ride for a
great part of the way—indeed, for half-a-lap he actually
rode “ hands off.”. He repeatedly called for more pace,
but could not get it, and so disgusted was he at the end
of 33 laps that he rode off the track. Walne rode splen-
didly throughout, and finished alone with a great sprint.
Green was so “narked ” that he refused to get
up for England in the pursuit-race, Great Britain v. Aus-
tralasia, and consequently the dear old mother country
fell rather badly, all its men dropping out, whilst only
two of the Southerners’ four were beaten. “ Newhaven”
Jackson beat Body by about 20 yds. for first place.
The 25-mile championship in the evening was a
fine race, little Gargurevich scoring his first big win.
Gargyis oneof Vic.’s most popular riders. He is a first-
class man, has performed always well and honestly, and
yet he has never won more than a £lO prize in bis life
prior to this. He is especially good behind pace, and
possibly he may have a go at the Brassard later on.
« * » *
Ainsworth, the English crack, is horrified at
the number of “ wobblers ” that infest the Australian
tracks, to the danger not only of themselves but of better
riders. He attributes this, with some degree of truth, to
the athletic ambitions of the average Australian .youth.
“ As soon as a lad here can raise the cash for a bicycle,”
says Ainsworth, “he rushes off and buys a racing-
machine, although he may never have been on a bike in
his life previously. The result is that nobody can follow
a strange rider with a feeling of safety.” But the worst
of Aust. wobblers are men who have been years at the
game and who have won big prizes.
THE LEATHER-HUNTERS.
(Inspired by It. Kipling.)
Where do we hunt the leather
By stubborn fingers stitched ?
On hill-tops crowned with heather
Our wicket stumps are pitched J,
Across the droughted grasses
Of southern station lands
The restless red ball passes
From eager hands to hands.
Cutting the Capeweed wayward
Or bruising shamrocks low,
It madly rushes havward
Or merely dribbles slow.
Otago’s sweetest clovers
Are born to bear the brunts
Of running maiden overs,
And long-day leather-hunts.
Our slim white-blousbd sisters
At wave-washed Warrnainbool
Send down their curly twisters
To ’tice the bearded fool.
And where Gib.’s rock unyielding
Frowns darkly on the main
One sees our sodgers fielding
At furthest point of Spain.
’Tis warmed by Austral gum-fire,
’Tis fixed in frozen zones—
The Hempire of the umpire
Who no-b’led nimble Jones.
Through regions green and barren,
In climates cold and hot
The trumdling of Maclaren
Is credited to Trott.
We hunt the bounding leather
Which batsmen “ time ” and “ place,”
Where e’er we herd together
To breed God’s ruling race ;
Where e’er the bit of bunting
Proclaims Great British state,
There do we hunt the leather
For glory and for—Gate.
Melb.
E.P.

Sydney Sunday Times geography: “Hayti,
the black republic in the Pacific. ”
When the great Jubilee orgy was in pro-
gress, the Cape of Good Hope got so especially
drunk that it promised to present Britain with a
splendid battleship. Then it cooled and sug-
gested that Britain should build the ship itself,
and the Cape people would pay interest on the
cost. Now it has frozen hard, and doesn’t quite
see its way even to pay the interest. Considering
how many people asked Australia, at that great
Jubilee whisky time, if it didn’t feel ashamed of
itself for not presenting a battleship also, this
result is highly interesting.
Cabled that 104,388 Scotchmen have peti-
tioned the Queen (Heaven only knows what she
has to do with it) that the terms “Britain” and
“ British ” be more widely used instead of
“England’’and “English.” It is mighty hard
to satisfy some people. The victories of the
“English” army everybody has heard of, still,
when the English army runs away, isn’t it always
described as a lamentable British defeat ? And if
these 104,388 Scotchmen are always remembered
when the skedaddle is in progress, what are these
104,388 Scotchmen grumbling about ?
TO DARKEN GREY HAIR.
