Many tales are told of Gov. Brassey’s visit
to Mildura. This isn’t a tale; only a-fragment.
Euston, like all towns on the Gov.’s route, wished
to be c-n fete, and the leading townsman gathered
flags from the four winds, so to speak—anyhow,
they were a pretty ragged lot, and wanted wash-
ing before being strung on a clothes-line across the
street. He was busy fixing them on to the line,
when a neighbor came along to do the overseeing.
The leading townsman knew nothing of nautical
lore —he was only learned in dust and horses and
wool —but he had heard of the.signal-code and the
Sunbeam, so, when the neighbor assumed a sneer-
ing aspect and inquired what on earth he meant
by placing this flag and that in this and that posi-
tion on the line, he felt awkward and doubtful,
and took the offending banners off again. Then
the line looked gappy, and the leading townsman,
with his hands full of banners, grew hot and cross,
and the neighbor grew more arrogant and lectured
him: “Don’t you know that Brassey can read
flags like anything? Some of them-banners might
read as a welcome, and others he might take for a
blessed insult.” “Well,” decided the leading
townsman, “ he can take them any way he likes.
Anyhow, I’m going to hang up the lot! ” But, after
all, Brassey didn’t see them. It was midnight when
he reached Euston, and instead of stopping at the
pub. and shouting for all hands, as was reasonably
expected of him, he went straight from the punt
out to Euston station, without so much as looking
in upon the town or its decorat.ons. Same thing
next morning on passing through the town ; and
the dry and disgusted Eustonites didn’t even raise
a cheer. “Is that what we pays Governors
for ? ” asked the local publican of the crowd ; and
the crowd supposed so.

A Russian chemist has found a new anaes-
thetic several thousand times more powerful than
chloroform, which volatilises most readily and)
when mixed freely with air, will act at great dis-
tances. Experiments are being made to see if it
cannot be enclosed in bombs, with a view to put-
ting opposing troops to sleep instead of to death.
In N.S.W. it is not necessary for a
death to be registered prior to burial of the
body, and a medical certificate as to cause of
death is not obligatory. The Australasian
Medical Gazette draws attention to the need of
tresh legislation in the public interest and for
private safeguard.
George S. Perrin, Tic. Conservator of
lorests, avers that Boyle’s “giant” gum, stated
by Baron von Mueller to be 500 feet high, is but
zlj feet 6m., the latter height being established
by a theodolite survey by Engineer Fuller, of Yio.
Water Works Department. Also, that no tree in
Victoria has yet been found which is up to or over
400 feet. Up to now the story has gone that there
were lots of 400 ft. eucalyptus in the Cape
Otway ranges.
A mechanical marvel is the new monotype-
machine, which will cast its own type, set, and
properly space-out its matter, without being
touched by a human finger. The matter to beset
is first punched on a roll of paper in round holes
corresponding to the position of the matrix of the
type on the composing machine, and this roll is
then ted into t e machine, which casts and sets
the types separately and places them in lines and
columns on a galley without further human agency*
Justification is rendered possible by the fact that
although the line is set forward, the machine sets
it backwards, and thus has the key to the spacing
before the line is set at all.
The late Earl of Peterborough was a iioted
character in London drawing-rooms for his consia-
tent intoxication, and other things* He once went
to a reception by the Duchess of Lansdowne, and
arrived early but full. Unluckily, as he ap-
proached to pay his respects to the hostess, art
ottoman interposed, and the old gentleman—whose
best-known nickname was ** Early —fell sprawl-
ing on the floor at the feet of the duchess, and
there he remained, perfectly content to lie till
morning. But the lady was indignant. “ Get
away, you drunkeii wretch!” she said, spurning
him with her foot. “Early” raised his head irt
pained remonstrance —“ Not drunk, duchess,” he
assured her, pathetically, not drunk i only very -
very tired 1” '

Dear Bulletin,— Re Texas (Q.) tobacco-
growmg. _ The persons responsible for the Chinese
invasion include some very big squatters and
pastoral companies. I hear there is enough
tobacco to last for six years, and although some
of the small men have to knock off for want of
funds, there is no decrease in the number of
Chinese ; on the contrary, people are still renting
ground and sub-letting to Chinese. To give an
idea of the beauties of hard graft, I know a man
who has just got £4 6s. for over twelve months’
work at tobacco, and another has received about
>*4oo as his share of 40 acres Chinese-grown leaf,
j This man pays Id. per acre per annum for his
lease of 4000 acres. As Tex As gave Foxtort 33 of
his majority of 50, and made him Minister for
Lands, he will no doubt do something for them. (?)
It was in Fox ton’s paddock that the Chir c -~*>
grew tobacco here. Why people want Chinese is
a puzzle, as some of the tobacco-growers have men
working for ss. per week. Chinamen would take
prussic acid rather than accept such a wage. Most
of the Chinese employers are natives. The Aus-
tralian is the most unpatriotic animal on God’s
footstool.—H. S. R.

Professor Hartigan, of West Virginia
University, U.S.A., reports in New York Medi-
cal Journal (Nov. 13) the birth of a tremen-
dously, big baby. Scanzoni (Italy) found, on nine
thousand births, an average of 71b. weight. Lusk,
of Bellevue Hospital, New York, got an average
from 200 births of seven and two-thirds lbs. But
Hartigan’s case—a young woman of 21 years, first
child—went up to 181 b. As a check, the father
and the doctor and another physician weighed the
child. Both parents were small and young.

His Own Sauce!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Morton the missionary hated Chinamen
tike—a Cornstalk. The feeling was an actual
part of the man—one of those despotic instincts
without which he would hare been simply some-
body else.
“ The worse Christian, I! ”he dolefully acknow-
ledged. “ God made the Chinaman, and Christ
died for him” (this was conventional) “but”
(and this was the man himself) “ I almost wish He
hadn’t 1 ”
So, when Kum Sang Lo, the chief trader of
Tibak, in Borneo, laid a fat, familiar paw upon
the gospeller’s shrinking shoulder, the white man
responded with a most ungodly kick. A Dyak
would for this have split his skull, a Malay would
have stabbed him, and another white man would
have knocked him down. Kum Sang Lo did none
of these things. He didn’t jabber curses either,
which was a bad sign; and he smiled benignly,
which was a worse one.
“Why you that?” he said quietly. “Me
Clistian ! Me b’long Singapore. Me Baba—
Blitish subjick, all same you ! ”
Then Morton knew that he had done a foolish
thing. The Baba, the Sirigapore-born Chinaman
—with the patience of one race, the intelligence
of two, and the rascality of a dozen —is about the
most solid and effective enemy any man can make.
And when Kum Sang Lo, still plaintively assert-
ing his British nationality, had shuffled away
amongst the sugar-canes, Morton told himself that
the climate of Tibak would hardly be a healthy
one for him in future. “I’ll get out of it to-
morrow ! ” said he.
But the latitude of Tibak is exactly 0, and such
intimate association with the equator is not con-
ducive to promptitude. The morrow came, and
Morton didn’t go—then another morrow, and he
couldn’t go, for it found him lashed —pending the
completion of a commercial transaction—to a post
of the head-house.
The business didn’t take long. Kum Sang
Lo, “Christian and British subject,”of the one
part, did engage and contract, upon receipt of the
body of one Moritun, a white man, to make over
and convey unto Joga-Rut, Orang-Kaya, pagan,
and subject only of himself, of the other part, ten
Rusa jars in good order and condition.* The
high contracting parties lighted each a cigar-
puffed—exchanged—puffed again—laid the cigars
end to end on the ground between them—and
Morton was the Chinaman’s chattel!
Not without protest. ‘ ‘ Thy father, Matu, would
not have done this thing,” Morton said, as with
rattan lashings he was being trussed neck-and-
heel for transport. ‘ ‘ Not for a hundred Rusas
would he have sold his friend to this yellow
reptile! ”
Matu had indeed been Morton’s friend, and
Joga was hardly his enemy. But Joga was
greedy, and the price was big—about .£l2O of
British money. So he made answer—sucking
durian-pulp the while:
“ My father, Matu, having made a bargain,
kept it. This bargain is made, and I am my
father’s son. There is no more to be said.”
• * * *
Unbound, but caged and left alone, Morton
wondered what was to come next. But not till
evening did he know. The trader’s pig-eyed
satellites brought him food, and them he ques-
tioned. Malay he used; then Dyak ; lastly Eng-
bsh ; but the fellows were newly-imported
binkehs—native-born Chinamen—and not a word
did they understand ; so Morton, hungry, turned
to the food they had brought. Stewed fowl and
yams, maize-bread and pratehan. Pratchan—
prawn-paste—is good, but it is fiery-salt, and
Morton instinctively took the cover off the big
water-jar in the corner. It was full, and, re-
assured, he set to work upon the solids. But
presently the pratchan told, and Morton dipped a
brass cup and drank—one mouthful only ! The
water was sea-water!
This was to. be it, then ! A heavy price for a
kick I the thirst-death of China; known also
well in Borneo, and not entirely unpractised by
the paternal Hollander upon the recalcitrant
Javani. The door? —the floor?—the walls? No
use ! The bamboos—smooth as glass and flinty
hard—let in between them light and air, but
would have held an elephant. What light there
was by this time was moonlight, and the prisoner,
peeping-through a chink, saw, black and moveless
on the glinting sea, a big Malay prahu.
• m ■ * m
When Morton, half-a-dozen years before,
had saved, in Singapore, the life of Radeen Alang,
he had made, unwittingly, a first-class investment
in gratitude. Beset by bhang-drunken Klings
upon the Tanjong Passar road, the little Malay
skipper—but for the sturdy man of God, his fist,
and foot—would have been bambooed to a jelly,
and quietly dropped thereafter into the mangrove
swamp. And now the prahu Morton saw was
Radeen’s, across from Singapore for cocoanuts and
sago, and loaded up this night for sea. Radeen,
ashore at midday, had seen the white man carried
into Kum Sang Lo’s house. The beard was long
and the hair grizzled, but the Malay eye is sharp ;
Radeen knew his man. The trader, questioned,
grinned and said nothing; whereupon Radeen,
ruminating, went off to his vessel and made his
crew a little speech.
At moonset they came ashore in a body—Madura
men and Sundanese—and, when the craft got her
anchor up at dawn, she had Morton and Kum
Sang Lo as passengers—the Chinaman against
his will. All day the. prahu slipped along eight
knots before a ‘ soldier’s wind ” and at night,
while Morton slept, she left behind upon the
moonlit water a little raft of empty casks. There
was a naked man upon it—Kum Sang Lo —a tam-
bok of salt fish, a jar of pratchan, and —well, he
had the whole ocean to drink from.
.Next day “ cooked ” the castaway, but not “ in
his own sauce,” after all. A shaven crown, an
equatorial sun, a mad plunge over, ana—a well-
dined shark.
ALEX. MONTGOMERY.
* Antique jars of peculiar make figure largely as repre-
sentatives of Bornean wealth. The Naga jar is worth
about £8, the Rusa £12, and the Gusi, which is extremely
rare, is value for about £500 worth of property.
The Fight at Brannigan's Hole.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Brannigan’s Hole was like a racecourse. The
fence by the creek was lined with buggies and
hacks, and everybody was talking about Uncle
Jim and “Stiffener” Welsh. “Crook” Moore
and “ Spud ” Maloney marked out a ring at the
bottom of the slope, where Brannigan had set up
a cask of beer. Uncle Jim came over the bridge
looking hot and flurried ; several bookies, who
had come up for the Skinner Flat Handicap, ran
over to meet him, and said he’d better have a rub-
down after his walk. Uncle Jim kept at a dis-
tance and waved them off.
“Gentlemen,” he said, “ I’m as fit as a nail-can ;
I’m trained to the hour.” He walked to the ring
and scrutinised it closely. “A man can’t be too
careful about his ground when he’s depending on
his footwork and reach,” he said. “ Reach and
footwork will pull me through, boys.”
(Uncle Jim has a wonderful reach. When Aunt
kept a boarding-house at Mudgee, the butcher
used to drive up under the window, and walk
round to the side-door for orders. One of the kids
would watch until his back was turned, and sing
out “ Now ! ” Like a flash Uncle Jim would reach
out of the window and collect all the loose corned
beef and sausages that happened to be at the
bottom of the cart. Uncle Jim’s reach is some-
thing above the average).
The crowd shifted suddenly, and everyone said,
“ Here he is.” “ Stiffener ” Welsh skipped out of
a buggy and shook hands with “ Crook ” Moore.
About 60 men left the cask of beer to ask
“ Stiffener ” how he felt. Stiffener didn’t say
anything. “Crook” Moore went up to Uncle
Jim, and told him that over a hundred pounds
would change hands on the event, and he wanted
to know whether Uncle would put up five pounds
as a guarantee that there would be no faking.
Uncle Jim looked into “Crook’s” one eye.
“ S’pose I lick hell outer Welsh, how much will I
git?”
“ A purse of fifty quid,” roared Crook, “ and
the championship of the Castlereagh.” Uncle
Jim took a long breath and looked at me.
“ Don’t mix it with Stiffener,” whispered
Crook—“hit him from a safe distance.”
Uncle Jim peeled to his singlet and tightened
his belt. Stiffener Welsh jumped into the ring;
he was stripped to the waist and eager to begin.
The referee asked Uncle Jim to take off his
singlet, but Uncle explained that he was afraid of
catching influenza.
“ Time ! ” shouted Crook. Uncle looked white
and haggard as he moved out of his corner ; some-
body said he was the dead image of Corbett.
Nobody laughed. Stiffener leaped forward like a
runner off the mark, but Uncle Jim retreated
cleverly. Stiffener followed, swinging his right,
but Uncle whizzed away and never gave him a
single chance.
Round 2. Stiffener jumped out at the call of
“Time.”. Uncle was soon away at the other side
getting in a lot of useful footwork, and never
allowing Welsh to become really dangerous.
Uncle ended the round in fine style, with Stif-
fener sprinting hopelessly after him.
Round 3. More footwork and comments.
Round 4. “Crook ” Moore advised Stiffener to
go in and disembowel Uncle. Stiffener replied
that if anyone would -lend him a good horse and a
week’s tucker, he’d ohase Uncle over to Condobo-
lin. The referee said he couldn’t allow remarks
of that kind to pass, as they were injurious to
Uncle’s reputation.
Roimd 5 and last.—The crowd closed in, and
Stiffeher suddenly cornered Uncle Jim, and swung
his right with a terrific hang on his ribs. Uncle
drew up stiffly and sighed.
“ Holy Moses! ” said Crook. “ What the h—l
was that ? ” You could have heard the blow half-
a-mile away. Stiffener looked at his fist and
breathed on it, and then held it between his knees
for three minutes by the referee’s watch. “ Time! ”
shouted “ Crook.” Stiffener stepped back a little,
picked up his coat, breathed on his fist, and
walked right out of Brannigan’s Hole without
saying a word. “ Crook ” Moore went up to
Uncle, and handed him a big, fat purse. Nbbody
spoke or made any remark whatever, and in three
minutes a line of buggies was tailing off towards
Skinner’s Flat.
I followed Uncle home, and when we got inside
he closed the door carefully, and began to laugh.
“ Took ’em all down, by G—d ! Did ye hear that
punch Stiffener gave me ? ”
I said it sounded like the bang of a wool-press.
“ They’re a smart crowd,” he chuckled. “ Syd-
neyites and spielers, every man of ’em. But the
brains are in the country, my lad, and there’s
many a wrinkle picked up in the backblocks.”
He stripped off his singlet, and X saw hundreds
and hundreds of wrinkles; his chest was cased in
crocodile skin, and each vulnerable' point was
studded with brass knobs: the skin interlaced like
a pair of stays. He looked hard at me, and threw
it under the bed. “Splendid thing for th’
kidneys,” he said. “My word, that bi J of hide has
saved me a heap of pain. ” He brought out the purse,
weighed it in his hand, and tossed it to me, play-
fully. I pitched it back; he refused it skittishly, and
tossed it back again. I opened it while he gigtrled
over my shoulder. The purse was fairly stuffed
with silver and gold. A half-crown slipped from
my fingers and dropped with a thud to the hearth-
stone. Uncle picked it up and bit off a piece. I
shook up the purse and emptied about a hundred
little Jubilee medals on to the table. Neither of
us spoke. I went out for a stroll and stayed away
until dark. When I returned he was standing in
the same position.
ALBA DORION.


When I entered the profession most of
the Judges had married their mistresses. The
rule _ was that the barrister (on attaining the
bench) must either marry her or cast her off.
■Lord Campbell.

The Seaman.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh, a song of the heart she lilted,
As we strayed in the evening’s haze,
And watched the strange, dim, distant range
Flush red in the western rays.
I remember the word she whispered
And the flash of the downcast eyes :
The low, hushed talk and the homeward walk
By the sheen of the starlit skies.
But the love that she dreamed at daydawn
Died down with the morning mist,
lake a breath that played on a gleaming blade,
Or a wave that the shore has kissed.
She has driven me out before her
With a g be and a scornful laugh,
And bowed her down for a tinsel crown
At the Shrine of the Golden Calf.
And . . . what held her wayward fancy?
The trick of a wastrel tale ?
The deep-voiced lays of the rovers’ ways—
The sons of the scud-clad gale ?
Was she led from her land-locked city,
From the glare of the wind-swept streets?
Did she face the surf from the spray-splashed turf,
Where the heart of the ocean beats?
But the vision was soon forgotten,
With a smile at the passing dream
Of some old, grey town, where the ships go down
On the breast of the mighty stream.
For how could she know the voices
Or the call of the yearning seas ?
Her heart’s o’ the land : should she understand
The song of the seaward breeze ?
So passing, she leaves behind her
Just the sting of a new-wrought wrong,
The low-breathed word the night-wind heard
And the ghost of a drifting song.
But the sea-breeze, high in the tree-tops
Is crooning the old refrain :
“Return to the track of the drifting rack,
Oh, Child of the Outer Main !
“We are sent of the sea to call you
By the strength of your early love.
Go down: she waits at the rock-bound gates
Where the Night-Guides gleam above.
For the tramp rides out at moorings,
And the liner will sail at noon,
And the coasters bide for the ebbing tide,
And the light of the waning moon.”
I have bought the pride of freedom
At the price that my heart has paid,
And I’ve lain too long with the town-bred throng
For the sport of a wanton maid.
There are spots in the reef-strewn ocean
(In the life of a soft south moon)
Where a man may rest on a ripe brown breast
By the side of the still lagoon.
Let the liner stand out for London
And the bellowing Biscay nights ;
I’ll sail again to the Wicked Main
By the track of the Great South Lights.
So it’s ho ! for the flying spindrift,
And the hiss of the creaming seas,
And the drunken reel of a trader’s keel
To the rush of the off-shore breeze.
M.L.
V.R.B.

He had said, “Aw ! go on, Steve ; she’ll
hang long enough.” And his mate, a green hand
just starved off a drought-ruined farm, bankrupt
now in purse and in spirit, had been ashamed to
show his fears ; for, after all, they might only be
due to his new-ehum ignorance. But when, two
hours after, Hawkins, dropping his hammer,
cried, “Back, back!” Steve Turner, the green
hand, still clutching the drill, looked up at the
overhanging ore face to know why. That wasted
moment cost him his life.
His widow, querulous as women hre apt to be
who bear many children in the midst of misfor-
tunes begotten still faster, cursed Hawkins, know-
ing none else to curse. Besides, she argued, in
justification, hadn’t the jury said that Hawkins
had not exercised sufficient care?
Which Hawkins knew even better than the jury
did. He was a fool, he said to himself, to have
taken a new-chum on as mate ; but having taken
the new-chum on—well, he alone was clearly re-
sponsible for the safety of the workings. And
the neck-band of his shirt always became unbear-
\ ini en he recallecl those words of his—“ Go on
she 11 hang long enough. ” That he had used them
was the torturing secret of his life
Turner’s widow distractedly wrote to the com-
pany asking whether they wouldn’t do something
for the penniless family of a man killed in the
mine.
For reply she got some type-written sympathy •
as to any claim, they begged to refer her to the
verdict of the coroner’s jury, which she would see
absolved the company from all responsibility
Ihe jury’s verdict blamed the mate. So she
sent the letter, with some parts underlined, to the
already tormented Hawkins.
I can t see no’t else to be done,” Hawkins said,
desperately j 111 have to marry her —and her six
Ung u uns * G ’blime, though, others can have all
the other new-chums for mates to do their bul-
iockm —no more for me.”
She married, of course. What else could she
do . And married him, too ; who else would have
her—in her condition ?
Two months afterwards he heard her still speak
of him as the man who killed her husband.
Yet he was patient. “It will be orlright,” he
’t. a^ter t * le child is born ” —Turner’s child.
But the months dragged through their course, the
child was born, and the load of his silent agony
was not lifted. 6 J
A quarrel would have been a relief. It might
have cleared the atmosphere. But she would not
quarrel.
“ Oh, yes,” she simpered, when appealed to, “ of
course I care for you ; ” then added, “I am quite
resigned.” It was torment.
She was utterly unreasonable, no doubt; but —
and his accusing secret softened his further
thoughts of her.
“ re we never to get any more pleasure out of
01 J r ii v ? s ' asked, half-confidentially and
wholly in sorrow.
“ Pleasure ? ” she echoed, with what might have
been either a sigh or a sneer. “ Pleasure ’ Oh
yes, by all means But don’t bring the woman
here. That's all.”
Another woman—merciful heavens !
“ When I go under,” he said, stung at last, “ I
hope to God you’ll be so provided for that no
ether poor devil 11 feel a call to marry you.”
M yes. No doubt the company’ll compensate
me handsome for the loss of you,” also with unac-
customed bitterness.
“ The comp’ny compensate you ? , It’s an idea,
certainly and about the only one worth a curse I
ever heard from you.”

The mine-timber was on fire. The engine-
whistles shrieked a tremulous call to all hands,
r roin the men gathered early at the shaft-mouth
awaiting the change of shift volunteers were
sought to rescue two workmen—some said that
three must be cut off—and to beat back the flames
from the shaft, if yet there was time for that.
Hawkins was the first man in the cage.
ffwo limp and helpless beings, grimy with
smoke, and bruised and bleeding, were brought to
the plat and sent up. And while yet the cage was
swiftly to the surface with them,
niL T groped his way back along the level.
Ihe flames roared in the stopes ; the clouds of
heavy pme-wood smoke rolled over and fought
with one another in their race to the outlet of the
open shaft.
Hawkins was still missing. No answering
sound came from the drive to the calls of the
rescue-party. Hawkins—and perhaps a third
man.
Toot by foot the gasping miners who sought
them were driven back.
“ Into the cage ; it’s no use. I won’t risk more, ”
the manager shouted above the roar. “ He’s suf-
focated by this. We’ll send the cage back, in case
he reaches the plat.”
The knocker-line was pulled, and the engine
answered in an instant.
1 resh volunteers sought to descend and search
for the missing rescuer ; but already the smoke in
the shaft was impenetrable.
*_ * * *
The shift-boss told the story of Hawkins’
bravery—how, having helped to rescue the two
men, he had crawled into the choking, blinding
darkness for the third man, whom he, no doubt
supposed to be cut off. And the shift-boss told
how the hero had been fully conscious of the
danger which he faced ; for he had called back, as
he disappeared in a rolling smoke-cloud—“ Good-
bye, chaps.”
The newspapers drew parallels with battle-field
scenes, of course; the coroner alluded to “the
subject s heroism; and the jury gave up their
fees to the widow.
And Mrs. Hawkins received another letter of
sympathy from the company, signed this time by
tne chairman, with a postscript notifying that a
cheque for -2100 had been sent to the secretary of
the relief fund.
“ <8280! ” she said—“ .£230 from the fund, and
±lOO insurance he had into the bargain,
wickxl j^ ,w ' clns cou ld ha’ done anything so
But the widow didn’t finish the sentence. Tor
what she had said to Hawkins—her one “idea”
worth a curse—was her secret.

A smart young man fell-m badly in Mel-
bourne, the other day. He had been sentenced to
18 months for bike-stealing. Now, a sentence of
two years or over carries a possible remission of
a quarter of the term, whereas any shorter sen-
tence only loses an eighth ; also the longer sentence
carries sundry other advantages, such as tobacco,
an increased dietary scale, less isolation, and a
better chance of a good “billet.” So this smart
youth, having got his 18 months, forthwith sent
for a detective and confessed to a paltry little
burglary with a view to getting six months more.
And that is where he slipped, for Magistrate Pan-
ton—knowing a thing or two—only g a warded an
extra three months. And now Pent ridge contains
one man who kicks himself severely for getting
advanta -es m ° nths without an y corresponding
An Arbitration Case.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Wk were talking over the M'Sharry case in camp,
a u the legal cormorants their pickings,
when Martin volunteered to tell his experience as
an arbitrator.
j .? ma .tt er o’ 10 year ago that I was nosin’
round Maonland quiet-like and struck a God-for-
gotten little place up Lawrence way. There was
two queer old codgers as was perpetooally bangin’
round the shanty there—kep’ by Missis Mills—a
widow-woman, so ’twas said. An’ one day these
two old chaps came to me mysterious-like and
arst me would T hact as harbitrator.
Well, I ain’t had no experience to talk of,”
says I, , but I ve knocked round some, and if the
case ain t too blessed technical, and the money’s
good, I’ll take it on.”
Then the old chaps ups and says as how it was
this way. A matter of four years afore, the
younger of ’em had picked up with this Mrs.
Mills in Noo South Wales; married her; and
taken her on to a contract where he had a goodish
job. Then old bloke number two comes along,
and gets a start. He gets friendly with the first
bloke and his missus; then he gets his job ; then
he takes hjs missus and both of ’em takes their
hook with the first man a-follerin’. Then a third
man comes along ; snavels the woman ; carts her
over to Maoriland; opens this shanty; dies and
leaves it to her.
Meantime my two old chaps has drowned their
sorrows together, and reckons to try their luck at
Lawrence. When they comes they finds me lady
the owner of a pub. and willin’ to take one of ’em
in on the old terms, providin’ they fixed it up
comfortable between themselves.
There they was stuck. Neither would give way.
if it had only been the woman they could ha’ fixed
•it—but the pub ! That was a different matter.
1 hen they thought of arbitration. Well . . . I
arbitrated ; and it ain t what it’s cracked up to be.”
Martin stopjied to fill his pipe.
“ Why ain’t it? ” asked'Smithers.
“Well I dunno exactly,” said Martin. “I
reckon where I fell in was in listening to the
woman and reserving my award. I reserved it for
a month—but we had to barricade the place from
the inside.”
He paused and rubbed an uglv scar on his cheek-
bone reminiscently. Then he turned up his right
trouser-leg, and gazed at an extraordinary mark
just below his knee.
<ip This is wot they call soovvneers,” he said.
I ve a few more of ’em distributed round sorae-
wheres. She was a fine woman, and the pub. was
all right, but it was a hot time. And, in the end
she chucked me out; and I never got rnv fees
from the old chaps neither.
” So that’s why—I think—this here—Barton—
maybe—hasn’t got such a soft thing as it looks,”
ended Martin drowsily.
We fell asleep pondering the matter.
N.S.W.
